She had excused herself from her hunters to take it, stepping away into the quiet of the surrounding trees. Now, alone for a moment, she leaned against the rough bark of an ancient oak. As the silence settled around her, her mind began to drift back through the years, how everything went from worst to worse to bad to good to bad to best to worse to now again best. She felt nostalgic as she thought about it. THIS WILL BE THE START OF A LONG FLASHBACK SO NO ITALICS ARE USED, THOUGH MAY BE USED FOR FLASHBACK IN A FLASHBACK 16 years ago, USA... The rain was a violation. It was not the gentle mist of a sacred grove or the brisk shower of a mountain peak; it was a cold, urban deluge that plastered the thin fabric of her chiton to a body that felt terrifyingly fragile. Each drop was a hammer blow against skin suddenly too thin, too vulnerable. Artemis stood, a small, drenched statue of misery on the rain-slicked sidewalk. The colossal shadow of the Empire State Building loomed behind her, a tombstone marking the grave of her divinity. The disbelief, a cold, hard stone in her gut, began to crack, and from the fissures seeped a poison so potent it burned. Hatred. Her head tilted up, a slow, deliberate motion against the weight of the rain. Through the watery veil, she glared at the hidden peak of Olympus. A silent snarl twisted her eight-year-old features, making them ancient and terrible. Let your thrones rot.The curse formed in her mind, a venomous litany against every single one of them.Let your ambrosia turn to ash.Her thoughts spared no one—not wise Athena, whose logic was a sharper knife than any blade, not warm Hestia, whose hearth's comfort was revealed to be the cruellest lie of all. A shiver—a violent, humiliating tremor she could not control—wracked her small frame. It hammered the finality of her fate into her bones. She was banished. Stripped. Alone. The crumpled note from Apollo in her numb hand was a pathetic mockery of an anchor. She was adrift in a world of men she despised, her godhood a fading echo, the cold reality of her mortality a shackle she could not break. The rain had settled into a miserable, persistent drizzle, soaking the world in a patina of grey. Every sound was an assault—the hiss of tires on wet asphalt, the jarring blare of a distant horn, the cacophony of human voices raised in argument or laughter. It was all so… loud. So, crude. To Artemis, whose senses had been attuned to the whisper of the wind through leaves and the soft footfall of a deer, it was a form of torture. A new, gnawing emptiness growled in her stomach, a demand so base and mortal it filled her with fresh shame. Her fingers, small and chilled to the bone, fumbled in the simple pocket of her damp chiton. They closed around a thin, crumpled wad of paper. For a heart-clenching moment, she feared it was the address, that she had already lost her only tether to this alien world. But no. She pulled it out. It was money. Human currency. Ten crumpled ten-dollar bills. A hundred dollars. A fortune to a mortal, she supposed, but to her, it was a final, insulting symbol of her fall. This was what her eternal existence had been traded for. A few pieces of green paper that felt cheap and flimsy between her fingers. She stared at it, the image of Zeus's cold, dismissive gaze flashing in her mind. Had he personally counted this out? Had it been part of the decree? 'The goddess Artemis is hereby stripped of her divinity and shall be cast out with… one hundred dollars.' The pettiness of it was breathtaking. With a shuddering breath that fogged in the chilled air, she shoved the money back into her pocket, her movements stiff and uncoordinated in her new, weaker body. Her other hand still clutched the note from Apollo. She had expected a mocking verse, a final jab to underscore her humiliation. She unfolded it with a sense of dread, her childish fingers struggling with the wet paper. The message inside was stark in its simplicity. No words of comfort, no explanation, no poetic farewell. Just an address:128 Myrtle Avenue, Woodbury, New Jersey. A destination. A set of coordinates in a world she no longer understood. Was this her brother's idea of a cruel joke? A place that didn't exist? Or was it… something else? A tiny, treacherous spark of hope tried to ignite within her chest, and she ruthlessly smothered it. Hope was for fools and mortals. She was now the latter, but she refused to be the former. Her eyes, once silver and sharp enough to track a sparrow in a gale from across a continent, now scanned the confusing urban landscape with hopeless confusion. A bus stop. She needed to find a bus stop. Following signs, she barely understood, her small, sandaled feet splashing through cold puddles that seeped into her footwear, she finally found a sheltered bench. She sat, her posture defeated, her body language screaming of a profound and total loss. She drew her knees up to her chest, a futile attempt to conserve warmth and appear smaller, invisible. The wait was an eternity filled with the discomfort of the hard plastic seat and the unsettling feeling of mortal eyes glancing her way. A woman offered a sympathetic smile. Artemis glared back with a venom that made the woman quickly look away. Pity. She was now an object of pity. A bus rumbled to a stop, its destination scrolls reading a name she didn't recognize. "New Jersey?" she asked the driver, her voice small and unused, lacking its former divine command. "Transfer at Port Authority, kid," the man grunted, not unkindly. She paid with one of the strange bills, receiving alien coins in return, and found a seat by a fogged-up window. As the bus pulled away from the curb, carrying her further from the only home she had ever known, the full weight of her circumstance crashed down upon her. She was no longer a goddess who saw humans as inferior beings. She was one of them. Helpless, cold, and utterly alone, with nothing to her name but a handful of paper and a scrap of hope she didn't want to trust. Her only prayer was that the address, the one concrete thing she possessed, was a genuine kindness from the brother who had stood alone in her defence, and not the final twist of the knife. Now all she could do was sit for the hour-long ride with only thinking about where it all went wrong. She sat on the window seat on the left aisle looking at the city as she was leaving it behind, not noticing the old lady that sat beside her. Thinking about her banishment she pictured it. Flashback: 2 Days Ago… New York Jonathan Stone was, by all mortal accounts, a paragon. A decorated fire captain from Queens, he was a local hero famous for running into burning buildings long after his colleagues were ordered to evacuate. His minor philanthropy—donating time and money to children's hospitals and veterans' charities—was done without press conferences or plaques, fuelled by a genuine, unassuming desire to do good. But the source of his near-supernatural luck and strength was a secret buried in his bloodline: he was a legacy of Zeus. If asked, Jonathan would never claim divine favour. With a humble shrug, he'd simply say his great-grandfather watched over him. His family was a dynasty of service, with veterans stretching from the beaches of Normandy to the jungles of Vietnam, a fact that filled Zeus with a pride he rarely felt for his modern children. In the constant, silent rivalry between the Brothers Three, Jonathan was Zeus's ultimate ace. While Poseidon could claim the serial killer Jeffrey Dahmer and Hades the genocidal dictator Adolf Hitler among their diluted descendants, Zeus could point to Jonathan Stone—a good man, a family man, a hero. He was living, breathing proof of Zeus's superior lineage. Their bond transcended the typical god-legacy dynamic. One stormy night, moved by Jonathan's unwavering courage in the face of a collapsing warehouse fire, Zeus appeared before him, not as a distant ancestor, but in a crackle of ozone and a flash of brilliant white light. "Ask of me any blessing," the king of the gods had boomed, his voice like thunder. "Wealth, power, immortality. It is yours." The mortal's response stunned him into a rare, profound silence. Jonathan didn't ask for anything for himself. He asked for good health and a long life for his infant son and daughter, and a happy, stable marriage for his wife, Clara. The sheer selflessness of it, so alien to the narcissistic world of Olympus, humbled Zeus. From that moment, Jonathan became more than a legacy; he became a son. A son Zeus could be proud of. Zeus became a frequent, invisible guest in their modest home, a spectral grandfather watching children's birthday parties and a confidant sharing a glass of ambrosia-laced whiskey with Jonathan after a long day. He told him everything—the glorious, the petty, the shameful. He spoke of his fears, his frustrations with the other gods, and his countless infidelities. The Stone family, with their profound and simple morality, never judged him. They offered a forgiveness and unconditional love Hera never could. The only rift in their relationship came with the revelation of the affair with Beryl Grace. Jonathan, whose own childhood had been shattered by his father's adultery, was fiercely disappointed. He didn't yell or preach; his quiet disapproval, the sad shake of his head, was a more potent punishment than any lightning bolt. It took time, patience, and a demonstrated effort from Zeus to earn back his trust, a scar that remained but ultimately healed. For Zeus, Jonathan was everything: a son, his closest confidant, his anchor to the best parts of mortality, and his personal therapist. For Jonathan, Zeus was the difficult, flawed, but ultimately loving father he'd never had. And for his children, he was just Grandpa Z, who told fantastic stories and made the lights flicker when he laughed. A Forest near New York The forest was a cathedral of ancient oak and dappled sunlight, a sacred space where the only sounds were the rustle of leaves and the distant, silver laughter of a stream. For Elara, a young hunter of Artemis barely two decades into her immortal service, it was a place of perfect peace. She had been tracking a magnificent stag for three days, not for the kill, but for the honor of the hunt, to observe its grace and power. Now, miles from her sisters, she had paused by the stream to refill her waterskin, her bow leaning against a mossy log. The first sign of the intrusion was the smell—acrid, unnatural. Smoke. Then came the sound, a low, hungry crackle that was utterly foreign to the serene melody of the woods. Her head snapped up, her senses, honed by her goddess's blessing, stretching out. A fire. And it was moving fast. Driven by a duty deeper than command, Elara abandoned her hunt and ran toward the threat. She crested a ridge and her blood ran cold. A wall of flame, fanned by a sudden, mischievous wind, was racing through the dry undergrowth toward a secluded cluster of homes on the forest's edge. Panic, raw and human, threaded through her divine calm. Mortals were in danger. She was not a goddess who could command the winds or summon rain. She was a hunter. Her first instinct was to run, to find her Lieutenant, to rally the hunters to help evacuate and contain. But as she turned, a thunderouscrackechoed over the roar of the flames. A giant old pine, its heart eaten by the fire, groaned and began its deadly fall—directly toward a small, wooden playhouse in the backyard of the nearest home. Without a second thought, Elara ran. Not away, but toward the falling tree. She moved with preternatural speed, a blur of silver and brown, leaping over a collapsing fence. She couldn't stop the tree, but she could save whoever was inside. She slammed into the playhouse door just as the shadow of the falling giant enveloped her, using all her enhanced strength to shove the small figure huddled inside—a young mortal girl, no more than five—clear of the structure. There was a splintering, deafening crash. A searing pain exploded in her leg as a branch from the falling tree pinned her to the scorched earth. The world narrowed to the smell of sap and smoke, the weight of the timber, and the terrified sobs of the little girl she had saved, who was now screaming for help. It was into this hellscape that Jonathan Stone arrived. He wasn't supposed to be home; his shift at the firehouse had ended an hour ago. He'd been reading to his own children when the smoke had billowed past the window. While others fled, he ran toward the danger, his father's—hisgrandfather's—courage thrumming in his veins. He'd been helping an elderly neighbour when he saw the tree fall and heard the child's scream. He found Elara first. A young woman, dressed in strange, archaic hunting leathers, trapped under a monstrous branch. Her face was a mask of pain and startling determination, her hands bloody from trying to free herself. And beside her, his neighbour's daughter, Mia, sobbing and clutching the hunter's tunic. "It's okay! I've got you! I'm a firefighter!" Jonathan yelled, his voice a steady anchor in the chaos. He didn't see a divine being; he saw an injured young woman who had clearly tried to save a child. He strained against the branch, his legacy-given strength making the impossible merely difficult. With a grunt of effort, he lifted the weight just enough. "Pull your leg out! Now!" Elara, dizzy with pain and disoriented, obeyed. The moment she was free, Jonathan dropped the branch with a ground-shaking thud. He didn't hesitate. Seeing the deep, ugly gash on her leg and the way she couldn't put weight on it, he made a split-second decision. He swept her up into his arms in a bridal carry, grabbing the crying Mia with a firm, reassuring hold against his side. "I've got you both," he promised, his voice calm and certain. To Mia, he was a hero. To Elara, in her pain-fogged state, his actions were a terrifying violation. Amanwas touching her. A man was carrying her. The sacred oath screamed in her mind. She struggled weakly. "Unhand me! Do not touch me!" "It's okay, you're safe," Jonathan insisted, mistaking her divine fury for panic. He carried them both through the swirling smoke, away from the advancing flames, and deposited them gently onto the soft grass of a safe yard two properties over, surrounded by other evacuated families. "My leg… it will heal," Elara gasped, pushing against his chest, her eyes wide with a fear he completely misread. "Leave me!" "It's a bad cut, you need a tourniquet," Jonathan said, his voice full of genuine concern. Thinking only to stop the bleeding, he quickly stripped off his belt. Before Elara could process his intent, he knelt and wrapped the leather belt tightly around her thigh, above the wound, pulling it firm to stanch the flow of blood. It was an act of pure, practical kindness. But to Elara, the feeling of the man's hands on her bare leg, the possessive circle of his belt around her thigh, was the ultimate defilement. She saw not a lifesaving measure, but a claim, a branding. Satisfied she was no longer bleeding out, Jonathan gave her a reassuring smile. "Medics are coming. You're going to be fine." Then he turned and ran back toward the fire, toward others who needed help, a hero to the last. Meanwhile, from the tree line, shrouded in smoke, Lysandra, a veteran hunter with centuries of bitterness etched into her soul, had seen it all. She had arrived just in time to see the massive man scoop up her wounded sister. She saw him carry her off. She saw him kneel between her legs. She saw him bind her with his belt. She did not see the context of the rescue, the child, or the kindness in his eyes. She saw only a narrative of violation, a mortal man abducting and claiming one of their own. Her face hardened into a mask of cold fury. As the first sirens wailed in the distance, she melted back into the forest, her mission changed. She had to report to the Goddess. Immediately. The sacred grove was a place of stillness, where moonlight fell in liquid silver through the canopy. Artemis stood by a reflecting pool, her mind touching the consciousness of a wolf pack miles away. The connection shattered as Lysandra, a veteran hunter with centuries of service etched into her stern face, burst into the clearing. Her armour was smudged with soot, and her breath came in sharp gasps, but it was the cold, controlled fury in her eyes that made Artemis snap to attention. "My Lady," Lysandra stated, going to one knee, her voice tight. "An incident. A potential violation." Artemis's face became a mask of icy calm. "Rise. Report everything you saw. Omit nothing." Lysandra stood, her gaze direct. She was a faithful servant, not a schemer. She reported the facts as her eyes had recorded them. "I was tracking Elara's trail near the mortal settlement of Woodbury. I smelled smoke. A wildfire, moving fast. I found her near the blaze. She had just saved a mortal child from a falling tree. She was brave. The tree fell; a branch pinned her. She was injured. Gravely." Artemis's hands clenched at her sides, but she remained silent, listening. "Then a mortal male arrived. Large. Powerful. He moved the branch that pinned her with significant strength. He did not flee the fire. He approached them." Lysandra's voice was flat, factual. "He lifted Elara into his arms. She struggled against him. She was clearly distressed and called out for him to release her. He did not. He also picked up the child and carried them both away from the immediate danger." Lysandra paused, her lips thinning. "He took them to a yard a safe distance away. Then… he knelt. He put his hands on her. He removed his belt and bound it tightly around her upper thigh. She was struggling throughout this. Once he was finished, he left her there and returned to the fire." She finished her report. "I retrieved Elara immediately after he departed. She is in the healing springs. The physical wound is deep, but she is shaken more by the… encounter. That is all I witnessed." Lysandra had reported the truth. But she had also reported it through the lens of a thousand years of hating and distrusting mankind. She had seen a man carry off a struggling, wounded hunter. She had seen him hold her down and bind her with his belt. The context of rescue, the medical necessity, the kindness in Jonathan's eyes—these were invisible to her. She reported the action, and the action alone, looked exactly like a brutal claim of ownership. Artemis did not ask for context. The image Lysandra's words painted was all she needed. A man. Touching one of her maidens. Handling her. Forcing . The sacred oath screamed in her ears, drowning out any voice of reason. Her vision tinted with a red haze of pure, undiluted rage. "Describe him," Artemis commanded, her voice dangerously low, a whisper of death. "Broad-shouldered. Dark hair. He moved with the confidence of a warrior. He wore a symbol on a chain around his neck—a bronze eagle." It was enough. For a goddess, it was more than enough. A moment later, Artemis stood on a wind-scoured cliff overlooking the town of Woodbury. The smoke from the fire still stained the horizon. Her divine senses, sharper than any bloodhound's, sought the unique signature of the man who had dared lay hands on her hunter. She found it—a spark of unusual vitality, a trace of ozone that was familiar and now utterly offensive. She found Jonathan Stone outside the town hospital. He was covered in soot and sweat, giving a statement to a paramedic, waving off attention for a minor burn on his own arm. "I'm fine, really. Check on the crew in Engine 4," he was saying, his voice warm with concern for others. He never saw her coming. Artemis did not announce herself. She did not demand an explanation. In her heart, the evidence was irrefutable. She became the vengeance of every woman wronged by a man. As Jonathan turned to walk toward his truck, exhausted but satisfied, the cliff face above the parking lot shuddered. With a subtle twitch of her divine will, Artemis loosened a single, massive boulder. It wasn't a blast of lightning; it was a terrible, plausible accident. The rock tore free with a grinding roar. Jonathan looked up, his eyes widening not with fear, but with a final, fleeting moment of calculation. There was no time to run. It struck the ground before him with cataclysmic force, and a shard of stone, sharp as a spear, shot out from the impact. It struck him through the heart. He was dead before he hit the ground, his body crushed and broken by the seemingly random act of nature. A tragic end for a hero. A perfect accident. Artemis watched, her fury cooling into grim satisfaction. Justice was served. Miles away, on Olympus, Zeus was in his private chambers, holding a simple, hand-drawn birthday card from a little girl named Mia. He smiled. The smile vanished. A searing, psychic pain lanced through him, followed by a void, a sudden, deafening silence where there had always been a vibrant, familiar presence. It was a feeling he had not felt in millennia, not since the fall of a favoured hero. The amulet he had given Jonathan—and matching ones to Clara and the children—was more than jewellery. It was a tether, a silent alarm for family. The connection had snapped. The card fluttered from his suddenly numb fingers. The air in the room crackled, and the scent of ozone became overpowering. His eyes, which moments before had been twinkling with grandfatherly pride, now swirled with the chaos of a nascent storm. Something had happened to Jonathan. And the King of the Gods would tear the world apart to find out what. The air crackled, thick with the promise of a storm that had not been on any mortal forecast. A flash of light, blinding and absolute, illuminated the hospital parking lot for a single, searing instant. Where it faded, Zeus stood, his massive form seeming to suck the sound from the world. His eyes, wide with divine panic, scanned the chaos until they found the epicentre of his pain. There, amidst the dust and rubble, lay Jonathan. Not the vibrant, laughing son of his heart, but a broken doll of a man, his life bleeding out onto the cold asphalt. A sound ripped from Zeus's throat, something between a roar and a sob, as he fell to his knees beside him, ignoring the terrified screams of mortals who scattered like leaves. He cradled Jonathan's head, his hands, which could command lightning, now trembling. "My boy," he whispered, his voice thick. "Who did this? Speak to me." Jonathan's eyes fluttered open, their focus swimming. He didn't see the King of Olympus. He saw a father. A final, rattling breath escaped his lips, a whisper meant only for one set of ears. "Hey pops… please… take care of… Clara… the kids…and…thank you…for being…my dad." The light in his eyes faded. The tether, the one Zeus had felt snap from a world away, was now a frayed and silent end. Jonathan Stone was gone. An agony unlike any he had known in centuries seized Zeus. A single, hot tear traced a path through the dust on his godly cheek. It hit the ground, and the sky above them exploded. Rain fell not in drops, but in sheets, a torrential, punishing downpour that mirrored his grief. As he held the body of the man he loved as a son, his divine gaze, sharpened by unimaginable pain, swept the area. He was looking for a reason, for a weapon, for any clue. And then he saw it. Half-buried in the mud next to Jonathan's body, trampled but not destroyed, was a single, perfect silver arrowhead. It was not from any mortal quiver. It hummed with a familiar, chilling magic, a power he knew as well as his own. It was Artemis's craftsmanship. It was the signature of the Hunt. The pieces crashed together in his mind: Artemis's fanatical hatred of men, her proximity to the woods, her swift and brutal justice. She must have seen him with the hunter and drawn the worst possible conclusion. The storm intensified, winds howling as his sorrow curdled into something infinitely darker. The rain felt like needles, the thunder his heartbeat of rage. "No…" The word was a low growl that vibrated through the very concrete. Then, it rose into a scream to the heavens, a vow etched in lightning. "ARTEMIS!" He would make her pay. He would strip her of everything she held dear, just as she had stolen everything from him. The King of the Gods wept for his son, and the world wept with him. Flashback end… The world had ended in a hospital parking lot. For Zeus, the following hours were a blur of a pain so profound it was a physical weight, crushing his divine form into something small and mortal. He had torn a hole in the world to return to Olympus, not to his throne room, but to a secluded grove high on its slopes, a place he went when the burdens of eternity became too heavy. There, he did not command the storm; he was consumed by it. He wept, and the skies over the Mediterranean wept with him. He raged, and lightning scarred the heavens without thunder. He was not a god, but a father grieving his son. He reached for a decanter of nectar, but his hand, still trembling, knocked it aside. The divine liquor was too weak for this agony. In a fit of despair, his form flickered, the edges of his identity blurring. The grief was too raw for the king of the Greek gods. Another aspect, colder, more disciplined, yet capable of burying pain deep within a rigid structure of duty, rose to the surface. Jupiter, the Roman Alter, did not weep. He sat on a marble bench, his posture ramrod straight, and summoned a bottle of the finest, most potent Falernian wine. He drank not to feel, but to numb. He drank to forget the feel of Jonathan's cooling skin, the final, trusting whisper. He drank until the sharp edges of the world softened into a tolerable haze. It was in this numbed state, his mind a fortress against feeling, that a familiar presence found him. Beryl Grace, a ghost from a life that now felt a thousand years past, stood at the edge of the grove. She had always been drawn to power, to turmoil. She saw not a grieving father, but a monument of a god in pain. She approached him not with words of comfort, but with a silent, understanding presence. For Jupiter, lost in the void, she was an anchor to a simpler, physical reality. A distraction. A way to feel anything other than the hollowed-out agony in his chest. The night became a blur of another kind, a desperate, meaningless attempt to outrun a sorrow that was as vast as the sky itself. Dawn was a betrayal. The pale light did not bring clarity, only a hammering headache and the cold, stark return of his reality. Jonathan was gone. And his family… Clara, the children… they did not know. The thought was a fresh lance of pain. He had to tell them. He had to look into the eyes of the woman he considered a daughter and the children he adored as his own grandchildren and lie. He found them in the warm, sunlit kitchen of the home he had secretly helped them buy. The table was set for five. There was a high chair for little Mia. The scene of perfect, mundane happiness was a knife in his heart. Clara was humming, flipping pancakes. "Morning, Dad Z," she said, using the silly nickname the kids had coined. "You're just in time. The coffee's fresh." He opened his mouth, but no sound came out. The words were ashes. It was then that the doorbell rang. Two men in crisp NYFD uniforms stood on the doorstep, their hats in their hands, their faces carved from stone. Zeus felt the world tilt. He had to stand there, he had to listen, as a mortal man, Captain O'Malley, a man whose hand Zeus had shaken at a hundred department barbecues, explained in gentle, horrific detail about the "freak rockfall." He had to watch as Clara's face crumpled, as her knees gave way and he caught her. He had to hold his grandson, Leo, who didn't understand the tears but began to wail in sympathy, and his granddaughter, Mia, who kept asking, "Where's Daddy?" He had to act. He had to let his own face pale, let a convincingly human shock glaze his eyes. He had to pull Clara into a hug, feeling her sobs shake his own body, and whisper the lie that shattered what was left of his heart. "What… no," he choked out, the words tasting like poison. "Clara, my dear girl, I am so sorry." He made the promises then, his voice hoarse with a grief they all believed was new. "I will take care of you. All of you. I promise. Everything will be okay." Every word was a betrayal of the truth, a shield he was forced to build around them, and it was killing him. The funeral was a public spectacle of grief for a fallen hero. Fire trucks lined the streets. Hundreds came. But for Zeus, it was a private hell. He sat in the front pew, a hand on Clara's shaking shoulder, the other holding a quietly sobbing Mia. He wore the mask of a devastated old man. When it was his turn to speak, he walked slowly to the podium, every step an eternity. He looked out at the sea of faces, but he saw only Jonathan. He cleared his throat, the sound echoing in the silent church. He approached the podium as a man approaching the gallows, each step a monumental effort of will. The simple wooden stand seemed to loom before him, an altar of unbearable finality. He placed his hands upon it, not for support, but to feel something solid, something real, to anchor himself against the tidal wave of grief threatening to pull him under. The silence in the church was a living thing, heavy and expectant. He looked down at the casket, and the world narrowed to that polished box, that unbearable truth. He cleared his throat, the sound raw and scraping in the quiet. "They asked me to say a few words about my son." His voice was a low, broken rumble, far from the thunder that usually commanded the heavens. It was just the voice of a broken old man. A grandfather. A father. A faint, sorrowful smile touched his lips, a fleeting ghost of a memory. "When Jonathan was a boy, he used to try and drag me outside during a thunderstorm. He'd say, 'Come on, Grandpa Z, it's just energy! It's exciting!'" A soft, choked sob escaped from Clara in the front pew. The sound lanced through him. He had to pause, collecting the shattered pieces of himself. "I… I would look at his face, so full of wonder, completely unafraid of the chaos and the noise, and I would think… I have never seen such courage." His voice grew stronger, infused with a painful, piercing love. "That was who he was. He wasn't just brave when the sirens blared. He was brave every single day. He was brave when he chose kindness in a world that so often rewards cruelty. He was brave when he built a family founded on a love so pure and so steadfast that it… it humbled me." He looked directly at Clara, his eyes glistening. "He looked at you, Clara, every day for fifteen years, like he was the luckiest man who had ever drawn breath. And he was." His gaze shifted to the children, and his composure nearly broke. "And you, Leo, Mia… you were his sun and his moon. His entire universe. To him, your laughter was a sweeter symphony than any ever played on Olympus. Your happiness was his only ambition." His fists clenched slightly on the podium. "He was my son. Not by blood, but by choice. By a bond far stronger. He was the best of humanity. He was everything I have ever aspired to be: selfless, loyal, true." His voice began to shake, the godly facade crumbling completely under the weight of the lie he was forced to tell. "And the world is so much darker, so much colder, without his light in it." A single, hot tear finally overflowed and traced a path through his beard. "We are here to say goodbye. But I cannot. I will not. I will carry him with me. I will see his courage in every storm. I will hear his laughter in the rain. I will feel his love in the embrace of his family, the family he built, the family I swear to you now, on my very soul, I will protect until my last, dying breath." He looked heavenward, as if begging for strength, but it was a gesture of pure, agonized accusation. "Goodbye, my boy. My wonderful, brave boy. Thank you… thank you for choosing me to be your father." His voice broke, and a single, genuine tear traced a path down his cheek. The congregation saw a grieving father. Only he knew it was a god mourning the beautiful, fragile mortality of the family he had failed, lying to them with every beat of his broken heart. For two days, Zeus wore the mask of a grieving grandfather, his divine heart breaking with every lie. On the third morning, he kissed Clara's forehead. "I must settle his affairs," he whispered, a final betrayal. Then, stepping into a hidden alley, his form crackled with power. The call went out: an emergency council of the gods. Now. Olympus The grandeur of the Olympian throne room was a force unto itself, a space that defied mortal comprehension. The air hummed with the accumulated power of eons, smelling of ozone, ambrosia, and cold, polished stone. Vast pillars of solid star-stuff held up a ceiling that depicted the ever-shifting tapestry of the night sky, constellations writhing and reforming in real time. The floor was a mosaic of living earth, showing forests, oceans, and deserts in miniature, their tiny inhabitants going about their lives unaware of the gods who watched from above. In the centre of the immense hall burned Hestia's hearth, its flames a warm, steady heartbeat in the cool, majestic expanse. It was the only source of comfort in a room designed for awe. One by one, the thrones flared with light as their occupants materialized, their divine forms casting long, dramatic shadows. They arrived with the air of immortals abruptly pulled from more pressing concerns, their expressions a mixture of irritation and keen curiosity. Poseidon, Lord of the Seas, sat upon a throne of carved coral and Leviathan bone. He smelled of salt and deep, dark trenches. His sea-green eyes, stormy and intense, scanned the room, his trident leaning against his armrest, droplets of seawater glistening on its points. He drummed his fingers, a gesture that made the water in every mortal glass on earth tremble slightly. Hades, King of the Underworld, was a stark contrast. His throne was of polished obsidian and frozen screams, radiating a chill that even the hearth's fire struggled to combat. He was dressed in the robes of a wealthy ancient king, his form seeming to drink the light around him. His face was pale, severe, and etched with the patience of the grave, his fingers steepled as he observed the gathering with an air of detached, grim interest. Hera, Queen of the Gods, was the picture of regal composure. Her throne of gold and peacock feathers was the most ornate. She sat perfectly straight, her hands folded in her lap, her beautiful face a mask of cool, unreadable neutrality. Only a slight tightness around her eyes betrayed her annoyance at the disruption to her schedule. Demeter, Goddess of the Harvest, radiated a palpable energy of growth and sudden decay. Her throne was woven from living, golden wheat that swayed in a non-existent breeze. She looked anxious, her gaze distant, as if already calculating how this unforeseen meeting would disrupt the growing seasons. Ares, God of War, slammed onto his throne of bronze and leather, forged from the shields of fallen heroes. The clang echoed through the hall. He was already grinning, a brutal, eager expression. An emergency meeting always spelled conflict, and conflict was his favourite pastime. Athena, Goddess of Wisdom, appeared with a softpopof displaced air, already seated on her throne of silver and polished gray marble. Her stormy grey eyes were sharp, missing nothing. She held a scroll she had been reading, her finger marking her place, her entire posture radiating intense, analytical curiosity. She was already running probabilities in her mind. Apollo, God of the Sun, arrived in a burst of golden light and the faint, fading chord of a lyre. His throne, shaped like the sun with rays fanning out behind him, seemed too bright to look upon directly. He looked mildly put-out, as if he'd been pulled from a particularly inspiring composition with a particularly interesting lady. Aphrodite, Goddess of Love, materialized on a throne of shimmering mother-of-pearl and swan's down, smelling of roses and myrrh. She examined her own flawless reflection in a handheld mirror, sighing with boredom. Mortal dramas were her entertainment; divine politics were often so tedious. Hephaestus, God of the Forge, limped to his throne, a magnificent construct of automated gears and shifting metal that whirred softly. Soot smudged his brow and his powerful hands were calloused. He looked merely tired, as if he'd been pulled from a complex project. Hermes, the Messenger God, was already there, lounging on his winged throne of mercury and traveller's stone. He was juggling three celestial orbs, a look of amused mischief on his face. He lived for news, and an emergency summons was the best kind. Dionysus, God of Wine, slumped onto his throne of grapevines and leopard skin, looking profoundly hungover. He nursed a double-sized goblet and muttered under his breath about "unreasonable o'clock" and "punishments" and "wood nymphs" Hestia, Goddess of the Hearth, was the only one who did not sit on a grand throne. She knelt by her fire, tending the flames with a simple poker. She was a warm, comforting presence in a room of cold power, her simple brown robe a stark contrast to the finery around her. She watched the others with gentle, sorrowful eyes, as if already sensing the pain to come. A tense silence settled. All were present. All were waiting. It was Ares who broke it, his voice a gruff bark that echoed in the vastness. "Well? Are we just here to admire the architecture? Spit it out, Father. What's so urgent you had to drag us all from our domains?" "Indeed," Athena added, her voice calm and logical, though her eyes were alight with the puzzle. "An emergency council with no prior agenda is… highly unusual. The threads of fate are disturbed. What has occurred?" Poseidon leaned forward, the sea in his eyes churning. "The oceans have been restless for days. They sense a deep imbalance. Is this the cause?" Their questions hung in the air, a collective demand for an answer from the one throne that still remained empty. The throne of Zeus. The air in the throne room, already thick with divine power, now crackled with a tension so sharp it was a physical pain. All eyes were fixed on the empty hearth where Hestia usually knelt; even its comforting glow had been extinguished by the palpable fury radiating from the king of the gods. Zeus did not merely appear on his throne. Hemanifested. The very ether tore apart with a sound of screaming winds and the ozone-scented aftermath of a lightning strike. He was the Storm bringer, the Earth-Shaker. His eyes were not eyes but swirling vortexes of a hurricane, and in their depths, lightning flashed with cold, murderous intent. "We are gathered," his voice boomed, a tremor that vibrated through the marble floor and up into the very bones of the gods, "because there has been a murder. Not of a mortal. Not of a monster. One of our own has murdered myson." The word hung in the air, devastating in its finality. A son. Artemis, who had been sitting stiffly on her silver throne, her face a mask of cold defiance, went perfectly still. "Artemis," Zeus thundered, the name itself an accusation. "You, in your arrogance and your fanatical hatred, sought him out. You saw his goodness, his light, and you deemed it a crime. You ambushed him. You crushed him. You murdered him in cold blood. Do you deny it?" She rose, her power a cold silver moonbeam against his raging sun. "I deny nothing! I executed justice! I punished a violator of my sacred oath!" A baffled silence met her words. "Your justice?" Zeus's voice was a deadly whisper. "What justice?" "He laid hands upon one of my hunters!" Artemis's cry was fervent, fanatical. "Hetouchedher! Heboundher! My lieutenant witnessed it! His death was not murder; it waspurification!" Poseidon, his sea-green eyes wide with confusion, shook his head. "Brother, what is this? What son? What madness is she speaking of?" He looked from Zeus's grief-stricken rage to Artemis's zealous fervour, utterly lost. Hera's regal composure finally broke into open astonishment. "A son? You have asonI know nothing of? Andshekilled him? For… touching one of her girls?" The Queen of the Gods looked as if the very foundations of her understanding had been upended. The scandal of a hidden son was momentarily overshadowed by the sheer absurdity of the reason for his death. Apollo watched his sister, his face a war of conflicting loyalties. The golden light around him dimmed. He knew the crime was unnecessary, the punishment grotesquely disproportionate. He saw the fanaticism in her eyes, the same zeal that had always defined her, now twisted into something horrifying. He was on a knife's edge, torn between the sister he loved and the horrifying truth of her actions. Athena said nothing. But the disappointment on her face was a colder, sharper weapon than any insult. She looked at Artemis not with rage, but with profound, analytical sorrow. She saw the flawed logic, the catastrophic misjudgement, the total failure of wisdom. This was not the sister she knew; this was a fanatic who had allowed dogma to override reason, and the result was a tragedy of epic proportions. She saw the path that had led here and knew Artemis had chosen every step, right off a cliff. Artemis saw their reactions—Poseidon and Hera's confusion, Aphrodite's disgust, Apollo's torn silence, Athena's crushing disappointment—and a sliver of her unwavering certainty finally cracked. But the most devastating blow was yet to come. She turned back to her father, her expression one of bewildered betrayal. This was the father who had always been a marshmallow with her and Athena, who had indulged their independence. "Father… you would take the word of a mortal-loving spy over mine? For a… amortal? You called this council over amortal?" "SILENCE!" The word was not a command; it was a cataclysm. It was the sound of a mountain cracking in two, of a sky being torn asunder. The very foundations of Olympus groaned under the weight of it. The assembled gods, even the most powerful among them, flinched as one. Ares's grin finally vanished. Dionysus sobered instantly. Hades leaned forward, his funereal patience replaced by sharp attention. In the echoing aftermath of the roar, a new sound emerged: a low, deadly hum. In Zeus's outstretched palm, a single, perfect silver arrowhead materialized. It was not thrown, not presented as evidence. It wasdisplayed. It hung in the air between them, rotating slowly, and with each turn, it caught the light of the celestial ceiling, casting brilliant, accusing shards of light around the room. Every god present could feel its essence, the unique, cold, moon-touched magic that was Artemis's and Artemis's alone. It was a scent, a signature, a confession made of metal. "You left your calling card, Daughter," he seethed, his voice dropping into a register of such profound, personal pain that it was more terrifying than any thunder. Each word was measured, weighted with a grief that seemed to dim the very thrones. "Your 'justice' is as sloppy as it is cruel. You were so consumed by your righteous fury, so assured of your moral superiority, that you did not even think to conceal your work. You believed the act itself was so justified that no consequence would follow." He took a step forward, and the floor trembled. "You ask if I would choose him over you?" The question hung in the air, charged with a lifetime of indulgence now utterly incinerated by betrayal. He gestured not just to the arrowhead, but to the entirety of her defence, to the fanatical glint still in her eyes. "Look upon what you have done. Look upon the life you extinguished for a perceived slight, based on the testimony of a single, biased witness. Look upon the family you have shattered. Look upon the grief you have causedme—not as your king, but as your father, who loved a son you never bothered to know. You did not just kill a mortal. You broke an unbreakable trust. You forced this choice. So know the answer. Yes." He rose to his full, terrifying height, the storm in his eyes eclipsing the room, his form casting a shadow that swallowed the light of the hearth. The arrowhead continued its silent, damning rotation. "The council has heard the accusation," his voice boomed, formal and final, the voice of a judge passing sentence. "And we have heard the…defence." He spat the word as if it were a foul taste, making it clear her justification held less than zero weight in his eyes. It was not a defence; it was an indictment of her own character. "We have seen the proof," he continued, his gaze sweeping over every stunned, silent face. "Proof of her presence. Proof of her method. Proof of her intent, gleaned from her own fanatical words. She stands not accused by me, but condemned by her own actions and her own pride." He let the silence stretch, allowing the weight of it all to press down on every deity present. "There are no more words. There is no debate. The time for excuses is over. The only question that remains is what Olympus will do about the goddess who believes her oath is a license for murder. The stability of this pantheon, the very concept of justice we are sworn to uphold, demands a response. There will be a vote. Now." The silence that followed Zeus's decree was heavier than any mountain. It was the silence of inevitability. The king's grief was a tempest that had scoured the room clean of dissent, leaving only the stark, terrible choice before them. Zeus's eyes, still swirling with the ghosts of storms, swept across the council. His voice was no longer a roar, but the cold, final scrape of stone on stone. "The vote is called. All in favour of the permanent banishment of Artemis from Olympus, the stripping of her godhood, and her casting down to the mortal world… signify by saying 'Aye'." He did not look at her. He looked only at his council. Poseidon, his face grim, understanding the unpayable debt of a lost son, spoke first. His voice was the deep rumble of a tidal wave. "Aye." Hera, her initial shock hardened into cold fury at the revelation of a hidden son and the chaos his death had unleashed, nodded once, sharply. "Aye." Demeter, seeing only the blight of unjust death upon the world, whispered, "Aye." Ares, who respected brutal efficiency but wanted to just see choas, grunted. "Aye." Athena, her eyes closed in profound disappointment, her logic providing no other path for such a catastrophic failure of judgment, said softly, "Aye." Aphrodite, her heart sickened by the perversion of love and compassion Artemis had displayed, did not hesitate. "Aye." Hephaestus, who valued creation over mindless destruction, shook his head sadly. "Aye." Hermes, the messenger who understood consequences, sighed. "Aye." Hades, from the depths of his obsidian throne, gave a single, solemn nod. "Aye." Zeus's gaze finally landed on his son, the only one who had not spoken. Apollolooked at his twin, his face a mask of agony. He saw the fanaticism, the horrifying mistake, but he also saw his sister, his other half. His voice, when it came, was choked, barely audible, the only dissent in the crushing unanimity. "Nay." The word was swallowed by the silence. Zeus did not acknowledge it. He turned his terrible gaze back to Artemis. "The 'Ayes' have it. The council has spoken." The sentence was passed. She was banished. The final, echoing "Aye" from Hades seemed to suck all sound from the world. Zeus did not look at his daughter. His voice was flat, drained of all emotion, hollowed out by grief. "The council is adjourned. The sentence commences in one hour. Use it to put your affairs in order. Then, you will depart Olympus. Forever." With a crackle of ozone, he was gone. The other gods vanished in their turn, a silent, swift exodus of light and power, none willing to meet Artemis's eye. In moments, the vast, echoing throne room was empty save for three goddesses: the condemned, the judge, and the comfort that was no longer there. Artemis stood as if turned to stone. The reality of it, the finality, crashed down upon her. Her silver eyes, wide with a dawning, horrified understanding, found Athena, who was slowly rising from her throne. "You," Artemis's voice was a raw, broken thing, all her earlier fury extinguished. "Athena. You voted with them. Why?" Athena met her gaze, her own grey eyes filled with a profound, weary sadness. There was no anger there, only a devastating disappointment. "I did not vote withthem, sister. I voted againstyou," she said, her voice calm and horribly logical. "Your justice was not justice. It was zealotry. You saw an act of compassion and called it defilement. You heard a single report and became judge, jury, and executioner. You did not seek truth; you sought confirmation of your own bias. You were too far gone to see reason. This banishment is not a punishment from us. It is the consequence of your own actions, finally and irrevocably caught up to you." The words were a colder blow than any physical strike. Artemis flinched, taking a step back. Her gaze then shifted, pleadingly, to the one constant, the one source of unconditional warmth in all of Olympus. Hestia still knelt by her hearth, but the flames were low, guttering. "Aunt Hestia…" Artemis's voice was a child's whisper, begging for solace, for the one person who would understand. "You… Please. Tell them. This is a mistake." Hestia looked up, and the grief in her warm eyes was more terrible than Athena's logic. She looked as if her very heart was breaking. "Oh, my dear child," she whispered, her voice thick with tears. "My fire grows low with sorrow for you. But I did not vote because my heart could not bear to condemn you, yet my soul could not absolve you. What you did… it broke the first and most sacred law of this home: compassion. Perhaps… perhaps away from the echo chamber of your hunt, away from the certainty that has curdled into cruelty… perhaps this is for the best. Perhaps you can find your way back to what you were meant to be." It was the final, crushing blow. There was no ally here. No defence. Only the cold, hard truth of her isolation. A soundless sob shook Artemis's frame. Without another word, she turned and fled the throne room. Hunters Camp She appeared in the midst of her hunters' camp like a ghost. Her maidens looked up, smiles dying on their lips as they saw her expression. "My Lady?" Zoe Nightshade was at her side instantly. Artemis spoke quickly, her voice hollow, reciting the facts as if they belonged to someone else. "I have been banished. Stripped of my godhood. By the vote of the council. I have one hour." The cries of shock and outrage were immediate, a symphony of fury. Zoe's hand went to her sword. "We will fight! We will—!" "You will do NO SUCH THING!" Artemis's command cracked through the air, the last vestige of her divine authority. "The order is given. You will not seek vengeance. You will not defy Olympus." She looked at Zoe, her most loyal, her oldest friend. "Zoe Nightshade. You are the Lieutenant of the Hunt now. You will lead them. Protect them. Obey my last command." She did not allow for goodbye. She could not bear it. She simply vanished. Back in her chambers on Olympus, she moved like an automaton. She changed out of her silken robes into simple, sturdy hunting leathers. She emptied a small pouch of drachmas and mortal currency—a contingency she'd never thought she'd need—into a belt pouch. That was all she took. As she turned to leave, Apollo stood in her doorway. He said nothing. His face was a mirror of the agony tearing her apart. He didn't try to stop her. He didn't offer empty comfort. He simply held out a crumpled piece of paper. She took it, her fingers brushing his. No words passed between them. None were needed. He turned and was gone. Clutching the note, Artemis walked the familiar halls of Olympus for the last time. She reached the edge, the precipice that looked out over the mortal world. She took a final, shuddering breath, and stepped off. The feeling was not sudden, but a terrible, swift unravelling. It was like a thread pulled from a tapestry, the very fabric of her being coming apart. The world, once so clear and full of hidden paths, became dull and flat. The song of the moon, a constant melody in her soul, faded into silence. The strength that had been as natural as breathing leeched from her muscles. The cold of the wind bit into skin that was no longer invulnerable. She was unravelling, diminishing, fading into a mere echo of what she was. By the time her feet touched the rain-soaked pavement of New York City, Artemis, the Goddess of the Hunt, was gone. All that was left was a lost, eight-year-old girl, drenched and utterly alone. Flashback End The bus shuddered to a halt with a final, wheezing sigh, pulling Artemis from the torturous reel of her memories. The driver called out a town name that meant nothing to her. New Jersey. This was the place. She moved like a ghost, her small body numb, the worn leather of her sandals scraping on the bus steps as she descended into the unfamiliar, rain-washed world. The bus pulled away, its red taillights fading into the gloom, leaving her utterly alone on a quiet, tree-lined street. The note, now soft and frayed from her frantic grip, was her only compass. 128 Myrtle Avenue. Her eight-year-old's legs carried her down the sidewalk, each step a monumental effort. The world was too loud, too bright, toorealin a way it had never been. She could feel every crack in the pavement through her thin soles, smell the damp earth and the distant, greasy scent of mortal food. She was a raw nerve, exposed to the elements. And then she saw it. A modest, white-sided house with a dark green door. The numbers*128*were nailed neatly beside it. This was it. The end of her journey. Or perhaps the beginning of a prison sentence. Her heart hammered against her ribs, a frantic, mortal drum. She pushed open the iron gate, its whine echoing in the quiet street, and walked the short path to the door. There were no more options, no more places to run. With a trembling hand that felt too small, too weak, she raised her fist and knocked. The door opened. Standing there, backlit by the warm, golden light of a hallway, was a woman. Her face was etched with a kindness so profound it was like a physical ache. Her hair was dark, her eyes the colour of twilight, and they held a deep, ancient sorrow that somehow also promised solace. She smelled of rosemary and dried lavender. It was Leto. Her mother. Artemis's breath hitched. The world tilted. The last vestiges of her divine composure, the stubborn pride that had carried her across a continent, shattered into a million pieces. The weight of the banishment, the betrayal of her family, the horrifying reality of her mortality, the crushing loneliness—it all crashed down upon her at once. She did not speak. A small, broken sound escaped her lips, a sob that had been trapped for centuries. The tears she had refused to shed on Olympus now fell in a hot, silent flood, streaming down her cheeks. Her shoulders slumped, her entire small frame beginning to shake with the force of a grief too vast for her body to contain. Leto did not gasp. She did not ask questions. Her own eyes welled with tears, mirroring her daughter's pain. In one fluid, graceful motion, she knelt and opened her arms. Artemis fell into them. She collapsed against her mother's chest, her small hands fisting in the soft wool of Leto's sweater. She buried her face in the familiar, comforting scent, and she wept. She wept for the father who had cast her out, for the sister who had condemned her, for the hunters she had left behind, for the power she had lost. Great, heaving sobs that wracked her tiny form, the unadulterated anguish of a child who had lost everything. Leto held her. She rocked her gently, her own tears falling into her daughter's hair. One hand cradled the back of Artemis's head, the other drew slow, calming circles on her back, offering a comfort so primal, so fundamental, that it could only come from a mother. As the storm of tears began to subside into shaky, hitching breaths, Leto pressed a soft kiss to her temple. She pulled back just enough to look into her daughter's red-rimmed, silver eyes. Still holding her, she gently reached into the pocket of her apron and withdrew a small, familiar celestial bronze dagger—a twin to the one Artemis had always carried, now lost to her. She placed it in Artemis's trembling hand and closed her small fingers around the hilt. Then, she touched her own heart and pointed to the sky, her gaze holding her daughter's, conveying a silent message of unwavering love and understanding. And in that gesture, in the offering of the blade and the pointed glance heavenward, Artemis understood. Apollo. Her brother, even in her fall, had not abandoned her. He had reached across the world to the one person who could offer true sanctuary. He had sent her here, to their mother. He had ensured she would not be alone. A fresh wave of emotion washed over her, but this one was different. It was not the sharp sting of betrayal, but the warm, salving balm of a love that had persisted through the ruin. Closing her eyes, she leaned her head against her mother's shoulder once more, and in the quiet of her own heart, she sent a single, grateful thought into the universe, a silent message for the twin who had, in the end, kept his promise. Thank you. ________________________________________ Arc: USA Arc [Flashback Arc] (Arc III) 24.08.2025 Words: 10,386 ~Cubicreates ________________________________________ Chapter 16: The Man-Wary Goddess ________________________________________ Okay, ladies and gentlemen, before I start with the usual shenanigans and excuses, I do read reviews and before starting this chapter, I want to address some of the recurring critiques I've seen so far. 'First, about the flashbacks and pacing' — yes, I know the flashback arc is long and the pacing feels slow to some readers. That part is intentional. If I skip or rush this context, the story turns into just another fic where things "happen" without proper grounding. Naruto's mindset, Artemis's behavior, and the choices both of them make later won't make sense without this foundation. I understand it requires patience, but it matters for where the story is going. 'Regarding villains acting illogically' — Naruto was literally considered an idiot by his own village for most of his life. Arrogance plays a huge role here. No one expects the person they've always underestimated to actually listen, understand, or connect the dots. Even in canon, political figures like Danzo, Homura, and Koharu held secretive and questionable discussions inside the village itself. This isn't villains being dumb — it's them being careless because they believe they're untouchable. 'About Naruto being "nerfed"' — this Naruto does not trust the world around him. He is cautious by necessity. Acting openly like a shinobi or showing his full abilities risks him being watched, studied, or used as an experiment. This is not a power-fantasy Naruto. If he solves everything instantly, the story simply doesn't work. 'As for Naruto's logic during the revenge arc', there's a simple reason for many of his choices: he's surrounded by people. He cannot casually use ninjutsu, clones, or sensory abilities without exposing himself. His cover as Dhrithi's "illegitimate grandchild" matters. One wrong move, and the entire situation collapses. 'On the topic of the story being "mislabeled as a crossover"' — it isn't. The crossover elements are coming. I'm developing Naruto and Artemis first because rushing a crossover without character grounding kills emotional payoff. Percy Jackson elements will appear when they make narrative sense, not just to tick a box. 'For those who say Naruto doesn't feel like canon Naruto' — that's intentional. I didn't want another Talk-no-Jutsu Naruto. I personally disliked that aspect, and this version reflects a Naruto shaped by loss, restraint, and distrust rather than endless optimism. 'About pop-culture references' — yes, I use them, and I will continue to do so. They're meant as flavor and parallels, not replacements for the story itself. 'Finally, update frequency' — that's on me. I acknowledge it, and I'm working on being more consistent going forward. If you've stayed with the story despite its flaws, thank you. If you're here for where it's heading, I appreciate your patience. Now, let's move on. Okay... Hello people!
