So... enjoy! ________________________________________ "Walk," Leto said again, her hand firm but not rough at Artemis' shoulder. "I am walking," Artemis snapped, though her feet had slowed to a near stop. The street was louder today. Not dangerously so — just alive. Voices layered over one another, laughter spilling from open doorways, vendors calling out prices. Men moved through the crowd with the ease of people who did not think about where they placed their feet. Artemis felt every step. "They're everywhere," she muttered, eyes fixed ahead. Leto guided her gently out of the way as a group passed. "So are we," she said. Her voice was calm, but tired around the edges. "That's not the same." Artemis jerked her shoulder free. "They belong here." "And you don't?" Leto asked. Artemis stopped walking altogether. "Don't do that." "Do what?" "Pretend this is normal." Her voice rose despite her effort to keep it flat. A man brushed past them, murmuring an apology without breaking stride. The casualness of it made her stomach twist. "You think if I stay long enough, I'll stop noticing." Leto turned fully toward her. "I think noticing isn't the same as hating." "That's a lie." Artemis' hands clenched at her sides. "If I notice, I remember. And if I remember, I get angry." Leto's mouth tightened. "And anger keeps you sharp." "It keeps me right." They stood there, the noise pressing in around them. Men passed. Some laughed. Some argued. Some barely looked at her at all. Nothing happened. No threat rose to meet her fury. The world refused to confirm what she felt burning in her chest. Artemis hated that more than anything. "You don't hear it," she said suddenly. "The way they talk. You don't feel it in your bones the way I do." Leto opened her mouth — then closed it again. The answer she wanted didn't exist. "I feel different things," she said at last. "That doesn't mean I feel nothing." Artemis laughed, sharp and humorless. "Then why are we still here?" Because running won't save you. Because standing here is costing you less than leaving. Because I don't know what else to do. "Because," Leto said instead, "we're not done yet." Artemis looked away, jaw tight. Being told she wasn't done felt like another punishment layered on top of the first. "Come on," Leto said, softer now. "Just a little farther." Artemis moved — not because she agreed, but because stopping would mean losing ground she wasn't ready to give up. They hadn't gone far. The market narrowed into a knot of bodies and sound, the air thick with heat and voices. Leto slowed instinctively, eyes scanning for space, for a way through that wouldn't trap them. Artemis felt it too—the tightening, the sense of being funneled. "Stay close," Leto said. "I am close," Artemis replied, sharp, already bristling. A stall owner shouted. Coins clinked. Someone laughed too loudly behind them. Artemis kept her eyes forward, counting steps, measuring exits. That was when she felt it—the look before the movement, the attention that lingered just a fraction too long. "Don't," she muttered, barely audible. "What?" Leto asked. A man leaned against a post near the fruit crates, older, eyes tracking them without shame. When Artemis shifted left, he shifted too. When she stopped, he smiled—thin, knowing. "There," Artemis said, voice low. "You see him?" Leto followed her gaze. The smile. The step forward that wasn't quite a step. The way his eyes dipped and lingered. "Yes," Leto said quietly. Relief flooded Artemis so fast it almost made her dizzy. "So don't tell me I'm imagining it." "I'm not," Leto replied. The man spoke—something casual, something that didn't need translation. Artemis felt her pulse spike. She turned, squaring herself between him and Leto, chin lifted. "Back up," she said. Her voice didn't shake. The man chuckled and took another half-step. "Enough." Leto's voice cut in, steady but firm. The man looked at her now, annoyance flashing. He muttered something under his breath, eyes flicking back to Artemis one last time before he turned away, disappearing into the press of bodies. It was over. Artemis stood rigid, breath loud in her ears. "There," she said again, louder this time. "That's what I mean." Leto didn't contradict her. She rested a hand lightly at Artemis' back, grounding without caging. "You were right to notice," she said. Artemis laughed, brittle. "Right to notice," she repeated. "That's not what you said earlier." "I said noticing isn't the same as hating." "And I said it is." Artemis pulled away, anger surging back now that the danger had retreated. "He proved it. They always do." "Some do," Leto said. "That's enough," Artemis snapped. "I don't need 'some.' I need you to stop pretending this is safe." Leto closed her eyes for a brief moment. When she opened them, the fatigue was unmistakable. "I'm not pretending," she said. "I'm choosing how we move through it." Artemis shook her head, fury tight in her chest. The proof burned bright, undeniable. She held onto it like a shield. Because if she let it go— then the world would get complicated again. They didn't talk at first. They moved away from the market in silence, the noise thinning into distant echoes. Artemis walked ahead, too fast, shoulders tight. Leto followed, giving her space she didn't feel entitled to close. "You saw it," Artemis said suddenly, stopping short. "You saw him." "I did," Leto answered. She didn't reach for her this time. "Then stop saying it's the same," Artemis snapped. "Stop acting like this is just noise I can learn to ignore." "That's not what I'm saying." "That's what it feels like." Artemis turned on her, eyes bright, jaw trembling with the effort not to shout. "Every time you make me stay, it feels like you're telling me to swallow it. Like if I don't, I'm the problem." Leto winced. "I'm not asking you to swallow anything." "Then what are you asking?" Artemis demanded. "Because it feels like punishment. Like this is what I get for being right." "That's not fair." "I don't care if it's fair." The words came out sharp, ugly. "You weren't the one he looked at." Leto's breath caught. For a second, she looked very tired—older than Artemis remembered. "No," she said quietly. "I wasn't." "Then don't tell me how it feels," Artemis said. Her voice cracked despite herself, and that only made her angrier. "You keep saying you understand, but you don't. You get to choose calm. I don't." Leto took a step closer, then stopped. "I don't choose calm," she said. "I choose what won't trap you." "By trapping me anyway?" Artemis shot back. The accusation landed harder than she meant it to. She saw it in Leto's face—the flinch she tried to hide, the way her shoulders sagged a fraction. "I'm trying," Leto said. The words were simple. Too simple. "And I'm failing you right now. I know that." Artemis stared at her, thrown off balance by the admission. "Then stop," she said, softer now. "Just stop making me do this." Leto swallowed. She wanted to say I can't. She wanted to say this will pass. She wanted to say anything that would make it hurt less. Instead, she said nothing. The silence stretched. Somewhere nearby, a door slammed. A man laughed. Life continued, indifferent to the small war unfolding between them. Artemis turned away first. "I hate this," she muttered. "So do I," Leto said. But they stood there anyway—neither yielding, neither winning. And in that space between them, something shifted: not understanding, not peace, but the quiet knowledge that this fight wasn't ending today. They left the street behind and climbed the narrow stairs to the room they were staying in. The door closed with a soft, final click that seemed louder than it should have been. Outside, the city kept moving. Inside, the air felt too still. Artemis dropped onto the edge of the bed, arms folded tight across her chest. She didn't look at Leto. She didn't need to. Her anger sat heavy and hot, a thing she held onto because letting it go felt like losing ground. Leto stood near the window for a long moment, watching the light slide across the opposite wall. She could still hear the market in her bones—the noise, the closeness, the way Artemis' shoulders had stayed rigid even after they'd walked away. It wasn't the danger that troubled her most. It was the way staying here sharpened her daughter into something brittle. "This place is doing more harm than good," Leto said at last. Her voice was quiet, not meant to argue. Artemis' head snapped up. "So now you agree with me?" Leto shook her head. "No." That single word landed harder than a lecture. Artemis' jaw clenched. She looked away again, fingers digging into the fabric of the blanket. Leto crossed the room and sat on the chair instead of the bed, giving space without retreating. She chose her words carefully, then let most of them go. Explaining hadn't worked. Reassuring hadn't worked. Staying hadn't worked. "We're leaving," she said. Artemis let out a sharp breath that was almost a laugh. "Because I was right." "Because you're stuck," Leto replied, and immediately regretted how harsh it sounded. She softened her tone, not the decision. "Because every day here turns into a fight you don't get to rest from." "And where does that stop?" Artemis asked. "We leave every place that feels like this?" "No," Leto said. "We leave this one." Silence stretched between them. Artemis stared at the floor, anger shifting into something tighter, more dangerous. Losing control of direction always felt worse than losing an argument. "You don't get it," she said, quieter now. Leto closed her eyes for a heartbeat. I do, she thought. Too well. Aloud, she said nothing. She began to pack instead—slow, deliberate movements, folding clothes that didn't need folding. It wasn't escape. It was choice. When she finished, she paused at the door. "We'll go somewhere calmer," she said, almost to herself. "Somewhere we can breathe." Artemis didn't answer. She lay back on the bed and stared at the ceiling, anger simmering, resistance intact. But beneath it, something else stirred—unease, maybe. The sense that time was moving whether she wanted it to or not. They left Mexico without closure. The heat in India was different. It pressed down instead of closing in, heavy but honest, as if it expected to be endured rather than escaped. Artemis felt it the moment they stepped outside—on her scalp, along her spine, settling into her clothes. The noise was louder than Mexico, but less sharp. It moved in waves. They didn't come here by accident. Artemis knew that much. Leto didn't explain on the flight. She rarely did anymore. She carried herself with a steadiness that suggested the decision had already been argued through—privately, painfully—and resolved without Artemis' consent. That alone made Artemis bristle. A woman waited near the curb, a hand lifted in a small, unmistakably familiar gesture. Leto's pace changed when she saw her—not quicker, not slower, just certain. "Dhriti," Leto said, and there was relief in her voice that Artemis had never heard before. Dhriti smiled, lines deepening around her eyes. "You're late," she said, the words light, practiced. She hugged Leto without ceremony, as if they'd parted yesterday instead of years ago. Artemis stood a step back, arms crossed. She took Dhriti in quickly—plain clothes, steady posture, a presence that didn't rush to fill space. Nothing about her felt impressed or intimidated. That irritated Artemis more than curiosity would have. Dhriti glanced at her, then away again, respectful in the way adults are when they don't know how close to stand to a child who doesn't want company. "This must be your daughter," she said, not asking. Leto nodded. "Artemis." "Welcome," Dhriti said. No warmth layered on top of it. Just the word. They rode in silence through streets that seemed to fold into themselves—shops spilling onto sidewalks, people everywhere, men and women moving in the same currents. Artemis watched from the window, jaw tight. It felt like being dropped into the middle of something already in motion, with no edge to retreat to. When they reached Dhriti's place, Leto exhaled as if she'd been holding her breath since Mexico. Artemis noticed. She hated that she noticed. "You didn't bring us here because it's safe," Artemis said finally, when Dhriti stepped inside ahead of them. Leto set their bags down. "I brought us here because I know her." "That's it?" "That's enough." Artemis looked past her, into a home that wasn't arranged for guests or comfort, just lived in. She felt the familiar urge to resist rising again—sharp, reflexive. This wasn't refuge. It was proximity. People. Voices. Men outside, laughing somewhere down the street. Leto met her gaze, tired but unyielding. "We're staying for a while." Artemis turned away. She didn't agree. She didn't understand. But for the first time, the reason wasn't abstract. It had a name. A face. A history she wasn't part of. And that made resisting it harder. The street outside Dhriti's place narrowed as the afternoon wore on. What had been passable in the morning thickened into a living corridor—bodies, voices, heat rising from the ground. Artemis trailed behind Leto, hands tucked into her sleeves, eyes sharp and restless. This kind of closeness made her skin itch. It happened quickly. Too quickly for warning. A woman ahead of them stumbled as a crate tipped, oranges spilling across the road. She dropped to her knees, scrambling to gather them before they rolled into the gutter. A man shouted—impatient, sharp—and kicked one of the oranges back toward her with more force than necessary. Another man laughed. Someone said something Artemis didn't fully understand, but the tone carried enough meaning. The woman's shoulders hunched. She worked faster. Artemis stopped. There it was. The tight, familiar burn in her chest—the sense of being proven right without having to ask. She watched the men hover, the imbalance obvious even without words. No one struck the woman. No one needed to. The pressure was enough. "Hey," Artemis said before she could stop herself. The word came out rough, cutting through the noise. Leto's hand came down lightly at her wrist—not restraining, just present. "Wait," she said, low. Artemis shook her off. "You see that," she said, not asking. "You see how they talk to her." "I do," Leto replied. The woman finished gathering the oranges. One rolled away again. She reached for it, hesitated, then left it where it lay. One of the men scoffed as he passed. Artemis' fists clenched. This was different from Mexico. This wasn't subtle. This wasn't a look that could be ignored or a threat that vanished if you stood your ground. This was ordinary. Accepted. A thing that happened because no one bothered to stop it. "That's what I mean," Artemis said, voice tight. "That's what you keep telling me to endure." Leto didn't answer right away. She watched the woman stand, dust herself off, and move on without looking back. The moment closed around them like a bruise. "I'm not telling you it doesn't exist," Leto said finally. "Then stop acting like it's rare." Leto's mouth pressed into a thin line. She didn't contradict that either. A few steps ahead, Dhriti had turned back. She hadn't said anything. She hadn't intervened. She simply looked at Artemis—not with approval or dismissal, but with something like recognition. As if she had seen this before and learned not to rush toward it. That unsettled Artemis more than anger would have. They moved on, the street swallowing the incident whole. The noise returned to its usual rhythm. Men laughed again. Someone called out prices. Life smoothed itself over. Artemis walked with her jaw tight, heart pounding—not from fear, but from vindication. This, at least, made sense. The world was cruel where it mattered. Her anger had somewhere solid to stand. And that made letting go of it feel impossible. The street outside Dhriti's place narrowed as the afternoon wore on. What had been passable in the morning thickened into a living corridor—bodies, voices, heat rising from the ground. Artemis trailed behind Leto, hands tucked into her sleeves, eyes sharp and restless. This kind of closeness made her skin itch. It happened quickly. Too quickly for warning. A woman ahead of them stumbled as a crate tipped, oranges spilling across the road. She dropped to her knees, scrambling to gather them before they rolled into the gutter. A man shouted—impatient, sharp—and kicked one of the oranges back toward her with more force than necessary. Another man laughed. Someone said something Artemis didn't fully understand, but the tone carried enough meaning. The woman's shoulders hunched. She worked faster. Artemis stopped. There it was. The tight, familiar burn in her chest—the sense of being proven right without having to ask. She watched the men hover, the imbalance obvious even without words. No one struck the woman. No one needed to. The pressure was enough. "Hey," Artemis said before she could stop herself. The word came out rough, cutting through the noise. Leto's hand came down lightly at her wrist—not restraining, just present. "Wait," she said, low. Artemis shook her off. "You see that," she said, not asking. "You see how they talk to her." "I do," Leto replied. The woman finished gathering the oranges. One rolled away again. She reached for it, hesitated, then left it where it lay. One of the men scoffed as he passed. Artemis' fists clenched. This was different from Mexico. This wasn't subtle. This wasn't a look that could be ignored or a threat that vanished if you stood your ground. This was ordinary. Accepted. A thing that happened because no one bothered to stop it. "That's what I mean," Artemis said, voice tight. "That's what you keep telling me to endure." Leto didn't answer right away. She watched the woman stand, dust herself off, and move on without looking back. The moment closed around them like a bruise. "I'm not telling you it doesn't exist," Leto said finally. "Then stop acting like it's rare." Leto's mouth pressed into a thin line. She didn't contradict that either. A few steps ahead, Dhriti had turned back. She hadn't said anything. She hadn't intervened. She simply looked at Artemis—not with approval or dismissal, but with something like recognition. As if she had seen this before and learned not to rush toward it. That unsettled Artemis more than anger would have. They moved on, the street swallowing the incident whole. The noise returned to its usual rhythm. Men laughed again. Someone called out prices. Life smoothed itself over. Artemis walked with her jaw tight, heart pounding—not from fear, but from vindication. This, at least, made sense. The world was cruel where it mattered. Her anger had somewhere solid to stand. And that made letting go of it feel impossible. The afternoon stretched thick and slow. Dhriti's ceiling fan pushed warm air in patient circles, its rhythm steady enough to fade into the background. Artemis sat on the floor near the window, back against the wall, knees pulled close. Outside, the street hummed—voices, footsteps, a vendor calling out something sing-song. Men's voices threaded through it all. She noticed every one. Dhriti moved around the room with unhurried familiarity, setting out cups, rinsing them, setting them down again because she'd changed her mind. Leto leaned against the doorway, arms crossed, watching in the quiet way she'd learned to adopt when stepping in would only make things worse. Artemis broke the silence first. "You don't say much." Dhriti glanced at her. "I say enough." "That's convenient," Artemis replied. "Means you don't have to take sides." Dhriti huffed a short laugh and reached for the kettle. "Everyone takes sides," she said. "Some of us just don't announce it." Artemis shifted, irritation sparking. "So which one are you?" Dhriti poured water without answering. Leto's gaze flicked to her—not a warning, not a plea. Just attention. Dhriti felt it and rolled her eyes lightly, the gesture so familiar it might as well have been shared language. "Your mother looks tired," Dhriti said, setting a cup in front of Leto. "You wear people out." Leto snorted. "You say that like it's new." "It isn't," Dhriti replied. "That's the problem." Artemis bristled. "You don't know me." Dhriti finally sat, elbows on the table, looking at Artemis directly. "I know enough to see when someone wants a fight." "And?" Artemis challenged. "And I'm not giving you one." Dhriti shrugged. "If you want to be angry, be angry. Just don't expect me to dance for it." The dismissal landed harder than argument. Artemis opened her mouth, then closed it again. There was nothing to push against. No authority to rebel against. No rule to break. Leto watched this exchange with something like relief—and guilt. She didn't step in. She let it stand. Dhriti noticed and gave her a sideways look that said you owe me later. Leto's mouth twitched despite herself. Outside, laughter rose and fell. Artemis' shoulders tightened, then loosened a fraction when no one reacted to her reaction. Dhriti stood and reached for a cloth. "Dinner's in an hour," she said. "You can sulk until then." Artemis glared. "I don't sulk." Dhriti smiled faintly. "Of course not." Leto met Dhriti's eyes as Artemis turned back to the window. A brief look passed between them—tired, knowing, unspoken. Leto exhaled, some small knot loosening in her chest. Artemis felt it without understanding it: the ground shifting under her feet, not because she was being forced—but because no one was pushing back. And that unsettled her more than resistance ever had. Evening settled in layers. The house softened as the heat eased, shadows stretching across the floor in long, familiar shapes. Artemis drifted to the far end of the corridor under the excuse of washing her hands, then lingered there longer than necessary. She could hear the kitchen—cups set down, a drawer opened, the quiet scrape of a chair. Adult sounds. Private ones. Dhriti and Leto stood at the counter, shoulder to shoulder, moving around each other without looking. Dhriti reached for sugar; Leto slid the kettle closer. It was practiced, wordless, the kind of coordination that came from years of knowing how the other occupied space. "You look worse than the last time," Dhriti said, not unkindly. Leto gave a tired huff. "That was before." "Before what?" "Before everything became a negotiation." Dhriti snorted softly and poured. "Children do that. You let them." Leto leaned her hip against the counter. "You say that like I enjoy it." "I say it like you're exhausted." Dhriti glanced at her, then back to the cups. "And stubborn." "That's rich, coming from you." Dhriti smiled to herself. "I never said I wasn't." They drank in silence for a moment. Outside, voices passed the window—men talking, a scooter sputtering past. Leto flinched, barely noticeable. Dhriti noticed anyway. "She's sharp," Dhriti said. "And she's hurting." "I know." Leto's voice was low. "I don't know how to help without making it worse." Dhriti set her cup down with care. "You don't fix this kind of thing. You stand next to it and hope time does its work." "That feels like doing nothing." "It isn't." Dhriti's tone was flat. "It just doesn't look heroic." Leto laughed quietly at that, a sound halfway to breaking. "You always hated heroics." "They're messy," Dhriti said. "And they make children think pain has an ending." Leto rubbed her eyes. "You make it sound so simple." "I make it sound survivable." Dhriti glanced toward the corridor. "That's different." Artemis shifted, heart thudding. She hadn't meant to listen. She told herself she'd leave—but she stayed, caught by the way the two women shared the weight without naming it. Dhriti picked up a plate and nudged it toward Leto. "Eat something. You get unbearable when you don't." Leto rolled her eyes, but took the plate. "You're unbearable regardless." "See?" Dhriti said lightly. "Sisters." They shared a look—fond, exasperated, unguarded. Artemis felt it then, sharp and disorienting: her mother was not alone here. The world had a place where Leto could lean without falling. Artemis retreated quietly, unease blooming where anger usually lived. She didn't know what to do with this knowledge. It didn't fit any argument she'd prepared. And that made resisting everything feel suddenly… harder. Morning arrived without ceremony. The house woke in pieces—the clink of a spoon, the fan's steady whir, footsteps crossing the hall. Artemis sat at the small table, tracing the grain in the wood with her finger. She hadn't slept well. The sounds from the street had followed her into dreams, familiar and intrusive. Dhriti moved past her with a cup of tea, setting it down where Leto would sit without asking. Leto appeared moments later, hair still damp, shoulders drawn tight in that way Artemis had begun to recognize. Tired, but resolved. They ate quietly. It wasn't tense. That almost made it worse. Outside, a man called out to someone across the road. Artemis' hand stilled. She waited for the familiar flare of anger. It came—smaller, slower, edged with irritation at itself. Leto noticed. She always did. "You don't have to be on guard every second," she said, not looking at Artemis as she spoke. The words were careful, almost tentative. Artemis' shoulders rose. "So now it's my fault if I am." "That's not what I said." "It's what you mean," Artemis replied, voice flat. She pushed her chair back a fraction, already retreating. Leto set her cup down. "What I mean is—" She stopped, then tried again. "What happened before doesn't get to decide everything that comes after." Artemis laughed once, humorless. "It already did." Dhriti didn't intervene. She rinsed a cup at the sink, listening without pretending not to. Leto's voice softened. "You're allowed to notice danger. I'm asking you not to let it become the only thing you see." Artemis stood. The movement was abrupt, a line drawn. "I see exactly what's there." "And when something doesn't fit?" Leto asked. Artemis' jaw tightened. She didn't answer. She picked up her bag instead, slinging it over her shoulder as if preparing to leave a room she couldn't escape. Dhriti turned from the sink. "The market's quieter in the morning," she said mildly. "If you want to walk." Artemis shot her a look. Dhriti met it without flinching. No challenge. No invitation. "I'm not hungry," Artemis said, already moving toward the door. Leto watched her go, the familiar knot tightening in her chest. She hadn't pushed. She hadn't forced. Still, the words lingered between them—questions Artemis refused to touch. Dhriti dried her hands and leaned back against the counter. "You poked the bruise," she said. Leto rubbed her temples. "I had to." "Maybe." Dhriti tilted her head. "Just don't keep poking the same spot and expect a different reaction." Leto nodded, though the answer didn't soothe her. Outside, Artemis walked fast, shoulders set, certainty clenched like a shield. But with each step, it weighed a little more than it had yesterday. The afternoon slowed into something softer. Heat lingered, but it no longer pressed. Dhriti sat on the low stool near the window, sorting through a small tin of old letters and photographs—things she touched without ceremony, as if they were tools rather than relics. Leto watched from the table, hands wrapped around a cooling cup of tea. Artemis was supposed to be in the back room. She wasn't listening. She told herself that as she paused near the doorway, half-hidden by the wall, the voices drifting toward her. "I found these cleaning," Dhriti said. "Thought I'd throw them out. Didn't." Leto smiled faintly. "You never do." Dhriti shrugged. She lifted a photograph—creased, corners soft. A man stood beside her, younger, uniform pressed too neatly. "He hated being photographed," she said. "Said it made him look like he was posing for a future he wouldn't get." Leto didn't respond right away. She knew when to let Dhriti keep going. "He wasn't gentle," Dhriti continued, matter-of-fact. "Not with words. Not always with patience. But he showed up." She set the photograph down. "When he didn't, it was never because he didn't care. It was because he couldn't." "That's not an excuse," Leto said quietly. "No," Dhriti agreed. "It's an explanation. They're different." Leto exhaled, the tension in her shoulders easing just enough to be noticed. "I worry I'm teaching her the wrong lesson," she said. "That endurance looks like approval." Dhriti leaned back, folding her arms. "You're teaching her how to stay standing," she said. "Approval is optional." Artemis felt a flicker of irritation. The words scraped at something she hadn't named. Dhriti tapped the tin closed. "I don't defend men," she said. "I don't condemn them either. I defend choices. Day by day. That's all I trust." Leto nodded slowly. "She wants certainty." "Everyone does," Dhriti replied. "Children just ask for it louder." They shared a brief look—one that carried years of disagreement, grief, and affection without needing any of it spoken. Artemis shifted her weight, suddenly aware she was intruding on something not meant for her. Dhriti stood, sliding the tin back onto the shelf. "She'll hear what she's ready to hear," she said. "Until then, let her be angry." "And if it hardens her?" Leto asked. Dhriti met her gaze. "Then we keep living anyway." Artemis stepped back before she could be noticed. The conversation left a strange taste behind—no comfort, no instruction, just the weight of things that had happened and continued to happen. She didn't agree with what she'd heard. But she couldn't dismiss it either. And that—more than any argument—made her angry all over again. The evening unraveled the way it always did—slowly at first, then all at once. The fan clicked as it changed speed. The radio shifted stations on its own, settling into a familiar tune. Dinner simmered, its smell filling the house until Artemis felt crowded by it. She paced. Not the restless pacing of boredom, but the sharp, measured steps of someone trying to outrun a thought that wouldn't loosen its grip. What she'd overheard earlier clung to her like heat—explanations instead of excuses, endurance instead of certainty. It felt unfair. Dangerous. Dhriti noticed first. She always did. She said nothing, just slid a plate toward Artemis when the food was ready. Artemis ignored it. Leto waited. When Artemis didn't sit, she spoke—too softly, too carefully. "Eat something." "I'm not hungry." "You are," Leto said, then stopped herself from saying more. The pause stretched. Dhriti continued serving, unbothered, humming under her breath. The normalcy pressed on Artemis harder than anger ever had. "You're both pretending," Artemis said suddenly. Leto looked up. Dhriti did not. "Pretending what?" Leto asked. "That this is fine," Artemis snapped. "That standing around talking about 'choices' changes what people do." Dhriti set the spoon down. "No one said it does." "That's what you meant," Artemis said, turning on her now. "You talk like if you just live with it long enough, it stops being wrong." Dhriti finally faced her. "I talk like someone who has lived with it," she said evenly. "That doesn't make it right." "No," Dhriti agreed. "It makes it real." The word hit harder than Artemis expected. Her chest tightened. The anger surged to cover it. "You think you understand," Artemis said, voice rising. "You think because you survived something, you get to tell everyone else how to feel about it." Leto stood. "Artemis—" "No," Artemis cut in. She turned back to Dhriti, words tumbling faster now, sharper, crueler than she intended. "You're just used to it. That's all. You let it happen so often it stopped bothering you." The room went very still. Dhriti didn't raise her voice. She didn't move. "That's not true," she said. Artemis scoffed. "Then why didn't you stop it? Outside. Earlier." Dhriti's gaze didn't waver. "Because not every wrong ends cleanly," she said. "And because you don't always get to be the one who fixes it." Artemis felt something crack. The words came out before she could stop them, ugly and unfair. "Then maybe you shouldn't talk like you know better." Silence followed—thick, heavy. Leto's face tightened. "That was unnecessary." Artemis spun toward her. "So now you're taking her side." Leto didn't answer right away. When she did, her voice was tired. "I'm asking you to stop hurting people because you're hurting." That was the worst thing she could have said. Artemis turned away, throat burning, anger collapsing into something she refused to name. She hated that she'd crossed a line. Hated that part of her knew it. And hated most of all that she couldn't take the words back. Night settled over the house like a held breath. The radio had been turned off. The fan still turned, but slower now, its rhythm uneven. Artemis sat on the edge of the bed in the back room, hands clenched together so tightly her knuckles ached. The words she'd thrown earlier kept circling back, sharper each time they returned. No one came to correct her. That was worse than being scolded. From the other room came the faint sounds of life continuing—plates stacked, water running, a cupboard opening and closing. Dhriti moved with deliberate quiet, not tiptoeing, not slamming anything either. Just existing. Leto helped for a while, then stopped. Artemis could picture it without seeing: her mother leaning against the counter, shoulders sagging, too tired to pretend she wasn't hurt. She waited for anger to return. It didn't. What came instead was a tight, hollow feeling under her ribs. The door creaked softly. Leto stepped inside, not looking at Artemis at first. She straightened a blanket that didn't need straightening, picked up a book Artemis hadn't opened. The small, ordinary motions felt heavier than shouting would have. "You crossed a line," Leto said at last. Artemis didn't answer. Leto didn't push. She sat on the chair near the window, facing away, giving Artemis the choice to speak or not. Outside, men's voices drifted past—low, unremarkable, fading as quickly as they came. Artemis noticed them and hated that they no longer sparked immediate rage. "I'm not asking you to apologize," Leto continued, voice subdued. "I'm asking you to sit with what you said." Artemis swallowed. Her throat burned. "She deserved it," she muttered, even as the conviction slipped. Leto closed her eyes briefly. "No," she said. "She didn't." Silence stretched again. The fan ticked. Somewhere, a door shut gently. Artemis stared at the floor. Images pressed in uninvited: Dhriti's steady gaze, the way she hadn't flinched. The absence of retaliation. It felt like standing at the edge of something and realizing there was no one there to push you back. "I didn't mean all of it," Artemis said finally. The admission felt like tearing something loose. "I know," Leto replied. That was it. No reassurance followed. No comfort wrapped around the moment. Leto stood, paused at the door, then left the room as quietly as she'd entered. Artemis lay back on the bed and stared at the ceiling. The anger she relied on felt dull now, blunted by the weight of consequences that weren't loud or dramatic. No one hated her. No one chased her away. They had simply gone on. And in that quiet, Artemis felt something shift—not understanding, not acceptance, but the unsettling awareness that her words mattered even when they didn't explode. She closed her eyes, wishing the certainty would come back as easily as the anger once had. They left early. The house was quieter than usual, the kind of quiet that comes from decisions already made. Dhriti moved through the kitchen with practiced efficiency, packing food into containers Artemis hadn't asked for and would pretend not to want. Leto stood nearby, checking nothing in particular, her attention divided between the clock and the back room where Artemis waited. Artemis emerged last, bag slung over one shoulder. Her eyes flicked briefly toward Dhriti, then away again. The anger from the night before had dulled, leaving behind a tight, uncomfortable awareness she didn't know how to shake. Dhriti handed Leto a small parcel. "For the road," she said. "And for when she says she isn't hungry." Leto took it with a faint smile. "She always does." "I always am," Artemis muttered. Dhriti's mouth curved, just slightly. "That too." They stood there for a moment that stretched longer than necessary. Not awkward. Weighted. Leto reached out and squeezed Dhriti's arm—brief, firm. A gesture that carried more than words would have. "Don't disappear," Dhriti said. Leto snorted softly. "I never do." "That's not what I said." Leto met her gaze, something weary and grateful passing between them. "I'll write." "You'd better," Dhriti replied. "I like knowing where my people are." Artemis watched this exchange from the side, unease stirring again. The certainty she clung to had no place here. There was no argument to win, no authority to rebel against. Just connection she wasn't part of. Outside, the street had already come alive. Men walked past carrying bags, laughing, arguing about nothing. Artemis noticed them—and noticed herself noticing. The reaction came slower this time, frustration replacing fury. As they walked away, Dhriti didn't follow. She stood in the doorway, arms folded, watching until they turned the corner. She didn't wave. Leto exhaled once they were out of sight. The release was small, but real. She adjusted the strap on Artemis' bag without asking. "We're going somewhere quieter," she said. Artemis nodded stiffly. She didn't ask where. Control had already slipped; knowing the destination wouldn't give it back. India receded behind them—not as a lesson learned or a truth accepted, but as a place that had unsettled her in ways she hadn't expected. The anger she brought with her hadn't vanished. It had just lost its sharpest edge. That frightened her more than hatred ever had. Because hatred was simple. And simplicity, she was beginning to realize, was no longer something the world was willing to offer her. Omaha, New Zealand The air felt different the moment they stepped outside. Cooler. Cleaner. It moved instead of pressing down, carrying the smell of salt and something green Artemis couldn't name. The sky seemed wider here, stretched thin and pale, as if it had more room to be what it was. They didn't rush. No crowds funneled them forward. No voices overlapped until meaning blurred. The road curved away toward the coast, and beyond it, water broke against rocks in an easy, patient rhythm. Artemis slowed without realizing she'd done it. Leto noticed. She always did. She didn't comment. They walked along the edge of the path, the ground uneven underfoot. Grass bent with the wind, then righted itself again. Men passed them—joggers, a couple carrying boards, someone calling to a friend farther down the beach. Artemis tracked each one automatically, waiting for the familiar spark of anger. It came late. Smaller. Like a match struck against damp stone. She frowned, unsettled by the delay. "This is quiet," she said finally. Leto nodded. "That was the idea." "That won't change anything." "No," Leto agreed. "It just gives you room." Artemis scoffed, but the sound lacked bite. She stared out at the water instead, watching the waves break and reform without force. Nothing demanded her attention here. No one leaned too close. No voices crowded her head. She didn't relax. Not fully. But the tension in her shoulders loosened enough for her to feel it slipping. They reached a low rise overlooking the shore and stopped. Leto sat on a rock, stretching her legs. Artemis remained standing, arms crossed, scanning the beach again. Men laughed somewhere behind them. A child shouted. A gull wheeled overhead, indifferent. Nothing happened. That was the problem. The anger Artemis relied on felt misplaced here, like armor worn in the wrong climate. She kept it on anyway, jaw tight, waiting for the world to justify it. "Don't wander too far," Leto said. Artemis glanced at her. The instruction wasn't sharp. It wasn't worried. Just practical. "I won't," she replied, surprised at herself. She walked a few steps closer to the water, boots sinking slightly into sand. The sound of the waves filled her ears, drowning out everything else. Men existed here too—moving, talking, living—but they didn't press. They didn't intrude. Artemis stood there longer than she meant to. The resistance was still there. Coiled. Ready. But for the first time, it felt tired—like something held too tightly for too long. She turned back toward Leto, irritation flickering as she recognized the shift. This place wasn't changing her mind. It was wearing her down. The morning came in pale bands of light, slipping through the curtains and settling on the floor like something tentative. Artemis woke before Leto and lay still, listening. Wind moved through the trees outside. Waves broke in a steady rhythm she hadn't learned to ignore yet. No horns. No shouting. No overlapping voices demanding attention. She sat up slowly, waiting for the familiar surge of readiness—the tightening in her chest, the reflexive anger that sharpened her senses. It arrived late again, dulled at the edges. That unsettled her enough to make her scowl. Outside, the beach was already awake. A few people walked along the shore, spaced far enough apart that their paths never crossed. Artemis lingered at the edge of the sand, boots still on, eyes tracking movement out of habit. A man passed with a dog, nodding once without slowing. Another jogged by farther down, focused on his own breath. They existed near her without claiming any part of her space. She waited for the moment when one of them would break the unspoken line. None did. Leto joined her after a while, carrying two cups. She handed one over without comment and sat on a low driftwood log, staring out at the water. Artemis took the cup, more out of reflex than hunger, and held it between her palms. A group approached farther down the beach—three men, loud in a way that grated. Artemis' shoulders tightened. She stepped back a pace, already bracing. The men veered naturally, their path bending away without anyone needing to say a word. Their laughter faded as they moved on. Artemis frowned. She hadn't moved quickly enough for it to be avoidance. She hadn't challenged them into giving her space. It had simply… happened. She felt foolish for having prepared for a fight that never came. "You saw that," she said, unable to keep the edge from her voice. Leto nodded. "I did." "They didn't—" Artemis stopped, frustration knotting her words. "They didn't do anything." Leto didn't answer. She sipped her drink, letting the quiet do what talking couldn't. Artemis kicked at the sand, irritation flaring. Respect without confrontation felt wrong. Unsatisfying. It left her with nothing to push against, nothing to prove. She walked closer to the water, watching it pull back and surge forward again. The boundary held every time—firm, predictable, unmoved by her presence. She could stand at the edge without being swallowed. Artemis hated how much that image fit. The resistance was still there, coiled tight. But it no longer felt sharpened by constant threat. It felt worn thin by the effort of staying ready when readiness wasn't required. She turned back toward Leto, annoyance flickering when she realized she didn't want to leave yet. That, more than anything else, frightened her. The beach had filled a little by midday—not crowded, just occupied. Families settled on towels farther down, a couple arguing quietly near the rocks, a few men wading into the water without ceremony. The rhythm of it all had become familiar enough to dull her edge, and she hated that familiarity most of all. She stepped closer. Not by accident. Not because she drifted. She walked toward a cluster of people near the shoreline, boots sinking into sand still damp from the tide. Her shoulders squared, senses sharpened. This was what she knew how to do: test the space, brace for the inevitable correction. A man looked up as she approached. Middle-aged, sunburned, distracted. He noticed her proximity and shifted without comment, angling himself away to give her room. No glare. No remark. Just adjustment. Artemis slowed, thrown. She took another step—closer than she normally would. Close enough to feel intrusive. The man glanced again, hesitated, then stepped back entirely, muttering a brief apology that sounded more reflex than meaning. "I didn't—" he began, then stopped, already moving away. Artemis stood there, heat rushing to her face. She hadn't said anything. She hadn't demanded space. She hadn't been threatened. She had been… accommodated. Leto watched from a distance, leaning against a low fence. She didn't intervene. She didn't call Artemis back. She let the moment exist exactly as it was—awkward, unfinished. Artemis' chest tightened. This wasn't how it was supposed to go. There was no justification for anger here, no proof to cling to. The familiar narrative slipped through her fingers, leaving her exposed to something worse: uncertainty. She turned sharply and walked back toward Leto, irritation sparking. "They moved," she said, almost accusingly. Leto nodded. "You looked like you wanted space." "I didn't ask for it." "You didn't have to." That answer didn't satisfy her. It felt like losing ground without a fight. Artemis kicked at the sand, then stopped. The movement felt childish, and she hated that too. "This place is wrong," she muttered. "It doesn't make sense." Leto studied her for a moment. "It doesn't have to," she said. "You're allowed to notice without deciding what it means yet." Artemis looked back at the water. The tide crept in and retreated again, leaving clear lines in the sand—boundaries drawn and erased without drama. She had crossed a line and nothing had punished her for it. The resistance inside her shifted, tightening not with rage, but with effort. Holding onto certainty here felt like gripping something smooth and heavy, slowly slipping no matter how hard she squeezed. Artemis stepped back from the shoreline and sat down hard beside Leto, arms folded tight. She didn't look at her mother. She wasn't ready to change her mind. But she was beginning to realize that resisting no longer came naturally. It didn't stay quiet forever. The afternoon thinned into a brittle kind of brightness, the sun sharp against the water. Artemis wandered along the edge of the beach while Leto sat farther back, half-watching, half-resting. The ease of the morning lingered—too smooth, too accommodating. Artemis hated how quickly she'd started to expect it. Voices rose near the path behind her. Not loud at first. Just careless. A group had gathered by the benches—young men, restless, bored. Their laughter carried, edged with something off. Artemis felt it before she understood it, the old tightening returning with unwelcome familiarity. She slowed, listening without meaning to. One of them said something that drew another laugh. A glance followed—brief, appraising. Not threatening. Not respectful either. The kind of look that measured and moved on. Artemis' shoulders squared automatically. There it was. Proof returning like a bitter relief. She stopped, waiting for it to escalate. It didn't. A woman pushing a stroller passed between them and the group, her presence cutting the moment in half. One of the men muttered something under his breath—annoyed, dismissive—but another elbowed him sharply. The comment died unfinished. The laughter shifted, losing its edge. Artemis watched, unsettled. Her mother was already watching—not alarmed, not distant. Present. "They said something," Artemis said when she reached her. Leto nodded. "I heard." "And you're just… fine with that?" "No," Leto replied. "I'm realistic about it." Artemis frowned. "That's the same thing." "It isn't," Leto said quietly. "Being aware doesn't mean giving up. It means choosing where to spend your strength." Artemis looked back toward the benches. The group had shifted, interest already moving elsewhere. The moment had passed without exploding into something she could point at and condemn. "That's worse," Artemis muttered. "It means it can happen and disappear like it never mattered." Leto considered her. "It mattered," she said. "Just not enough to own the whole day." Artemis sat heavily in the sand, anger flaring and then stalling. The old certainty tried to rise—this is why I'm right—but it didn't fit cleanly anymore. The wrong hadn't been ignored. It hadn't been indulged either. It had been handled. She hated how unsatisfying that felt. The resistance inside her tightened again, but it no longer burned. It weighed. Holding it together took effort now, constant and dull. Artemis stared out at the water, jaw clenched. The world wasn't proving her wrong. It just wasn't letting her be completely right anymore. The tide was lower by late afternoon, leaving long bands of wet sand that reflected the sky like dull mirrors. People moved around it now—families packing up, a couple shaking sand from towels, a man jogging past with earbuds in. The group of young men was still there, but quieter, their energy dispersed. One of them argued with another in low tones; a third wandered off toward the path. Nothing dramatic. Nothing neat. A woman with a clipboard approached them, speaking calmly. Artemis couldn't hear the words, only the tone—firm without being sharp. One of the men shrugged, annoyed, but he listened. Another nodded, embarrassed. The interaction ended quickly, almost anticlimactic. Artemis stopped walking. No one had been chased away. No one had been shamed into spectacle. The moment didn't become a story anyone would retell later. It simply… closed. She felt oddly cheated. Leto came to stand beside her without comment. They watched together as the woman checked something off and moved on, already focused elsewhere. The beach resumed its rhythm, the interruption absorbed without leaving a mark. "That's it?" Artemis asked. Leto didn't answer immediately. She followed the woman's path with her eyes. "That's often how it looks," she said. "When it works." Artemis frowned. "No one paid for it." "They adjusted," Leto replied. "Sometimes that's the point." Artemis kicked at a shallow pool, ripples breaking the reflected sky. "It feels like letting them off." The resistance inside her shifted again, no longer spiking in response to threat. It reorganized itself, searching for a shape that fit this version of the world—one that corrected without spectacle, that spread responsibility thin instead of pinning it to a single face. She didn't like it. But she couldn't deny it existed. The sun dipped lower, light softening. Artemis turned away from the shoreline and walked back toward Leto without comment. She sat beside her, close enough that their shoulders nearly touched. She didn't say she was tired. She didn't have to. They packed without discussion. Leto folded clothes on the bed, neat stacks forming and reforming as she reconsidered what they'd need next. Artemis watched from the doorway, arms crossed out of habit rather than defiance. The room smelled faintly of salt and clean fabric, the window cracked open to let the air move. Normally, this was where it happened. The question. The protest. The sharp insistence on knowing why. Artemis felt it rise—and stall. She told herself she didn't care where they were going. That it didn't matter. But the truth sat heavier than indifference: she was tired. Not the physical kind. The kind that came from holding a position long after it stopped giving anything back. Leto paused, glancing over her shoulder. She waited. Artemis said nothing. The silence stretched, unfamiliar. Leto didn't fill it. She returned to packing, slower now, careful not to turn the quiet into a challenge. They walked outside together once the bags were ready. The path curved away from the beach, grass bending underfoot. Men passed them—some nodding, some absorbed in their own conversations. Artemis noticed them and let the noticing pass through her without gripping it. Leto stopped near the car. She rested a hand on the door, hesitating just long enough for Artemis to register the moment as an opening. "Anywhere is fine," Artemis said, the words coming out flatter than she expected. Leto turned, surprise flickering across her face before she masked it. "Are you sure?" Artemis shrugged. "It's all the same." It wasn't. They both knew that. Leto nodded once and opened the door. As they drove, the coastline fell away behind them. The road stretched ahead, empty in long, forgiving lines. Artemis watched the scenery change—water to trees to open fields—without marking the transitions as losses or victories. Her resistance was still there. She felt it, coiled and ready. But it no longer surged to meet every decision. It waited now, conserving itself. That frightened her. She preferred anger. It burned clean. This felt like erosion—slow, invisible, inevitable. Leto glanced at her once, then back to the road. She didn't comment. She didn't reach out. She let the quiet stand as it was, trusting it not to break. Artemis closed her eyes and let the movement lull her, the road carrying them forward without asking permission. For the first time since this chapter began, she didn't fight the direction. She didn't accept it either. She simply let it happen. The work was simple. That was the point. They spent the morning helping an older couple a short walk from where they were staying—nothing official, nothing framed as volunteering. Just lifting crates, rinsing sand from equipment, carrying things from one place to another because there were hands available. Artemis hadn't been asked directly. Leto had agreed, and Artemis had followed without comment. She took a crate when it was passed to her. It was heavier than it looked. She adjusted her grip and carried it across the yard, set it down where the woman gestured, and went back for another. No one praised her. No one corrected her. The task existed whether she participated or not. Men worked alongside her—quiet, focused, older than the ones on the beach. They spoke when necessary and not when it wasn't. Space was negotiated without words: a step back here, a pause there. Artemis noticed it all, the way she always did, but the noticing didn't escalate into readiness. It stayed practical. She wiped sweat from her forehead and reached for another crate. The woman thanked her, brief and sincere. Artemis nodded once, unsure what to do with gratitude that didn't demand anything in return. Leto worked nearby, sleeves rolled up, movements steady. She didn't hover. She didn't check on Artemis more than anyone else. The equality of it pressed gently, insistently. Artemis was neither protected nor tested. She was simply included. By midday, the work wound down. The couple offered water. Artemis took it without thinking, drank, and felt the small relief spread through her chest. She realized then how long she'd been bracing for nothing. A man stepped aside to let her pass, apologizing when their paths nearly crossed. Artemis nodded and moved on. The interaction didn't linger. It didn't echo. She sat on the low wall and watched the rest of the work finish. The resistance inside her shifted again—not loosening, not tightening. Settling. Like something that had learned it wouldn't be needed every moment. Leto joined her, handing over a piece of fruit. Artemis accepted it automatically, then paused, surprised at herself. She ate anyway. No one asked her what she thought about any of it. That, more than anything, kept the day intact. They walked back in the afternoon light, quiet stretching comfortably between them. Artemis didn't feel lighter. She felt… less strained. The difference was subtle, but it mattered. The world hadn't demanded anything from her today. She'd done things anyway. And the resistance, for once, hadn't been the thing carrying her forward. The invitation was small enough to be missed. They were sitting on the low steps near the water when it came—someone asking if Artemis wanted to help untangle a fishing line snagged around a post. No pressure. No expectation. Just a question offered and left hanging. Artemis hesitated. Her body leaned forward before her mind caught up. She stood, took two steps, and then stopped. The resistance flared—not sharp, not angry, but instinctive. Connection required proximity. Proximity invited expectation. Expectation led to disappointment or demand. "I'm fine," she said, the words arriving late and thin. The person nodded and turned away without comment, already moving on. No irritation. No insistence. The ease of it unsettled her more than refusal would have. Artemis sat back down harder than necessary, irritation flickering at herself. She watched the task continue from a distance—hands working patiently, the line loosening inch by inch. A man laughed quietly when it finally came free. Someone thanked someone else. The moment closed without her. She told herself she preferred it that way. Leto noticed the shift beside her—the tension returning like a reflex—but didn't ask. She stared out at the water, letting the horizon hold her attention. The choice not to comment felt deliberate. Artemis hated how visible that made her hesitation feel. "I almost went," Artemis muttered. Leto glanced at her, expression neutral. "I saw." "And?" "And you didn't," Leto said. "That's allowed." The permission landed awkwardly. Artemis frowned. "You're not going to say anything?" "I just did." Artemis picked at a loose thread on her sleeve, jaw tight. The world here kept offering her exits that didn't slam shut when she ignored them. It was exhausting to keep refusing without the fuel of outrage. A breeze picked up, carrying salt and the distant sound of waves breaking. Men and women moved past them in loose patterns—talking, working, laughing—without forming the lines Artemis expected. The resistance inside her strained, searching for something solid to brace against. She stood again, abruptly, and walked a few steps away—far enough to reclaim distance, close enough to remain present. The compromise irritated her. Artemis looked back once, catching Leto's eye. Her mother hadn't moved. She hadn't followed. She hadn't turned away either. That steadiness made Artemis' chest ache. She returned to the steps and sat, shoulders hunched, anger simmering at nothing in particular. The near-connection lingered like an itch she couldn't scratch—unresolved, unfinished. She hadn't crossed the line. But she'd seen it. And knowing where it was made pretending it didn't exist a little harder than before. It happened later, when no one was watching for it. The afternoon had thinned into a soft, slanted light. The beach grew quieter as people drifted away in small groups, the rhythm easing into something unclaimed. Artemis walked along the edge of the path, hands in her sleeves, mind busy with the familiar work of scanning and counting. She told herself she was just stretching her legs. A child struggled near the rocks, tugging at a plastic bucket wedged awkwardly between stones. The tide crept closer, nudging the waterline up with patient insistence. The child pulled harder, frustration tipping into panic. Artemis slowed. She didn't like children. Not really. They were unpredictable, loud, and demanded attention she didn't want to give. She took another step, meaning to pass by—and stopped. The resistance flared, then hesitated, uncertain what it was meant to protect against here. The bucket slipped free suddenly, sending the child backward. Artemis moved before she decided to. She caught the bucket with one hand and steadied the child with the other, quick and efficient. "Careful," she said, the word clipped. The child blinked, then nodded, clutching the bucket with renewed determination. A woman called out from a short distance away, relief obvious even before she reached them. "Thank you," the woman said, breathless. She didn't smile. She shifted her weight and continued watching the water. Artemis sat down beside her, jaw tight. The resistance inside her stirred, late and dissatisfied. It hadn't erased anger or clarified belief. It had just… bypassed it. Artemis clenched her hands, annoyed at herself for noticing the absence of argument where one usually lived. She didn't look at Leto. She didn't need to. The resistance was still there—coiled, alert. But for the first time, it hadn't been the thing that moved her. Artemis noticed it first while lying awake, the room washed in pale moonlight, the window cracked just enough to let the sound of waves drift in. Her body was tired in an honest way—muscles sore, eyelids heavy—but her mind stayed alert, turning over the day with the same precision she used to scan a crowd. She replayed the moment by the rocks. The child. The bucket. The way her body had moved before she decided to let it. No argument had preceded it. No justification followed. The resistance had arrived late, like a guard who'd missed the signal. She frowned into the dark. Men are not one thing. The sentence formed unbidden, clean and uncomfortable. She didn't like it. It felt unfinished, too broad to be useful, too narrow to be satisfying. She tried to sharpen it—some men are bad, many men are careless, systems fail women—phrases she could grip and wield. None of them fit the day she'd lived. In the morning, she watched people from the edge of the path. A man lifted a child onto his shoulders, laughing when the child squealed. Another argued with a woman over directions, irritation flaring and fading just as quickly. Someone else held a door without looking to see who followed. The actions overlapped, contradictory and ordinary. Artemis tracked them all, irritation simmering. She wanted a rule. A single line she could draw and defend. But the world kept offering her clusters instead—patterns with exceptions, behavior shaped by place and moment. It felt slippery, unreliable. It required attention instead of certainty. That cost more. Leto walked beside her in silence, letting the morning stretch. Artemis knew her mother noticed the change—the quieter steps, the longer pauses—but she also knew Leto wouldn't name it. Naming made things real. Real things demanded decisions. Artemis wasn't ready for that. They stopped near the water. The tide had come in farther overnight, smoothing away yesterday's lines in the sand. New ones formed as the waves pulled back, temporary and precise. The resistance inside Artemis shifted again, not tightening, not loosening. Reorienting. Men are not one thing didn't mean men are safe. It didn't mean nothing bad happens. It meant something far more irritating: that she would have to look each time. Decide each time. Stay present instead of hiding behind a single answer. She hated how much work that sounded like. Artemis turned away from the water and walked back toward the path without comment. The thought followed her, unwelcome but persistent, settling into the space where certainty used to sit. She didn't accept it. But she didn't reject it either. And that, she realized with a tight knot in her chest, might be the most dangerous place of all. The change showed itself in small refusals. Artemis noticed them as absences first—the moments where she didn't tense, didn't catalogue exits, didn't rehearse what she would do if something went wrong. They were brief, easy to miss, but once she saw them she couldn't stop seeing them. The resistance was still there, coiled and alert, yet it no longer filled the entire space inside her. It shared it now with something else: attention. She walked the path alone while Leto waited near the water. The distance wasn't defiance; it was permission. Artemis took it cautiously, aware of how easily freedom could turn into exposure. People passed—some close, some not—and she adjusted without bracing, stepping aside when needed, holding her ground when it felt right. No rules guided her. Only judgment. That unnerved her more than fear ever had. A man nearly collided with her at a bend, distracted by a phone. He startled, apologized quickly, and moved on. Artemis stood there a beat longer than necessary, irritation flickering and then dissolving. She hadn't been endangered. She hadn't been proven right or wrong. The moment didn't demand a conclusion. She exhaled and kept walking. The resistance inside her shifted again, responding to this new pattern. It wasn't a wall anymore. It was a tool—heavy, familiar, sometimes useful, sometimes not. Carrying it required choice. Setting it down, even briefly, felt like a risk she hadn't agreed to take. She returned to Leto's side without comment and sat, knees drawn up. The wind tugged at her sleeves. The water moved as it always did, indifferent to her reckoning. Leto glanced at her once, then back to the horizon. No questions. No praise. Artemis felt a flash of irritation at how visible her quiet had become—and then a strange gratitude for the space it was given. "I'm still not changing my mind," Artemis said, the words emerging as if to reassure herself. Leto nodded. "I know." The simplicity of the answer left no room to argue. Artemis frowned, then looked away. The part of her that had once defined herself by resistance alone felt unmoored. If she wasn't the girl who refused, who was she becoming? Not someone trusting. Not someone forgiving. Someone watching. Someone choosing when to step in and when to step back. Someone who would have to carry the weight of judgment instead of hiding behind certainty. That felt dangerous. It also felt real. Artemis tightened her arms around her knees, eyes fixed on the water. The resistance remained—shaped now, not blunted. It no longer defined her entire outline, but it was still something she claimed as hers. She wasn't ready to put it down. But she was beginning to understand that it wasn't the only thing she was carrying. They left at dawn. The sky was thin and colorless, the world not quite awake yet. Artemis stood near the edge of the overlook while Leto loaded the last bag into the car. The water below was calm, stretched flat and pale, as if it were holding its breath along with her. She tried to inventory what she was taking with her. Not memories—that felt too neat. Not lessons—no one had taught her anything she hadn't fought first. What remained sat heavier and less cooperative: the sense that the world refused to stay simple no matter how hard she pressed against it. Artemis crouched and dragged her fingers through the sand. It slipped away immediately, lines collapsing as soon as she drew them. She frowned and tried again, slower this time. The line held for a few seconds longer, then softened and disappeared. She didn't try a third time. Behind her, the car door closed with a quiet thud. Leto came to stand beside her, close enough that Artemis could feel her presence without being crowded. They looked out at the water together, neither speaking. Men walked along the far edge of the beach—small shapes at this distance, indistinct. Artemis noticed them, as she always did. The familiar readiness stirred, then settled. No surge. No flare. Just awareness, sharp but no longer screaming. The resistance was still there. She felt it clearly. It hadn't been dismantled or disproven. It hadn't apologized for itself. It remained part of her, shaped by what she'd endured and what she still refused to forget. But it wasn't alone anymore. Artemis straightened and brushed the sand from her hands. She glanced once more at the shoreline, at the place where the waves kept redrawing their edges without complaint. The image lodged somewhere deep, unwelcome and persistent. Leto touched her shoulder briefly. Not to guide. Not to comfort. Just to signal that it was time. Artemis nodded and walked back to the car. She climbed in without argument, without asking where they were going next. The road would decide that soon enough. As they pulled away, the coastline fell back and flattened into distance. Artemis watched it recede until it was nothing more than a pale line against the horizon. She wasn't calmer. She wasn't kinder. She wasn't convinced. But the certainty she'd once carried like a weapon no longer fit cleanly in her hands. It shifted when she tried to grip it too tightly, demanding attention instead of obedience. Artemis leaned her head against the window and closed her eyes, the hum of the road steady beneath her. She was still resistant. She just wasn't untouched anymore.
