A man stepped aside instinctively, giving her room without comment. Another passed close enough that she felt the brush of air, then was gone, attention already elsewhere. Nothing pressed. "This place is… quiet," Artemis said at last. Leto nodded. "It listens before it moves." That answer irritated her. It sounded like meaning where none was needed. Artemis stopped near a low wall, pretending to adjust her bag while she recalibrated. Quiet didn't mean safe. Order didn't mean kind. She knew this. She had learned it the hard way. And yet—her body hadn't followed the lesson. They crossed a small bridge, water sliding beneath them in smooth, uninterrupted lines. The city didn't demand reaction. It didn't challenge her vigilance or reward it. The lack of friction made her feel exposed, as if she were walking without armor and no one had noticed. She tried to summon anger—just a flicker, enough to ground herself. It arrived late, thin and unsatisfying. Artemis' jaw tightened. She picked up her pace, irritation rising at the delay. She didn't like having to wait for herself. Anger was supposed to be immediate, reliable. It had always been. Leto glanced at her, reading the tension without naming it. She didn't slow or speed up, simply matched Artemis' stride and kept moving. They passed a group of men speaking softly near a storefront. One looked up briefly, nodded in acknowledgment, and returned to the conversation. No assessment. No lingering interest. Artemis felt a pulse of something sharp and misplaced—anger searching for a target that refused to exist. She clenched her hands inside her sleeves and kept walking. Kyoto did not resist her. And for the first time since this change began, Artemis realized with a chill that she didn't know how to move through a world that wouldn't push back. The thought followed her down the street, quiet and persistent: If nothing challenges me… what am I supposed to be ready for? She didn't like the question. Not at all. Kyoto made its expectations felt without announcing them. Artemis noticed it in the way people paused before crossing paths, in the small shifts of posture that prevented collision before it became a possibility. No one raised their voice to claim space. No one needed to. The city moved on agreements that were never spoken aloud. She hated how quickly she began to notice the pattern. At a narrow street corner, she stopped short, deliberately slowing. A man approaching from the opposite direction adjusted instantly, stepping aside without irritation, without acknowledgement. Another followed, doing the same. The exchange was clean, efficient, over before it could be catalogued. Artemis' shoulders tensed. She hadn't moved to assert herself. She hadn't challenged them into giving way. It had simply… happened. That felt wrong. She walked on, eyes scanning for the familiar tells—carelessness, entitlement, the subtle pressure she'd learned to recognize before it surfaced. Instead, she found attention without intrusion. Awareness without interest. A shopkeeper spoke quietly to a customer, voice even. Two men laughed softly nearby, their conversation folding in on itself instead of spilling outward. The noise never rose high enough to demand response. Artemis felt the absence like a weight. "You're counting again," Leto said, not looking at her. Artemis stiffened. "I always count." "Yes," Leto replied. "But you're listening harder than you need to." That annoyed her more than if Leto had been wrong. Artemis stopped at the edge of the street, pretending to study a display while she sorted the feeling burning behind her eyes. "I don't trust this," she said finally. Leto joined her, gaze unfocused, as if she were watching the street rather than the people in it. "You don't have to." "That's not what I meant." Artemis' voice dropped. "It feels like I'm missing something." Leto considered her. "Or like nothing is demanding anything from you." Artemis scoffed. "That's worse." They walked again. The street narrowed further, the space compressing without becoming crowded. Artemis waited for irritation to spike, for her body to brace itself as it always had. It didn't. She felt herself adjusting instead—mirroring the pauses, timing her steps to avoid friction. The realization struck her hard enough to make her falter. She wasn't resisting. She was adapting. Artemis clenched her jaw and forced her pace forward, refusing the rhythm the city offered her. She brushed past a man intentionally, testing the boundary. He startled, apologized reflexively, and stepped aside. The interaction ended before it began. The anger she expected to feel didn't arrive. What came instead was a sharp, rising discomfort that had nothing to do with him. Artemis pressed her hands together, nails biting into her palms. The rules here weren't enforced. They were assumed. And without a rule to push against, she felt something unsteady open inside her—an unease she couldn't name, only recognize. Fear didn't arrive yet. But the ground beneath her certainty had started to tilt. The moment should have been simple. They were crossing a small courtyard near a temple, gravel crunching softly underfoot. A group approached from the opposite side—tourists, distracted, their formation loose and careless compared to the flow Artemis had been learning to read. One of the men drifted out of line, path intersecting hers at an angle that was too close, too unplanned. This was where it usually happened. The tightening. The flare. The clean, familiar surge that told her how to move. Artemis felt nothing. She slowed, confused, waiting for her body to catch up. The man looked up at the last second, startled, and adjusted sharply, murmuring an apology as he stepped back. The near-collision dissolved into the gravel without friction. Artemis stopped walking. The delay hit her a breath later—cold and unmistakable. It wasn't that anger hadn't come. It was that it had arrived after the moment had passed, thin and misplaced, like an echo that didn't belong to the sound that caused it. Her chest tightened, not with rage, but with something closer to alarm. She pressed her lips together and forced herself to keep moving, but the rhythm was gone. Each step felt slightly off, her timing no longer aligned with the space around her. "You froze," Leto said quietly, once they were clear of the path. "I didn't," Artemis replied too quickly. "You stopped." Artemis shook her head, irritation flaring at the wrong target. "I was thinking." "That's new," Leto said—not teasing, not accusing. Just observing. Artemis bristled. "I always think." "Yes," Leto agreed. "But you didn't react." That word landed hard. Artemis looked back toward the courtyard. People moved through it smoothly, the moment already forgotten. She tried to replay it in her mind, searching for the spark she'd missed, the instinct that should have guided her. It wouldn't come back. She felt exposed, like someone who'd reached for a weapon that wasn't there anymore. "I don't like this place," Artemis muttered. Leto studied her, then nodded once. "You don't like not knowing what comes next." Artemis didn't answer. The truth of it pressed too close to something she wasn't ready to touch. She set her jaw and walked faster, as if speed might force her instincts to reassert themselves. They passed under the shadow of a gate, the air cooler beneath it. Artemis deliberately stepped closer to the next group she saw, testing herself. The people adjusted automatically, their movements smooth, unbothered. Again—no reaction. Her breath came shallow now, irritation curling inward. The problem wasn't them. The problem was that her body no longer moved ahead of her mind. That delay—small, almost invisible—terrified her more than any confrontation ever had. Because hesitation wasn't something she knew how to fight. And she had no idea how many more moments like this it would take before the fear caught up to her completely. Artemis spent the rest of the afternoon watching herself. Not the city. Not the people. Herself. They walked along a narrow canal where the water moved slowly, reflecting the wooden facades in fractured lines. Tourists drifted in and out of the path, their movements loose, uncoordinated compared to the rhythm Artemis had begun to recognize. She tracked every approach, every angle, waiting for the reflex to return. It didn't. Instead, something else took its place—calculation. She adjusted her steps consciously, measured distance, anticipated movement the way one plans rather than reacts. It worked. Too well. The absence of friction made her stomach twist. This wasn't how it was supposed to feel. She slowed, then stopped, pretending to study the water. Her reflection wavered back at her, broken and reforming with each ripple. She frowned at it, unsettled by how unfamiliar her own stillness felt. "Are you tired?" Leto asked, stopping beside her. Artemis shook her head. "No." "That was quick." "I'm fine," she said, sharper than she meant to. Leto didn't press. She rested her arms on the low railing and watched the water instead. The silence stretched, unchallenging and persistent. Artemis turned away from the canal and walked ahead again, deliberately placing herself closer to passing bodies. She wanted the surge back—the clarity, the certainty that came from knowing exactly when to brace. A man passed too near. Artemis felt the brush of fabric. She waited for the jolt. Nothing. Her breath caught. She took another step, heart thudding now, not with anger but with something colder. She tried to force the feeling, dredging up memories that had once been enough to ignite it. The response came sluggish, dull and distant. She clenched her hands inside her sleeves, nails biting into her palms. This is wrong, she told herself. I don't lose this. I don't. Leto glanced back at her, concern flickering briefly before being masked. "You're pacing yourself," she said. "I don't need to," Artemis snapped. Leto raised an eyebrow. "You didn't used to." That stung more than accusation. Artemis opened her mouth to retort, then stopped. The words she wanted relied on a certainty she couldn't quite reach anymore. They continued walking, the canal giving way to a broader street. Artemis felt the weight of attention shift inward, her awareness folding back on itself. She was no longer preparing for others. She was preparing for her own hesitation. The realization made her throat tighten. If she couldn't trust her instincts, then every moment required decision. Judgment. Responsibility. There was no shield in that—only exposure. Artemis swallowed and kept walking, posture rigid, senses strained. The fear still hadn't fully surfaced. But she could feel it gathering, patient and inevitable, waiting for the moment she could no longer pretend this was temporary. The evening settled softly over Kyoto, lantern light spilling into the street in warm, deliberate pools. The shift from day to night felt rehearsed, as if the city had practiced it countless times and no longer needed to think about it. Artemis walked beside Leto, her steps slightly too quick, her shoulders held tight. She waited for Leto to say something. Usually, this was where it happened—guidance, correction, reassurance framed carefully so it didn't feel like instruction. Artemis had learned to brace for it, to reject it before it could take root. Nothing came. They passed a small restaurant, the murmur of voices low and contained inside. Men and women sat close together, their movements relaxed, unguarded. Artemis noticed herself noticing, irritation flickering at the familiarity of the scan. Still, no surge followed. She slowed, then stopped altogether. Leto took a few steps more before realizing Artemis wasn't beside her. She turned, studying her daughter with quiet attention. "Well?" Artemis asked, unable to keep the edge from her voice. Leto waited a beat. "What do you want me to say?" Artemis faltered. The question landed wrong, pulling the ground out from under the irritation she'd prepared. "That this is strange," she said. "That something's off." "It is," Leto replied evenly. "And?" "And you've noticed." Artemis stared at her. "That's it?" Leto nodded once. "That's enough for now." The answer left no room to argue against. Artemis turned away sharply and resumed walking, jaw clenched. The absence of correction felt heavier than disapproval ever had. Silence wasn't neutral—it demanded attention. It forced her to sit with the discomfort she'd hoped Leto would name and contain for her. They crossed another bridge, water dark beneath them now, reflecting fragments of light that refused to stay whole. Artemis leaned against the railing briefly, pressing her palms to the cool wood. "You're not helping," she muttered. Leto joined her, gaze on the water. "I'm not stopping you either." "That's not the same thing." "No," Leto agreed. "It isn't." Artemis exhaled sharply, frustration curling inward. She wanted Leto to tell her this was a phase, that her instincts would snap back into place if she waited long enough. She wanted proof that nothing fundamental had changed. Leto offered none. They stood there, the city breathing around them, unbothered by Artemis' quiet crisis. The world didn't slow to accommodate her uncertainty. It moved on, steady and precise. Artemis pushed off the railing and walked ahead again, heart beating faster now. Leto followed at an unhurried pace, close enough to be present, far enough to avoid crowding. The fear crept closer, no longer held at bay by irritation or denial. If Leto wasn't stepping in, then this wasn't something that could be corrected. It was something Artemis would have to face on her own. And that realization tightened her chest in a way no external threat ever had. Tokyo pressed in without touching her. The station pulsed with movement—streams of bodies flowing in practiced lanes, footsteps aligning and separating with near-mathematical precision. Artemis stood just inside the threshold, senses flaring out of habit, mapping the angles, counting exits. This was density. This was where vigilance was supposed to matter. Nothing snagged. People passed close enough for her to feel the warmth of their sleeves, the rush of air as they slipped by. No elbows. No stares that lingered too long. Adjustments happened before contact, a choreography so ingrained it barely registered as choice. Artemis took a step forward, then another. The crowd parted a fraction, then sealed behind her, leaving no trace she'd been there. Her pulse climbed anyway. She tried to provoke the reflex—slowing suddenly, shifting her path at the last second, moving against the flow. The response was immediate and impersonal: a half-step aside, a pivot of shoulders, a murmured apology from someone who hadn't actually done anything wrong. Still no surge. The noise swelled and fell around her, contained within invisible bounds. Announcements chimed overhead. Shoes scuffed. Laughter flickered and vanished. The city demanded attention without offering a target. Artemis' breathing went shallow. She focused on keeping pace, on not being swallowed by the rhythm she hadn't agreed to follow. Every successful adjustment felt like a small failure—proof that she could move through this without the tools she'd trusted. She reached the platform edge and stopped, heart hammering. The crowd flowed around her as if she were a stone in a stream, pressure diverting cleanly without complaint. "This should be chaos," she muttered. Leto stood beside her, steady, eyes forward. "It's controlled," she said. "There's a difference." Artemis clenched her jaw. Control was supposed to be hers. The train arrived in a smooth rush of air. Doors opened. People exited and entered with the same unspoken rules. Artemis stepped inside, forced by proximity more than choice. She stood, gripping the overhead strap, surrounded on all sides. Her body waited for the jolt. It didn't come. The absence roared louder than noise. Her muscles tensed anyway, readying for a threat that refused to appear. She felt ridiculous—overprepared in a world that didn't need her readiness. At the next stop, a man shifted to give her space without looking at her. Another adjusted his stance to avoid brushing her arm. The courtesy felt clinical, stripped of meaning. It didn't care who she was or what she believed. Artemis closed her eyes for a beat, fighting the tightness in her chest. If anger didn't rise here—packed in, pressed on all sides—then when would it? The thought landed heavy and unwelcome. Tokyo didn't challenge her. It rendered her vigilance unnecessary. And standing there, suspended between stops, Artemis felt the first true edge of fear cut through the noise—not fear of them, but fear of what it meant that she could be here, surrounded, and still not know what she was supposed to be ready for anymore. Artemis didn't trust the quiet inside her. They exited the station into a narrower street, the city compressing again into controlled lanes of movement. Neon signs buzzed softly overhead, their light reflecting off glass and pavement. Tokyo at night was louder than Kyoto, brighter, sharper—and still it refused to meet her where she stood. She slowed deliberately. Not out of caution, but calculation. She let herself drift closer to the edge of the sidewalk, nearer to passing bodies than she preferred. This was a test. A controlled one. She needed the reflex back—the spike, the clarity that told her exactly where she stood in relation to everyone else. A man brushed past, close enough that the fabric of his sleeve grazed her arm. Artemis waited. Nothing happened. Her chest tightened, irritation flaring too late, directionless. She tried again, angling herself into the path of oncoming pedestrians, forcing proximity. The crowd compensated instantly, paths shifting with mechanical ease. Apologies surfaced and vanished without weight. She stopped walking altogether, breath shallow now. The anger should have been there—sharp, grounding, familiar. She searched for it like one might search for a misplaced object, patting down memory and muscle alike. Come on, she thought, jaw clenched. Now. She replayed old moments in her mind, images that had once been enough to light the fuse. The response flickered weakly, a shadow of what it used to be. It felt staged, like repeating lines that no longer belonged to her. "This isn't working," she muttered. Leto glanced back, concern surfacing before she masked it. "What isn't?" Artemis shook her head sharply. "Nothing." But it wasn't nothing. The failure sat heavy in her chest, spreading cold. Anger wasn't just an emotion—it was orientation. It told her where the edges were. Without it, everything felt too open, too undefined. She pressed her hands together, nails biting into her palms, trying to force sensation through pain if nothing else. The city continued around her, indifferent to her internal struggle. Men passed, women passed, lives intersected and separated without pause. Her breath came faster now, the beginnings of panic edging in. She hated it immediately. Panic was useless. Panic meant losing control. "I should feel something," she said, the words slipping out before she could stop them. Leto slowed, studying her. "You are," she said carefully. "That's not what I mean." Artemis' voice dropped. "I need the other thing." Leto didn't answer. She didn't have to. The absence of reassurance made the truth settle harder. Artemis turned away and resumed walking, pace uneven. The effort of summoning anger exhausted her more than the anger ever had. Each failed attempt deepened the unease curling in her stomach. She wasn't numb. She was misaligned. And the fear that followed wasn't sudden or sharp—it was slow, creeping, and absolute. If she couldn't call the anger back when she needed it, then she didn't just risk being wrong. She risked being undefended. That thought stayed with her long after the lights blurred into distance, heavy enough to make her shoulders ache. They stopped at a quiet side street where the noise dropped away all at once, as if the city had folded in on itself. The sudden stillness made Artemis' skin prickle. She leaned against a vending machine, the low hum vibrating faintly through her shoulder, grounding and insufficient at the same time. She closed her eyes and tried again. Not theatrically. Not angrily. Carefully. She searched for the familiar sequence—the tightening first, then the surge, the clean alignment of body and intent. The thing that had always come when she needed it, no matter where she was or who stood in front of her. It didn't respond. The absence felt deliberate now, like something withholding itself. Her stomach twisted. This was no longer irritation or discomfort. This was fear, finally arriving in full, cold clarity. Fear not of the men passing at the end of the street, not of the city pressing in—but of the quiet inside her where certainty used to live. She opened her eyes and watched a group walk past the corner. Their footsteps faded. Nothing followed. "I used to know," she said quietly. Leto turned toward her, expression open, careful. "Know what?" "When to be ready," Artemis replied. The admission felt like pulling something loose. "It was instant. I didn't have to think." Leto nodded once. She didn't soften the moment by reframing it. "And now?" Artemis swallowed. "Now I have to check." The words landed harder than she expected. Checking meant doubt. Doubt meant delay. Delay meant exposure. Her breath shortened again, chest tight, the edges of panic brushing closer. She pushed off the machine and took a few steps, then stopped, suddenly unsure where she was going. The city around her felt unchanged—orderly, predictable, indifferent. The danger wasn't out there. It was the gap between impulse and action. She wrapped her arms around herself, nails pressing through fabric into skin. The pain helped, but only a little. Fear curled tighter, whispering questions she didn't want to hear. What if this doesn't come back? What if this is permanent? What if I need it and it isn't there? Leto watched her without stepping closer, giving Artemis the space to spiral without interruption. That restraint hurt almost as much as interference would have. "I don't trust this," Artemis said again, but the words sounded thinner now. Leto answered softly, "You don't trust yourself." The truth of it knocked the air from her lungs. Artemis turned away sharply, jaw clenched, eyes burning with something dangerously close to tears. She hated that the fear had found a shape. She hated that it had a name. Because now that she could see it clearly, she couldn't pretend it belonged to the world anymore. It belonged to her. And standing there in the narrow Tokyo street, with the city moving calmly around her, Artemis felt the terrifying realization settle in: She had built herself around a certainty that no longer obeyed her. And she didn't know who she was supposed to be without it. Artemis compensated. If the reflex wouldn't answer, then she would replace it with attention—constant, exhausting attention. She straightened, forced her shoulders back, and began to watch harder than she ever had before. Every step was measured. Every face catalogued. Every sound evaluated for meaning it might not have. The city didn't reward the effort. They walked through another stretch of Tokyo streets, busier now, the night deepening. People flowed around them with the same effortless order, their paths intersecting and separating without friction. Artemis tracked it all, her mind running faster than her body, searching for patterns she could control. Her head began to ache. She adjusted her pace again, staying just behind Leto, then drifting ahead, then falling back. None of it settled the tightness in her chest. The vigilance felt hollow, like gripping a blade without an edge. "You're hovering," Leto said quietly, not accusing. Artemis bristled. "I'm paying attention." "Yes," Leto replied. "Too much." Artemis scoffed and moved ahead again, irritation flaring briefly before dissolving into fatigue. She hated the weakness in that—the way anger refused to sustain itself, leaving only effort behind. At a crosswalk, she stopped short, heart racing for no reason she could point to. The light changed. People moved. She moved with them, half a step late. That delay echoed louder now. Her breath shortened. The world felt sharper at the edges, like everything might cut her if she misjudged the angle. She clenched her hands, nails biting into skin again, grounding herself through pain because nothing else worked. This was supposed to make her safer. It didn't. The more she monitored herself, the less room there was to breathe. Each decision demanded justification. Each moment required calculation. The freedom that anger had once given her—clean, immediate, unquestioned—was gone. She slowed again, dizziness flickering at the edges of her vision. Leto noticed immediately and stopped, turning toward her fully this time. "Sit," she said, firm but calm. Artemis hesitated, then obeyed, lowering herself onto a low concrete barrier. The city continued around them, unbothered by her sudden stillness. A man glanced at her, concerned, then looked away when Leto nodded slightly. The exchange was small. Unremarkable. It shouldn't have mattered. It did. Artemis stared at the ground, chest heaving now, the fear pressing in hard enough to make her hands tremble. Overcorrection hadn't restored control. It had stripped it further. "I can't keep doing this," she muttered, the admission barely audible. Leto crouched in front of her, not touching, just present. "Then don't," she said. Artemis shook her head sharply. "I don't know how to stop." The truth settled heavy and inescapable. Without her instinct to rely on, vigilance became a cage of her own making. The more she tried to hold herself together, the more brittle she felt—like one wrong movement would shatter whatever fragile balance she'd forced into place. Fear had fully arrived now, no longer creeping or distant. It sat in her chest, solid and undeniable. And for the first time, Artemis understood that fighting it the way she always had was only making it worse. They didn't go far after that. The street narrowed into a quieter lane, storefronts shuttered, light thinning into pale reflections on wet pavement. Artemis walked beside Leto now, close enough that their steps almost aligned. She hated the proximity and needed it at the same time. Her hands still trembled. She tried to steady her breathing, counting in her head the way she always had when control threatened to slip. The numbers tangled, losing their rhythm halfway through. Her chest felt too tight, like the air had grown heavier without warning. "This is stupid," she said, the words brittle. "Nothing's happening." Leto glanced at her. "Something is." Artemis shook her head hard, as if she could dislodge the feeling. "I'm not in danger." "I know." "That's the problem." The admission came sharper than she meant. "I don't know what to do when I'm not." They stopped beneath an awning as a light drizzle began to fall. The sound softened the city, muting footsteps and distant voices. Artemis leaned against the wall, suddenly exhausted, every muscle taut with effort. Fear pressed in fully now—not sharp, not panicked, but dense and suffocating. It didn't give her a direction to move or a target to fixate on. It simply existed, heavy and undeniable. She closed her eyes and tried one last time to summon anger. She reached for it deliberately, the way she would reach for a weapon she'd carried too long to imagine being without. Nothing answered. Her throat tightened. For a moment, she thought she might cry, and the possibility terrified her more than the fear itself. She swallowed hard and forced her eyes open, staring at the rain-darkened street. "I don't trust this," she whispered. "I don't trust me." Leto stood close, not touching. "You don't have to decide anything right now." "That's easy for you to say." Artemis' voice wavered despite her effort to keep it steady. "You still know who you are." Leto didn't argue. She simply said, "I know who you are." That wasn't comforting. It was pressure. Artemis pushed off the wall and took a step forward, then another, testing her balance. The fear didn't recede. It followed, constant and patient. She realized with a sick twist that this was the peak—there was no surge left to come, no escalation waiting to break it open. This was it. She was standing in the middle of a city that refused to threaten her, inside a body that refused to react the way it used to. The old equation—danger equals anger, anger equals safety—no longer solved anything. Her certainty had failed her. The fear wasn't teaching her a lesson. It wasn't offering clarity. It was simply showing her the cost of not knowing who she would be when the anger didn't arrive. And as the rain deepened and the city moved on around them, Artemis stood there, breathing shallow and uneven, understanding one thing with terrible clarity: Whatever came next, it wouldn't be protected by the person she used to be. The fear didn't follow them onto the train the way Artemis expected it to. She sat by the window, forehead resting lightly against the glass, watching the city blur into bands of color as they moved. Her body felt wrung out—muscles sore, joints heavy, the kind of fatigue that came from holding tension too long. The panic had burned itself down to embers, leaving behind a dull ache that was harder to name. This was worse in its own way. Fear, at least, had direction. It demanded response. What remained now felt empty, undefined, like the space left behind after something essential had been torn out. Leto sat across from her, eyes on the darkened reflection in the window rather than on Artemis herself. She didn't ask questions. She didn't fill the quiet. The restraint felt deliberate, respectful—and infuriating. Artemis flexed her fingers, then stilled them when she realized she was checking for tremors that weren't there anymore. Her hands were steady. Her breathing had evened out. She should have felt relieved. Instead, she felt hollow. The train slowed at a station. People stepped on and off, the same practiced flow she'd come to expect. Artemis watched it distantly, no longer trying to test herself against it. The effort required to care felt too high. She let the world pass without engaging, and the world let her. That mutual disengagement frightened her more than the fear had. "What if it doesn't come back?" she asked suddenly, voice low. Leto didn't pretend not to hear. "What?" "The thing," Artemis said. "The way I used to be." Leto considered her for a long moment. "Then you'll be something else." The answer landed without force. No reassurance. No warning. Just fact. Artemis turned back to the window, jaw tightening. Something else sounded too close to disappearance. She wanted to argue, to insist that there were things you didn't replace, only lost. But the argument felt heavy, effortful. She didn't have the energy to hold it together. The train emerged briefly above ground, the city opening into a stretch of dark sky and distant lights. Artemis watched it with the strange detachment of someone observing a place she might never return to—not because she couldn't, but because she wouldn't be the same person when she did. Her thoughts drifted, unanchored. The fear no longer pressed at her chest, but it hadn't left either. It had settled somewhere deeper, quieter, waiting. This wasn't resolution. It was aftermath. Artemis leaned back in her seat and closed her eyes, letting the movement carry her without resistance. For the first time since arriving in Japan, she didn't scan the space around her. She scanned herself instead. And in that inward turn, something shifted—not toward peace, but toward the uneasy awareness that fear wasn't the end of this change. It was only the beginning of having to understand what came after. Time blurred between places. Airports, gates, the dull choreography of travel folded over Artemis without leaving impressions sharp enough to hold. She moved when prompted, followed signs, handed over documents, reclaimed her bag on the other side. The motions required attention, but not judgment. That, more than anything, let her breathe. The fear stayed quiet. Not gone. Quiet. She noticed it in the way her thoughts circled without landing, in the way she watched people pass without attaching meaning to them. Men, women, families, individuals—faces flickered by, distinct and then gone. Artemis didn't test herself against them anymore. She didn't try to summon anything at all. The effort felt unnecessary. And that scared her in a different way. Leto walked beside her through the terminal, close enough to be present, far enough not to intrude. They spoke when required—logistics, directions, nothing that reached inward. The normalcy of it pressed gently, insistently, against Artemis' awareness. She realized, dimly, that she wasn't bracing. That should have felt like progress. It didn't. It felt like standing in unfamiliar shoes—functional, but not yet hers. On the plane, Artemis took the window seat again. The ground fell away beneath them, lights thinning into patterns that meant nothing from this height. She rested her head back and watched the wing cut through cloud, steady and unbothered. Fear had peaked in Japan. She knew that now. What remained wasn't panic or dread, but a low, persistent unease that hummed beneath everything else. The question wouldn't leave her alone. If I don't react the way I used to… what am I reacting as? She didn't have an answer. She didn't try to invent one. The absence of anger made even speculation feel exposed, like guessing without armor. Leto shifted in her seat beside her. Artemis glanced over, then away again. There was something she could ask—something about change, about whether this was temporary—but the thought of hearing an answer made her chest tighten. Answers implied direction. She wasn't ready for that. They landed in the early hours, the air different the moment they stepped outside. Softer. Familiar in ways Artemis hadn't anticipated. The smell of the sea carried on the breeze, subtle and unmistakable. Greece. The word settled in her chest with unexpected weight. This wasn't relief either. Familiarity didn't soothe the unease—it sharpened it. Whatever had shifted in Japan hadn't stayed behind. It had traveled with her, quiet and intact, crossing borders as easily as she had. As they walked toward the waiting car, Artemis felt the distance from Tokyo close behind her, not as memory but as residue. Fear had cooled, yes. But it hadn't resolved. It had simply made space. And standing there on familiar ground, Artemis realized that whatever understanding waited for her next would not come from escape. It would come from facing what this quiet had replaced. Greece felt wrong in a way Japan never had. The road curved along the coast, the sea flashing between breaks in stone walls and low trees. Artemis knew this light—the way it settled, the way it made everything look sharper than it was. She'd grown up inside it. It should have fit like memory fits. Instead, it pressed against her skin, too intimate, too aware. She stepped out of the car and stood still, listening. The sounds were familiar: waves against rock, distant voices carrying without effort, the hum of a place that didn't hurry. Men moved through the street with the ease of people who belonged to it. Artemis noticed them automatically—and then noticed that her body didn't brace. That absence felt louder here. In Japan, the quiet had been foreign. Here, it was personal. She walked a few steps ahead of Leto, then slowed, unsettled by the way her feet knew where to go without instruction. This path. That turn. The small dip in the stone she'd once tripped over and learned to avoid. Her past moved beneath her without asking permission. "I remember this," she said, half to herself. Leto nodded. "You would." Artemis frowned. "I don't feel like I should." They reached the edge of the overlook, the sea opening wide and blue below them. Artemis rested her hands on the stone railing, staring out at the water. This place had once made her feel anchored—grounded in who she was, what she stood for. Now it felt like a mirror she didn't recognize. A man passed behind them, greeting Leto with a casual nod. Artemis tracked the movement, waited for the familiar tightening. Nothing. Her fingers curled around the stone. The lack of reaction made her feel careless, as if she were neglecting something essential. She replayed old moments in her mind—times when this place had sharpened her resolve instead of dulling it. "You're quiet," Leto said. Artemis didn't answer right away. "It's easier here," she said finally. "That's the problem." Leto studied her, then looked back toward the sea. "Ease doesn't always mean safety." "I know," Artemis replied, sharper than she intended. "I used to know that." The words hung between them, heavier than accusation. Artemis straightened and stepped back from the railing, unsettled by how exposed she felt standing in a place that knew her so well. Japan had taken something from her without asking. Greece was showing her what it was. And that recognition cut deeper than fear ever had. Because whatever had changed wasn't tied to location or culture or distance. It was tied to her. Standing on familiar ground, Artemis felt the first clear edge of understanding begin to form—not as comfort or clarity, but as an unwelcome truth: She could no longer hide behind the world to explain herself. The silence that followed wasn't peaceful. It was expectant. And she had the uneasy sense that Greece would not let her leave until she faced what she'd brought back with her. Artemis walked the path she used to take without thinking. The stones were worn smooth in places, edges softened by years of footsteps and weather. She remembered running here once—barefoot, reckless, certain of her balance. Now she moved more carefully, watching her footing, aware of how easily a misstep could send her skidding toward the drop. That awareness felt new. And wrong. She stopped where the path narrowed, the sea visible between the rocks below. This had been a place of certainty once. A place where she'd stood tall, shoulders squared, convinced that vigilance alone could hold the world at bay. She remembered the weight of that confidence—how clean it had felt, how absolute. She tried to summon it now. The memory responded, sharp and vivid, but the feeling didn't follow. The certainty stayed lodged in the past, refusing to bridge the gap. Artemis exhaled slowly, frustration stirring. "I used to know how to stand here." Leto paused a few steps behind her. "You still do." "That's not what I mean." Artemis glanced back, irritation flickering. "I didn't have to think about it." Leto nodded once. "You trusted yourself." The words landed heavier than expected. Artemis turned back toward the sea, jaw tightening. Trust implied reliability. Reliability implied something she could count on. She wasn't sure she had that anymore. A pair of men passed farther down the path, voices low, their presence unremarkable. Artemis tracked them automatically, then caught herself doing it. The habit felt exposed here, too visible against the backdrop of memory. She hadn't always watched like this. Once, she'd simply been. "That girl wouldn't hesitate," Artemis said quietly. Leto joined her at the edge of the path, gaze following the line of the coast. "That girl didn't have your experiences." "That girl had conviction," Artemis shot back. "And certainty," Leto replied. "They're not the same thing." Artemis stiffened, the distinction scraping against something tender. She turned away from the sea and continued along the path, steps quickening, as if motion might outrun the thought. But Greece didn't allow distance. Every turn carried another memory—another version of herself she could no longer fully inhabit. The contrast pressed in, tightening her chest. This place hadn't changed. She had. And the understanding that had begun to form earlier sharpened here, cutting through denial with uncomfortable precision: what she'd lost wasn't safety or vigilance. It was the simplicity of believing that her reactions alone defined who she was. The path widened again ahead, the tension easing slightly, but Artemis didn't feel relieved. Recognition without comfort settled into her bones, heavy and unavoidable. She kept walking, knowing now that Greece wasn't offering answers. It was asking her to measure the distance between who she had been and who she was becoming—and to sit with the difference, no matter how much she disliked it. The town center hadn't changed in any way that mattered. The same narrow streets curved inward toward the square. The same café stood at the corner, chairs stacked unevenly outside, their metal legs scraping softly as someone rearranged them. Artemis recognized the rhythm of the place immediately—the way voices carried, the way people paused mid-conversation to greet one another without breaking stride. She remembered moving through this space with certainty. Now, she moved like a guest. A group passed close enough for her to hear fragments of conversation. A man laughed, sharp and sudden, then softened it into something quieter. Another adjusted his path to avoid colliding with her, nodding briefly in apology. Artemis noticed each action automatically—and then noticed that her response was no longer immediate. There it was again. The pause. She stopped walking. Leto continued a few steps before realizing Artemis wasn't beside her. She turned, waiting without comment. "I see it," Artemis said finally. Leto tilted her head slightly. "See what?" "The difference." Artemis' voice was steady, but her hands curled into fists at her sides. "I'm not reacting. I'm deciding." The word tasted strange. Deciding implied responsibility. It meant there was no instinct to hide behind, no automatic shield to raise when the world brushed too close. Leto said nothing, and Artemis hated how much that space made room for the thought to expand. She resumed walking, slower now. The realization followed her through the square, heavy and unrelenting. In Japan, the absence of reaction had felt like failure. Here, surrounded by familiarity, it felt like exposure. She wasn't safer. She was more visible to herself. A man carrying groceries nearly bumped into her at a corner. Artemis stepped aside without thinking, then froze. The movement hadn't come from vigilance or anger. It had come from judgment—fast, quiet, unannounced. She stared after him, pulse quickening. "That wasn't instinct," she said. Leto joined her again, gaze soft but attentive. "No." Artemis swallowed. "It was me." The understanding settled then—not as comfort, not as clarity, but as something unavoidable. Her fear in Japan hadn't been about men or crowds or space. It had been about this. If her reactions were no longer automatic, then every moment required choice. And choice meant accountability. It meant that when she stepped back or forward, it was because she had decided to—not because something inside her had done it for her. She hated how adult that sounded. "I don't like it," Artemis admitted. Leto nodded once. "You don't have to." That wasn't reassurance. It was permission to dislike the truth without rejecting it. They reached the edge of the square and stopped. Artemis looked around, the town moving on as it always had, indifferent to her internal reckoning. The understanding didn't make her feel wiser. It made her feel unarmored. And standing there in a place that remembered who she used to be, Artemis realized something she couldn't undo: Her fear hadn't come from losing protection. It had come from realizing that protection had quietly become responsibility. The street they walked down next was quieter than the square, lined with low houses and uneven stone walls. Artemis remembered it from years ago—not for anything important, just because it had always been there. A place you passed through without thinking. Now she thought about every step. The understanding she'd reached in the square didn't settle into her comfortably. It followed her instead, close and insistent, changing the weight of ordinary things. She noticed how she adjusted her pace when someone approached, how she chose when to look up and when to look away. Each movement felt deliberate in a way that left no room to hide. She missed the certainty of reaction. A man stepped out of a doorway ahead of them, carrying a crate balanced awkwardly against his hip. Artemis registered the situation instantly—distance, trajectory, timing. She slowed, then stepped aside without pause. The man nodded in thanks and continued on. The exchange was smooth. Efficient. It also left her unsettled. She hadn't reacted out of anger or instinct. She had assessed, chosen, acted. The responsibility of it pressed against her chest like a weight she hadn't agreed to carry. "I don't like having to think this much," she said quietly. Leto glanced at her, then back to the street. "You don't have to like it." "That doesn't make it easier." "No," Leto agreed. "It just makes it honest." Artemis frowned, irritation flaring briefly before ebbing again. Even irritation felt harder to sustain now, like a spark that couldn't find dry ground. She kicked lightly at a loose stone and watched it skitter ahead of them, then disappear into the gutter. They continued walking. The houses grew closer together, voices drifting from open windows—domestic, unguarded sounds. Artemis felt the urge to brace and resisted it, not because she felt safe, but because she knew now that bracing was a choice too. That realization didn't calm her. It made her hyperaware. She slowed again, breathing carefully, the effort of constant decision wearing at her nerves. This was the cost she hadn't anticipated. Understanding hadn't simplified anything. It had complicated everything. "You're not doing it wrong," Leto said, sensing the tension without looking at her. Artemis shot her a glance. "That's not reassuring." Leto nodded faintly. "I know." They reached the end of the street and paused. Artemis leaned against the stone wall, eyes closed for a moment, gathering herself. The understanding was still there, solid and unmoving. She could act without anger. She could decide instead of react. And she hated how exposed that made her feel. When she straightened again, the tension hadn't vanished. It had settled into something more manageable—resentful acceptance rather than resistance. She wasn't at peace with it. But she was carrying it. And as they turned onto the next street, Artemis understood one more quiet, unsettling truth: This wasn't a phase she could wait out. This was a way of moving through the world she would have to learn how to survive. The harbor smelled like salt and rust, familiar in a way that tugged at old memories Artemis didn't fully want. Boats rocked gently against their moorings, ropes creaking in a slow, uneven rhythm. She remembered coming here when she was younger—how the openness of the water had once made her feel sharp and awake, ready for anything that approached from any direction. Now it only made her feel watched by her own thoughts. They stopped near the edge of the pier. Men worked along the boats, movements practiced and unremarkable—hauling nets, checking lines, arguing quietly over something technical and unimportant. Artemis observed them automatically, cataloguing distance and posture, the way she always had. The difference was what followed. Nothing hardened inside her. She waited for it, the familiar tightening that used to turn observation into armor. It didn't arrive. The choice not to harden felt conscious now, deliberate in a way that made her skin prickle. She could still do it—she knew that—but she didn't. And that unsettled her more than if she had. A man passed close behind them, carrying a coil of rope. Artemis sensed him, adjusted her stance without thinking, and let him pass. The movement was clean, efficient. It didn't cost her anything. It also didn't give her the sense of control she used to crave. She frowned at the water, watching the distorted reflection of herself ripple and reform. "I could still do it," she said suddenly. Leto looked at her. "Do what?" "Harden," Artemis replied. "I know how." Leto nodded slowly. "Yes. You do." Artemis' jaw tightened. "Then why don't I want to?" The question hung there, unanswered. Leto didn't rush to fill the silence. She watched the harbor instead, letting the sounds of work and water carry on without commentary. Artemis crossed her arms, irritation flickering—not at the men nearby, but at the absence of something she'd relied on for so long. Choosing not to harden felt like leaving a door unlocked, even if nothing was trying to come through it. She hated how exposed that felt. A shout rose briefly from one of the boats, sharp but not angry, then softened into laughter. The moment passed. Artemis noticed it, felt the reflex stir, and consciously let it settle again. The effort surprised her. Not hardening took work now. She shifted her weight, restless. "This isn't safer." "No," Leto said. "It's different." Artemis huffed softly, dissatisfied. Different didn't come with rules. Different didn't promise protection. It just asked her to remain present, moment by moment, without the comfort of automatic defenses. She stared out at the open water, tension coiled but contained. Understanding hadn't made her softer. It had made her selective. And standing there at the edge of the harbor, Artemis realized that the question she was asking herself now wasn't whether men could be trusted. It was whether she could trust herself not to harden every time the world brushed against her. She didn't know the answer. But she knew she was choosing, again and again, not to default to the armor she'd worn for so long. That choice didn't feel like peace. It felt like standing still while everything else kept moving. The market along the harbor had grown busier by midday. Stalls crowded the narrow stretch of stone, canvas awnings flapping softly in the breeze. Voices overlapped in familiar rhythms—vendors calling out prices, customers negotiating without urgency, laughter surfacing and fading as easily as the tide. Artemis remembered this place too. It had always felt alive without being chaotic. Today, it felt demanding. She moved through the space slowly, aware of every adjustment she made. Each step was a choice now. Each pause carried intent. The understanding she'd reached followed close behind her, shaping how she navigated the press of bodies. A man stepped backward suddenly from a stall, arms full, not looking where he was going. Artemis noticed in time and shifted aside, quick and clean. The movement cost her nothing physically. Emotionally, it did. The familiar surge never came. No flare of anger, no tightening that told her she'd been wronged by proximity alone. Instead, there was a thin, unsettling awareness of herself—of how easily she'd handled it without the defenses she'd once relied on. She felt… exposed. Another body brushed past her shoulder, this time unavoidable. Artemis stiffened reflexively, the urge to harden flashing bright and sharp. She held it back, jaw clenched, letting the contact pass without reaction. Her chest tightened—not with fear, but with effort. This was the cost she hadn't expected. Staying open required work. It demanded attention, restraint, and judgment all at once. Anger had been simpler. It had drawn clear lines and kept them drawn. Now the lines blurred unless she redrew them herself. "Watch it," someone snapped nearby—not at her, but at a vendor who'd knocked over a crate. The moment flared and fizzled out as quickly as it appeared. Artemis tracked it, waiting for the familiar pull to align herself against it. It didn't come. Instead, she felt the weight of standing in the middle of things without retreating into armor. The world brushed against her again and again, and each time she chose how much to let in. By the time they reached the far end of the market, her shoulders ached with tension. She leaned against a low wall, breathing carefully, the noise washing over her in waves. "This is harder," she admitted quietly. Leto stood beside her, gaze sweeping the crowd. "Yes." Artemis frowned. "You're not going to tell me it gets easier." Leto shook her head. "Not today." That honesty grounded her more than reassurance would have. Artemis closed her eyes for a moment, letting the sounds settle. The understanding she'd reached hadn't made her safer or calmer. It had made her responsible. When she straightened again, the fatigue remained—but so did the choice. She stepped back into the flow of the market without hardening, without retreating. It wasn't victory. It was endurance. And for now, that was enough to carry her forward. The path along the cliffs rose gently away from the harbor, the noise thinning with each step. The sea stretched wide and patient below, waves breaking in slow repetition against the rocks. Artemis welcomed the space—not because it felt safe, but because it allowed her to breathe without negotiating every movement. She walked ahead of Leto again, not out of defiance, but because she needed to feel where her own edges were now. The understanding she carried from the market hadn't faded. It had settled, heavy and persistent. Staying open took effort. Hardening took effort too. The difference was that now she had to decide which effort she was willing to make. A man approached from the opposite direction, his stride uneven as he adjusted the strap of a bag slung over his shoulder. Artemis noticed him early, tracked his line, calculated distance. This time, she didn't step aside immediately. She waited. Not to challenge him. To feel the moment stretch just long enough to matter. He noticed her at the same time, slowed, and shifted to give her space. No apology. No tension. Just an unspoken agreement reached without words. They passed each other cleanly, the space between them intact. Artemis felt the difference immediately. She hadn't hardened. She hadn't yielded reflexively. She had chosen her ground. The realization steadied her more than anything had all day. She stopped a few steps farther along the path and looked out over the water, heart beating evenly for the first time since the market. The choice hadn't cost her as much this time. It still required attention, but it didn't drain her in the same way. Leto came to stand beside her, following her gaze. "You found your balance," she said. Artemis shook her head. "I found a moment." Leto smiled faintly at that. They stood in silence, the wind tugging at their clothes, the sea indifferent below. Artemis replayed the exchange in her mind—not with judgment, but with curiosity. She hadn't needed anger to hold her space. She hadn't needed to withdraw either. She'd stayed present. The understanding sharpened, not into certainty, but into something usable. Maybe this was what it meant to live without armor—not unprotected, but attentive. Not hardened, but not yielding blindly either. "I still don't like it," Artemis said. "I wouldn't expect you to," Leto replied. Artemis glanced at her, then back at the horizon. The world stretched out in front of her, unchanged and complicated. Men would still exist in it. So would moments that demanded choice. She didn't feel prepared for all of it. But standing there on the cliffside path, Artemis recognized that her fear had shifted shape. It was no longer about losing herself. It was about learning where she was willing to stand. That wasn't resolution. But it was direction. The afternoon drifted on without announcing itself. They moved back toward the town along streets Artemis knew by heart—corners she could turn without looking, steps she could count without thinking. Familiarity used to make her sharper. Now it tested whether the understanding she carried could survive outside moments that felt staged by necessity. She watched herself closely. A cart rattled past, too close, and she stepped aside without stiffening. A voice rose briefly behind her—irritation flaring over nothing—and she glanced back, assessed, and moved on. Each response arrived without ceremony, the decision forming and completing itself before she could second-guess it. That unsettled her. The absence of struggle made it feel less earned, as if she were cheating something by not paying the emotional cost she'd grown used to. Anger had always demanded energy; this didn't. It simply worked. She stopped near a low wall and leaned back, eyes following the line of the street. "I didn't think about it that time." Leto slowed, then nodded. "No. You didn't." Artemis frowned. "That doesn't mean it's gone." "No," Leto agreed. "It means you're practicing." Artemis pushed off the wall and resumed walking, irritation flickering briefly at the word. Practice implied repetition. Repetition implied habit. Habit implied permanence—and that thought tightened her chest. She didn't want this to become automatic. She didn't want to replace one reflex with another and pretend it was choice. A group passed them, laughing loudly, energy spilling outward in a way that would have once sharpened her focus. She noticed the rise, felt the old reflex stir faintly, and let it pass without gripping it. That, too, felt like a choice. The realization followed her down the street, heavier than the fear had been earlier. If this became habit, then she wouldn't get to decide each time. The burden would lighten—but so would the awareness. She wasn't sure she wanted that. "I don't want to forget," Artemis said suddenly. Leto looked at her. "Forget what?" "Why I learned to harden in the first place." Artemis' voice stayed steady, but her hands curled into fists. "I don't want this to turn into pretending it never mattered." Leto didn't argue. "It won't," she said simply. "Not if you remember." Artemis breathed out slowly, the tension easing just enough to let the words settle. Remembering wasn't the same as reacting. It didn't require anger. It required attention. They reached the end of the street where it opened back toward the sea. Artemis paused, taking in the view—the water catching late light, the town humming behind them. The understanding she carried hadn't resolved anything. It hadn't healed her or softened her edges. But it had begun to repeat itself. Not as instinct. As habit. And that realization, unsettling as it was, told her something important: this way of moving through the world wasn't temporary. It was becoming something she would have to choose whether to keep. The evening cooled the town without quieting it. Lights flickered on in windows one by one, soft yellow spilling onto the street. The air smelled faintly of food and salt, familiar enough to be almost comforting. Artemis noticed the familiarity and felt the old reflex twitch—memory trying to assert itself as authority. She didn't let it. They walked without destination, following streets that bent and doubled back on themselves. Artemis let Leto set the pace this time, not because she needed guidance, but because she wanted to feel what it was like not to lead with tension. It wasn't easier. Her awareness stayed sharp, but it no longer leapt at every movement. She tracked people as they passed—men, women, couples, groups—but the tracking ended where it should, not spiraling into preparation. Each moment resolved itself without demanding escalation. That restraint felt deliberate now. Chosen. She hated how normal it was becoming. A man stepped out of a doorway ahead of them, startled when he nearly collided with Artemis. He apologized quickly, embarrassed, and moved on. Artemis paused, the old anger flickering faintly before fading again. She stood there, unsettled by how little the moment asked of her. She resumed walking without comment. Leto glanced at her, reading the tension. "You're holding it carefully," she said. "I don't trust it," Artemis replied. "That's fair." Artemis frowned. "You're not going to tell me it's good." Leto shook her head. "No. I'm going to tell you it's yours." The words settled heavy in Artemis' chest. Yours meant responsibility. It meant that whatever shape this understanding took next, it would belong to her—not to circumstance, not to location, not to someone else's behavior. They reached a small overlook where the street dipped away toward the sea. Artemis stopped, resting her hands on the stone barrier, breathing in slowly. The water below reflected the last of the light, calm and indifferent. "This isn't finished," Artemis said quietly. Leto stood beside her, gaze steady. "No." "And it's not going back." "No," Leto agreed again. Artemis exhaled, irritation flaring and fading in the same breath. The certainty she'd once relied on was gone. In its place was something narrower, more precise, and far more demanding. She straightened and stepped back from the overlook, the understanding settling into her bones not as peace, but as direction. She would have to carry this forward—into new places, new encounters, new versions of herself. Not because it was better. But because it was real. As they turned back toward the town, Artemis didn't brace herself the way she once would have. She didn't relax either. She walked alert, deliberate, aware. The questioning hadn't ended. It had simply learned how to move with her. Night settled fully by the time they reached the older part of town. The streets narrowed, stone pressing close on either side, the lamps fewer and farther between. Artemis recognized this stretch immediately. It was the kind of place she would once have sharpened herself for without thinking—muscle memory snapping into place before the mind caught up. She waited for that snap. It didn't come. Instead, she felt the understanding settle again, firm and unwelcome: nothing was going to move for her unless she chose how to move first. The absence of reflex didn't make the street safer. It made her more responsible for how she occupied it. A pair of men approached from the far end, their voices low, indistinct. Artemis tracked them automatically, noting their pace, the way they walked side by side without urgency. The old armor stirred faintly, a suggestion rather than a command. She didn't reach for it. She shifted her path instead, angling just enough to keep distance without retreating. The adjustment was small, precise. When they passed, one of the men nodded briefly, more acknowledgment than apology, and continued on without slowing. The moment closed cleanly. Artemis felt the familiar urge to catalog it—to decide what it meant. She stopped herself. Meaning was no longer something she assigned afterward. It was something she chose in the moment. They continued on. The town breathed around them, quiet but not empty. Doors closed. A radio played somewhere behind a wall. Footsteps echoed briefly, then faded. "You're still watching," Leto said softly. "Yes," Artemis replied. "But you're not bracing." Artemis frowned, considering. "I'm not leaving either." Leto nodded, satisfied with that answer. They reached a small square where the street widened again, tension loosening with the space. Artemis paused under a lamp, light catching in her hair, and felt the strange duality settle deeper: vigilance without hostility, awareness without armor. She realized then that this wasn't something she could set down at the end of the day. It wasn't an exercise or a phase. It was a way of carrying herself that would follow her into sleep, into waking, into places that didn't announce themselves as dangerous or safe. That thought made her chest tighten—not with fear, but with weight. "I'm not putting it away," she said quietly. Leto looked at her. "I know." Artemis stepped forward again, shoulders squared—not hardened, not relaxed. Just present. The night didn't test her. It didn't reward her either. It simply let her pass. And as they walked on, Artemis understood that whatever came next—Italy, America, the wider world—this was what she would be bringing with her. Not certainty. Not anger. But a vigilance she chose to carry, even when she wished she didn't have to. They cut through a small courtyard Artemis hadn't thought about in years. It sat tucked between two rows of houses, stones worn smooth at the center where people had crossed the same path for generations. A fig tree leaned over the low wall, leaves rustling softly in the night breeze. Artemis slowed without realizing she had, the pull of memory stronger here than anywhere else they'd walked. She used to come here when she needed to think. Back then, thinking had been simple. You chose a position, hardened into it, and waited for the world to challenge you. Whatever came next would either prove you right or sharpen you further. Now, the space felt narrower—not physically, but emotionally. The memories didn't align cleanly with who she was standing there as. She stepped into the courtyard and stopped at the center, hands at her sides. The quiet pressed in, intimate and unhurried. A man's laughter drifted from a nearby house, followed by a woman's voice telling him to keep it down. Ordinary. Domestic. Untouched by her internal reckoning. Artemis waited for irritation. It didn't come. She felt instead the weight of remembering how she used to occupy spaces like this—alert, closed, prepared to defend even against imagined threat. The armor had been heavy, but it had been clear. It told her exactly who she was supposed to be. She didn't have that clarity anymore. "This used to be easy," she said, more to the air than to Leto. Leto stepped into the courtyard after her, stopping near the wall. "Easy doesn't mean light," she said. Artemis exhaled slowly. "It meant I didn't have to carry it with me." She shifted her weight, the stones cool under her boots. The vigilance she carried now felt different—less rigid, more demanding. It didn't lock her into place. It followed her, asking to be recalibrated constantly. She hated how much attention that required. A door opened at the far end of the courtyard. Someone stepped out, glanced briefly in their direction, then disappeared again. No judgment. No curiosity. Just a moment passing through another. Artemis tracked it, then let it go. She realized then that she wasn't nostalgic for who she'd been. She didn't want to return to that version of herself. What she missed was the simplicity of having a single answer to everything. The understanding tightened around that truth. Her anger hadn't protected her because it was right. It had protected her because it was easy. Artemis straightened, feeling the weight of that realization settle into her posture. She didn't feel relieved by it. If anything, it made the path ahead feel longer. They left the courtyard quietly, Artemis casting one last glance back at the fig tree silhouetted against the light. Memory stayed with her, but it no longer dictated how she moved. She carried it forward instead—another piece of the weight she was learning not to set down. And as they turned back toward the main street, Artemis understood something she couldn't undo: She wasn't losing herself. She was losing the shortcut she'd relied on to survive. Dawn found her awake before the light fully reached the hills. Artemis stood at the edge of the road that climbed out of town, the stones still cool beneath her boots, the sky thinning from black to gray above the sea. The world felt paused here—not asleep, not yet awake—caught between states in a way that mirrored her too closely to be comfortable. She breathed in slowly, measuring the air the way she now measured everything. The understanding she carried hadn't loosened overnight. If anything, it had settled deeper, no longer sharp but dense, like something she would have to lift every day whether she wanted to or not. She felt alert without being tense, present without bracing. That balance felt fragile. A car passed on the lower road, engine quiet, headlights fading quickly into the distance. Artemis tracked it out of habit, then let her attention fall away once it was gone. The movement felt deliberate—not forced, not rehearsed. She was choosing what to hold. Leto joined her without sound, standing a step back so Artemis could keep her space. The gesture mattered more than any words would have. They watched the light shift together, the horizon sharpening slowly into color. "I don't know how long this lasts," Artemis said finally. Leto nodded. "You don't have to." Artemis frowned. "That's not reassuring." "No," Leto agreed. "It's just honest." The road ahead curved out of sight, climbing toward places Artemis hadn't been yet. Italy. America. Names that carried weight without detail. She didn't feel ready for them. She doubted she ever would. But readiness no longer felt like the requirement. She stepped forward, then stopped, testing the motion. The old armor stirred faintly, reflex whispering that she should harden before moving into what she couldn't see. She let the whisper pass. Not because she trusted the world. Because she trusted herself to choose again when it mattered. That distinction felt thin and vital. "I won't forget," Artemis said, more to herself than to Leto. Leto glanced at her. "You don't strike me as someone who does." Artemis huffed softly, not amused, not angry. The understanding hadn't softened her. It hadn't forgiven anything. It had only clarified the shape of what lay ahead. Not answers. Responsibility. She turned back toward town, the first light spilling fully across the rooftops now. Greece hadn't healed her or anchored her. It had simply shown her the distance between who she was and who she would have to be next. That was enough. As they walked back down the hill, Artemis carried the weight with her—not as burden alone, but as orientation. She didn't know what Italy or America would demand of her. She only knew she wouldn't meet them the way she once would have. And that knowledge, unsettled and incomplete, pointed her forward without pretending the path would be easy. Morning arrived without ceremony. Suitcases stood by the door, half-packed and slightly mismatched, the kind of disorder that came from movement rather than haste. Artemis watched Leto check them, her motions practiced, unhurried. The routine felt familiar—leaving had always been easier than staying. This time, it didn't feel like escape. Artemis lifted her bag and adjusted the strap, feeling the weight settle across her shoulder. It was light compared to what she carried inside, and the contrast made her lips press thin. She hadn't slept much, not because of fear, but because her thoughts refused to arrange themselves into anything that resembled rest. Leaving Greece didn't feel like relief. It felt like continuation. They stepped outside together, the street already stirring with early movement. A man unlocked his shop across the way, glancing up briefly before returning to his work. A woman swept her doorway, the sound steady and rhythmic. Ordinary life asserting itself without pause. Artemis watched, alert but unarmed. She didn't brace. She didn't relax. She let the moments pass, measuring herself against them rather than against the people moving through them. At the end of the street, she stopped and looked back once more. Not searching for closure. Not looking for reassurance. Just acknowledging the place for what it had given her—and what it hadn't. "This isn't finished," she said quietly. Leto nodded. "It wasn't meant to be." Artemis exhaled, the tension in her shoulders easing just enough to let the words settle. Greece hadn't solved anything. It hadn't answered her questions or made the path ahead clearer. What it had done was strip away the last illusion that this change could be waited out. She turned forward again. The road stretched ahead, uncertain and demanding, but no longer abstract. Italy waited somewhere beyond it. America after that. Places that would test whether what she carried could hold under pressure—or whether she would reach for the armor again when the cost became too high. She didn't pretend to know the answer. As they walked toward the waiting car, Artemis felt the old reflex stir faintly, then settle again without command. The movement was almost automatic now—not instinct, but practice. That unsettled her. It also grounded her. She climbed into the car and closed the door, the sound final without being definitive. The engine started. The town began to slide away, rooftops giving way to open road. Artemis watched it go without regret or longing. This wasn't an ending. It was the moment she accepted that questioning wasn't a phase. It was the ground she would be walking on from here forward—whether she liked it or not. The road unwound ahead of them, pale under the morning light. Artemis watched it from the back seat, eyes following the line where stone gave way to scrub, where familiar curves straightened into something less forgiving. Greece receded without ceremony. No moment marked its passing. No feeling announced itself as final. That felt right. She rested her head against the window and let the movement carry her. The weight inside her hadn't lightened since dawn. If anything, it felt more settled now—less sharp, more constant. Like something she would have to account for every day, the way one accounts for gravity. Her thoughts drifted, not backward, not fully forward. Japan still sat somewhere in her awareness, not as memory but as origin. Greece pressed closer, not as comfort but as clarification. Neither place had solved anything. They had only narrowed the question. She flexed her fingers slowly, noting the absence of tension. No bracing. No hardening. Just awareness, steady and alert. The ease of it unsettled her again—not because it felt wrong, but because it felt repeatable. That was the dangerous part. If this became her default, then anger would no longer arrive uninvited. She would have to choose it. And choosing meant examining it. Justifying it. Carrying the consequence of it instead of letting it carry her. She didn't like that. She also didn't look away from it. Leto shifted in the front seat, adjusting her posture, sensing without turning that Artemis was still awake. She didn't speak. Neither did Artemis. The silence between them wasn't heavy anymore. It was functional. Enough. The car took a turn, the last glimpse of the sea vanishing behind a rise in the road. Artemis felt the loss register without pulling at her chest. She didn't reach for it. She didn't need to. The understanding she carried didn't ask her to forgive the world or soften toward it. It asked something far less generous and far more difficult: To remain present without certainty. To act without the comfort of inevitability. To let questions exist without demanding they resolve into answers. Her jaw tightened briefly, irritation stirring and fading. She let it go. Not because it was wrong—but because it wasn't required. As the road opened wider ahead, Artemis closed her eyes for a moment, not to rest, but to check herself. The awareness remained. The weight remained. So did the question. Not are men dangerous? Not can I trust them? But: Who am I when I don't already know how to react? She opened her eyes again, gaze steady on the road ahead. That question didn't frighten her the way it had before. It didn't comfort her either. It simply stayed. And as Greece disappeared fully behind them, Artemis understood that this—this ongoing, unresolved questioning—was not something she would grow out of before Italy or America or anything that followed. It was something she would be bringing with her. Not as armor. As awareness. And that was where this chapter ended—not with certainty, not with peace, but with a question she was no longer running from. ________________________________________ Read and Review ~Cubicreates ________________________________________ Chapter 19: The Man-Understanding Goddess ________________________________________ 150k+ words and 100k+ views!
