Ending the year with a bang! Presenting: "The Man-Understanding Goddess" ________________________________________ Italy did not feel foreign to her. That, more than anything else, unsettled Artemis. The air was warmer than Greece, softer around the edges, carrying the faint smells of stone, coffee, and something fried nearby. Voices drifted through the street in a language that felt familiar without being hers, rounded and expressive, alive with gestures. She walked among them with an ease that surprised her body even as her mind stayed alert. Here, she was Diana. Not Artemis of the Hunt. Not the daughter of Zeus. Not the goddess sharpened by old certainties. Just a woman moving through a city that had once reshaped her name and stories into something slightly different—less severe, more grounded, more human. The change was intentional. Leto had suggested it quietly, not as a command but as an option. Artemis had accepted without argument. Diana fit this place better. The name slid over her like borrowed clothing that had been worn in before. She adjusted the strap of her bag against her shoulder and kept walking. What she noticed first was not the men. It was the effort. Her attention no longer snapped outward like a bowstring pulled too tight. Instead, it hovered, alert but restrained, as if she were holding something heavy just above the ground. Every step carried awareness with it—of posture, of proximity, of tone. She was present in her body in a way she wasn't used to being. Tiring, she thought, without judgment. A man brushed past her at the corner of the street, muttering an apology without looking back. Instinct flared—brief, sharp, automatic. Her shoulders tensed, breath catching for a fraction of a second. Then she stopped it. Not forcefully. Not angrily. She let the tension loosen before it could settle, exhaled once, and kept walking. The correction was small. Almost invisible. It still cost her something. By the time she reached the small square ahead, her jaw ached faintly, as if she'd been clenching it without noticing. She paused near the fountain, pretending to admire the stonework while she checked herself again. No danger. No insult. Just movement, coincidence, a crowded street. Normal. That word still felt strange in her mouth. Leto lingered at the edge of the square, giving her space without retreating too far. Artemis caught the familiar shape of her in the corner of her vision and felt steadier for it, even as she resisted the impulse to lean on that presence too much. This was the point, wasn't it? To walk forward without armor. To notice herself without immediately judging. To exist in the space between reaction and response. Artemis straightened, rolled her shoulders once, and stepped back into the flow of the city. Diana, for now, would have to learn how to move here. And learning, she was beginning to understand, was far more exhausting than certainty ever had been. The café was small enough that the noise never rose above a murmur. Metal chairs scraped softly against stone, cups clinked, a radio hummed somewhere behind the counter. Artemis chose a table near the edge, back to the wall without thinking—and then noticed she'd done it. She didn't move. The choice had already been made. Undoing it now would feel theatrical. A man approached with a tray, navigating the narrow space between tables with practiced ease. He wore an apron dusted lightly with flour and moved like someone used to apologizing with his shoulders before his mouth caught up. "Scusi," he said, sidestepping her chair. Instinct sparked. Her spine straightened, attention narrowing, senses sharpening in the old, familiar way. It was automatic, precise, efficient. A thousand past encounters had trained her to read movement first and intention second. Then she stopped it. Not the awareness—just the conclusion. She let the tension ease a fraction, loosened her shoulders deliberately. The man was already past her, already turning toward another table, his focus elsewhere. No threat. No intrusion. Just proximity in a crowded place. Her breath came out slow through her nose. When he returned to take her order, she felt the effort more keenly. Her mind hovered just behind the moment, ready to intervene if she slipped. The man smiled out of habit rather than warmth, pen poised. "What can I get you?" he asked, in careful English. There it was again—that tightening, the reflexive appraisal. She caught it mid-formation this time, smoothed her expression before it reached her eyes. "An espresso," she said. After a pause she added, "Please." The word felt heavier than it should have. He nodded, scribbled, moved on. Artemis watched him cross the café, noticing details she usually filtered out: the way his shoulders slumped slightly when he reached the counter, the tired set of his mouth, the efficiency of hands that had done this too many times today. The world did not sharpen around him. It simply existed. When he returned with her cup, he set it down carefully, fingers lingering just long enough to steady the saucer. "Here you go." "Thank you," she replied, meeting his eyes briefly and then looking away—not as avoidance, but as choice. He smiled again, a little more genuine this time, and moved off without another word. Nothing had gone wrong. That was the strange part. She wrapped her hands around the cup, feeling the heat seep into her palms. The effort drained through her in a slow wave, leaving behind a faint ache behind her eyes. It had taken focus to remain where she was, to let the moment pass without armor or edge. Neutral. Polite. Ordinary. Artemis sipped her coffee and stared into the dark surface, aware of the cost even as she acknowledged the success. She had checked herself. It hadn't been hard. It also hadn't been free. The street market was louder than the café had been—voices overlapping, vendors calling out prices, footsteps pressing close in narrow lanes. Artemis moved through it with Leto a few steps behind, pretending not to notice how easily her stride adapted, how naturally she threaded through bodies without touching them. She felt fine. Grounded. Balanced. That was when a man reached for her arm. Not roughly. Not forcefully. Just a brief, instinctive touch—two fingers brushing her sleeve as he leaned in to keep her from stepping backward into a passing cart. "Careful—" She twisted away before the word finished forming. The reaction was sharp and immediate, her body already turning, eyes hardening, hand lifting half a breath from where a weapon would have been if this were another life. The man froze, palm raised in reflexive surrender, surprise written plainly across his face. "I—sorry," he said quickly. "You almost—" "Don't touch me," Artemis snapped. The words landed heavier than she intended. The space around them thinned, nearby conversations stuttering as attention tilted their way. She realized it a second too late. The man's expression shifted—not anger, not offense, but something quieter. Embarrassment. A tightening around the eyes, a faint flush creeping up his neck. He nodded once, stiff and abrupt. "Right," he said. "My mistake." He stepped back into the crowd without another word, swallowed by movement and noise as if he'd never been there. Artemis stood very still. The market resumed its rhythm around her, but something had slipped out of alignment. The air felt heavier, her pulse loud in her ears. She replayed the moment unbidden—the touch, the warning, the look on his face when she cut him off. Too fast. Too hard. Leto appeared at her side, quiet, observant. She didn't speak. She didn't need to. The absence of comment pressed more than any reprimand could have. "I didn't mean—" Artemis started, then stopped. The excuse formed easily, polished and ready, but she let it die unspoken. Intent didn't erase impact. She exhaled slowly, unclenching her jaw. The irritation that usually followed such encounters didn't come. No righteous anger. No sense of justified distance. Just a dull, uncomfortable awareness that she had misread the moment and acted on an old script. It would have been easier if he'd argued. If he'd pushed back. If he'd deserved it. But he hadn't. As they moved on, Artemis felt the cost settle in—a thin, persistent discomfort, like a stone in her shoe. She had wanted to do better. She had almost done better. Almost wasn't enough. She didn't spiral. She didn't condemn herself. She simply carried the mistake with her, unlightened, unresolved. And that, somehow, was worse. The train south was late. That much was clear from the way people stood rather than sat, from the glances cast at the departure board as if staring might shame it into changing. Artemis leaned against a pillar, bag at her feet, counting breaths without meaning to. A man stood beside her—not close enough to crowd, not far enough to vanish. Middle-aged, neatly dressed, reading a folded newspaper with the concentration of someone killing time rather than consuming news. She noticed him. She always noticed. What she didn't do was decide anything about him. The effort showed itself in small ways. She resisted cataloguing his movements, the angle of his elbows, the rhythm of his breathing. She let him be a shape in space rather than a possibility. It took more focus than she liked to admit. After a few minutes, he sighed softly and folded the paper. "If this gets any later," he said to no one in particular, "we may arrive before we depart." The comment hovered, unclaimed. Artemis felt the familiar urge to retreat inward, to keep silence as a shield. Conversation invited variables. Variables led to assumptions. Assumptions led to friction. She answered anyway. "Trains," she said. "They do that." He smiled, brief and genuine, and nodded as if she'd confirmed something he already believed. "You'd think after all this time they'd perfect it." "Maybe delay is part of the design." A pause followed. Not awkward. Just… present. He didn't push. Didn't fill the space. Simply stood there, hands tucked into his coat pockets, eyes drifting back toward the board. The restraint surprised her. She realized she'd been bracing for the next line. It never came. When the announcement finally crackled overhead, he inclined his head toward her in mild triumph. "There we go." They boarded in the same carriage but chose different seats without ceremony. No lingering. No expectation. Just two people sharing a moment that ended when it ended. As the train pulled away, Artemis watched the countryside slide past the window—olive trees blurring into green streaks, stone walls dissolving into distance. Her reflection ghosted faintly in the glass, superimposed over the world beyond. She waited for a reaction that didn't arrive. No irritation. No pride. No relief sharp enough to be called victory. Just a quiet, persistent awareness of effort spent and effort saved. She hadn't defaulted. She hadn't overcorrected either. It hadn't felt good. It had felt… workable. When Leto joined her a few minutes later, settling into the seat opposite, Artemis didn't immediately speak. She didn't need to explain the exchange; nothing remarkable had happened. That, she realized, was the point. As Italy receded behind them and the journey toward Greece began, Artemis closed her eyes briefly—not in escape, but in recalibration. The path forward wasn't smooth or clear. But it was navigable. And for now, that was enough. The ferry smelled of salt and diesel and old paint baked warm by the sun. Artemis stood at the rail while the shoreline pulled away, Italy shrinking into a suggestion of hills and stone and memory. The wind tugged at her hair, cool and persistent, carrying voices from the deck behind her—laughter, irritation, a child crying and being soothed. She kept her eyes on the water. Travel had always meant motion without attachment. Leave before roots form. Move before familiarity turns into expectation. It was a rule she had lived by for centuries, and it had never asked anything from her in return. Now it did. A man leaned on the rail several steps down, tossing breadcrumbs to the gulls with absentminded precision. He wasn't trying to draw attention, wasn't watching her reflection in the glassy metal. He existed alongside her, parallel rather than intersecting. She felt the old itch to catalogue him—and didn't. Instead, she focused on the rhythm of the waves slapping against the hull, the steady pulse of the engine beneath her feet. The world was large here, indifferent, ancient. It reminded her of herself in ways she didn't entirely like. Leto joined her without comment, resting her forearms on the rail. For a long moment they watched the water together. "You did better," Leto said at last, quietly. Artemis stiffened, then relaxed. "I wasn't trying to." "That's usually when it counts." The words landed softly, without weight or demand. Artemis resisted the urge to argue—not because she agreed, but because she didn't want to turn the moment into a debate. The effort cost her more than the silence would have. "I don't like this," she admitted after a while. "Not knowing which parts of me are… reflex. Which are choice." Leto's gaze stayed on the horizon. "Most people never separate the two." "I'm not most people." "No," Leto agreed. "That's why it's harder." A gull swooped low, snatching a crumb midair. The man down the rail laughed under his breath, pleased. Artemis watched him for a second too long, then looked away, irritated with herself for noticing at all. She wasn't changing because she wanted to be liked. She wasn't changing because the world demanded it. She was changing because staying the same had finally started to feel wrong. The ferry horn sounded, long and low, announcing their approach. Greece waited ahead—white stone and sun-bleached roads, the weight of home and expectation layered thick in the air. Artemis straightened, rolling her shoulders once as if shedding water. Italy had been practice. A place to stumble without consequence. Greece would not be so forgiving. As the ferry cut through the last stretch of sea, Artemis stayed at the rail, eyes forward, bracing herself—not against men, not against the world, but against the effort she knew she would have to keep making. And the quiet fear that one day, she might choose not to. Greece did not feel like arrival. It felt like pressure. The port was bright and loud in a way Italy hadn't been—voices sharper, gestures broader, familiarity assumed rather than offered. Artemis felt it settle over her the moment her feet hit the dock, a weight she had carried before and resented every time. Here, people recognized her shape, even if they did not know her name. A dockhand waved them forward, calling instructions with easy authority. His tone wasn't rude. It was efficient. Used to being obeyed. Artemis felt her shoulders tighten anyway. "Slow down," Leto murmured beside her, not a command, just a reminder. Artemis exhaled through her nose and adjusted her pace. The man was already turning away, attention shifting to the next group. Nothing lingered. Nothing pressed. Still, the irritation stayed. They walked uphill toward the town, stone buildings stacked close together, balconies heavy with flowers. This was familiar ground—too familiar. Every interaction carried history, whether earned or imagined. At a small bakery, Leto stopped to buy bread. Artemis waited outside, arms crossed, watching people pass. A pair of older men argued animatedly nearby, their voices rising and falling like a practiced dance. She tracked the volume, the posture, the proximity—then stopped herself. You don't need to be on guard here, she told herself. The lie tasted thin. One of the men glanced her way, curiosity flickering across his face. Not appraisal. Not challenge. Just noticing something out of place. Artemis met his gaze for half a second longer than necessary before looking away, jaw tight. When Leto returned, bread tucked under her arm, she studied Artemis's expression without comment. "It's different here," Artemis said, before Leto could speak. "Yes." "They don't mean harm," Artemis added, sharper than she intended. "No," Leto agreed again. "But meaning isn't the only thing that matters." They continued walking. Artemis felt restless, skin too tight, patience fraying at the edges. Greece asked her to be herself in a way Italy hadn't. It remembered her. Expected her to fit. At a crossroads, a young man stepped slightly into her path while gesturing to someone behind him. She adjusted automatically, sidestepping with clipped efficiency. He apologized quickly, sheepish, and moved on. She realized she'd never looked at his face. That bothered her. Not because she'd avoided him—but because she hadn't chosen to. As they climbed higher into the town, Artemis felt the effort return, heavier now, less forgiving. This was not a place to experiment. This was a place that would reflect her back at herself, unchanged. She wasn't sure she was ready for that reflection. But she kept walking anyway. The afternoon heat settled in thick layers, pressing down on the town until even conversation seemed slower. Artemis sat on a low stone wall overlooking the narrow road below, watching shadows shorten and stretch as people passed. This was where habits returned. A group of young men walked together up the incline, laughing loudly, voices bouncing off stone. Nothing about them was threatening—sunburned skin, casual clothes, careless energy—but Artemis felt her attention narrow anyway. She tracked them without meaning to, gauging distance, predicting movement. One of them glanced up and caught her looking. The reflex fired before thought. Her posture shifted, chin lifting just enough to signal warning, eyes sharpening into something colder. The message was old and well-practiced: Don't come closer. Don't try. The young man slowed, confusion flickering across his face. He said something to his friends, laughter fading as they adjusted course, giving her a wider berth than necessary as they passed. The effort she'd been making—catching herself, correcting—felt suddenly inadequate. It was easier in places where no one knew her, where nothing pressed old memories into motion. Here, the world expected the huntress. And she gave it exactly what it wanted. "I don't want to be this," Artemis said quietly. Not angry. Not dramatic. Just tired. Leto's hand rested briefly against her back, a grounding touch that didn't ask permission and didn't demand acceptance. Artemis didn't flinch—but she noticed that she'd allowed it. "That's why we stay," Leto said. "Not because you're ready. Because you aren't." The words settled, uncomfortable but steady. Below them, the road filled again—vendors closing stalls, neighbors exchanging greetings, life resuming its ordinary rhythm. Artemis watched it all, aware of the distance she'd placed between herself and everyone else. She hadn't failed. But she hadn't succeeded either. And for the first time, she couldn't tell which bothered her more. The evening market unfolded in slow, deliberate layers—lanterns flickering on, voices lowering as the heat eased, the smell of grilled food drifting through the narrow streets. Artemis moved beside Leto, neither leading nor following, just matching pace. She kept her hands visible. It was a small thing. Deliberate. An anchor. At a produce stall, a man argued cheerfully with the vendor over the price of figs. His gestures were broad, expressive, his laughter loud enough to turn a few heads. Artemis felt the familiar flicker of irritation spark, ready to harden into judgment. She didn't feed it. Instead, she shifted her weight and focused on the figs themselves—their deep purple skins, the sticky sweetness clinging to the air. The man paid, still smiling, and turned, nearly colliding with her. "Oh—sorry," he said quickly, stepping back. The moment hung there, fragile. Her first instinct rose sharp and fast—distance, boundary, control. She felt it crest, ready to break into words or posture. She chose something smaller. "It's fine," Artemis said. The words were plain. Unremarkable. They didn't carry warmth or warning—just acknowledgment. The man nodded, relieved, and moved on without another glance. Artemis stood very still, heart beating too fast for the insignificance of the exchange. The effort pulsed through her muscles, a quiet tremor she hid by reaching for a fig from the stall. "That took work," Leto said eventually. Artemis didn't argue. "It shouldn't have." "But it did." The admission sat heavy in her chest. She hadn't done anything remarkable. She hadn't learned a lesson or gained clarity. She'd simply not reacted—and the restraint had cost her more than any confrontation would have. Her mind replayed the moment, searching for error, for overcorrection. Had she sounded cold? Dismissive? Had she traded one distortion for another? She didn't know. That uncertainty itched. As they climbed toward their lodging, Artemis felt the fatigue settle deeper, seeping into her bones. This wasn't fear. It wasn't anger either. It was the steady drain of vigilance turned inward. At the doorway, she paused, fingers resting against the stone frame. "I don't like how much this takes," she said, not looking at Leto. Leto's response was immediate and gentle. "That doesn't mean it isn't worth doing." Artemis closed her eyes for a brief second, then nodded once. She hadn't grown softer. She had simply taken a smaller step. And somehow, that felt harder. Shopkeepers raised shutters, neighbors exchanged greetings, the day arranging itself without concern for her internal calculus. They walked downhill toward the square, and Artemis felt the familiar tension creep back—not sharp, not immediate, but constant. Like holding a bowstring half-drawn for hours. A man stepped out of a doorway ahead of them, nearly colliding with her shoulder. He muttered an apology, distracted, already turning away. She caught herself after the reaction started. Her body angled away, expression tightening, breath pulling shallow. The old response didn't fully form—but it didn't vanish either. It lingered halfway, awkward and incomplete. The man glanced back, misreading her stiffness as offense. "I said sorry," he added, a note of defensiveness creeping in. There it was. This was the moment she used to understand perfectly. The escalation point. The place where clarity lived. Artemis hesitated. The pause stretched just long enough to become uncomfortable. "I know," she said finally. "I'm just—tired." They stood there for a second longer than necessary, then he nodded and moved on. Artemis felt the exchange settle into her chest—not clean, not victorious. The moment hadn't gone wrong, but it hadn't gone smoothly either. It carried friction, the residue of a near-miss. She hated that. The old way would have been sharper. Faster. Clean. This way was messy. As they continued walking, Artemis felt the weight of it press in—not fear, not doubt, but frustration. She had chosen differently. She had tried. And it had still cost her. Leto glanced at her, reading the tension without comment. She didn't intervene. Didn't soothe. Artemis was left alone with the outcome of her choice. "I did what you said," Artemis muttered at last. Leto stopped walking. "I never said it would feel better," she replied calmly. "Only that it would be yours." The distinction landed hard. Artemis resumed walking, jaw tight, mind churning. Understanding wasn't clarity. It wasn't peace. It was work—ongoing, imperfect, exhausting work that didn't even promise immediate reward. She wasn't afraid anymore. She was irritated. Wooden tables, uneven legs, the smell of oil and bread. Artemis chose a seat in the shade and set her bag at her feet, deliberately not scanning the room. She was tired of scanning. A server approached—a young man, not much older than she was now in this body. He smiled automatically, pen ready, eyes moving between them. "What can I get you?" he asked. Artemis felt the familiar tightening start—then stopped it. She softened her shoulders, kept her gaze neutral, waited an extra beat before speaking. "I'll have the grilled vegetables," she said. Not disastrously. Just… not what she'd ordered. Meat instead of vegetables, the smell heavy and unmistakable. Her first instinct surged—sharp, indignant, ready. This was an error. It needed correcting. Immediately. She checked herself. Don't snap. Don't assume. Don't turn it into a thing. She raised her hand when the server passed again. He stopped, attentive but wary now, something in her posture already signaling complication. "Excuse me," she said, keeping her tone even. "I ordered the vegetables." He frowned, glancing at the plate. "You did?" "Yes." He hesitated, uncertainty flickering. "I wrote this down," he said, tapping his notepad. Not accusing. Just… defensive. Artemis felt irritation spike—then push it down harder than before. Too hard. "Oh," she said. "Then it's fine. Don't worry about it." The words came out too quickly. Relief washed over his face. But she hadn't wanted this either. "I should've said something," Artemis muttered. "Yes," Leto agreed quietly. The simple confirmation stung. Artemis pushed the plate away slightly, appetite gone. The loss was small—lunch, comfort, correctness. Nothing meaningful. And yet it scraped. This was the cost she hadn't anticipated: not just effort, but miscalibration. Pulling back so far she disappeared. Avoiding conflict by swallowing herself instead. She hadn't defaulted. She'd overcorrected. As they left the table behind, Artemis felt the frustration settle deep, heavy and unresolved. Understanding, she realized, wasn't just knowing when not to react. It was learning when to speak—and she wasn't there yet. They walked for a long time after lunch, the road winding upward into quieter streets where tourists thinned and locals reclaimed the day. Artemis felt the mistake from the café sit heavy in her chest—not as guilt, but as irritation sharpened by hunger. Not at the food. At herself. She replayed the moment again and again, seeing where she'd veered too far, where restraint had tipped into disappearance. The effort wasn't in noticing anymore. It was in deciding how much of herself was allowed to exist in a moment. At a small roadside stand, Leto stopped to buy water. Artemis lingered a few steps back, watching dust drift lazily in the heat. The vendor—an older man with weathered hands—passed a bottle to Leto, then glanced at Artemis. "You want one too?" he asked, tone casual, inclusive. The reflex stirred. Not sharp this time—just wary. Artemis felt the familiar urge to defer, to let Leto answer, to avoid even this small engagement. She didn't. "Yes," she said, stepping forward. "Please." The man nodded and reached for another bottle. When he handed it to her, their fingers brushed briefly. Her body reacted—and she caught it in time. She didn't flinch. Didn't pull back. Didn't harden. She took the bottle. "Thank you," she said, and meant it. The exchange was ordinary. Unremarkable. It ended exactly where it should have. As they moved on, Artemis noticed her breathing had steadied faster than it had earlier. The effort was still there, but it hadn't spiked into discomfort or collapse. She hadn't erased herself to maintain peace. That mattered. "I didn't disappear that time," she said, almost to herself. Leto glanced sideways. "No." "I didn't feel… punished for speaking either." "That's usually how it works," Leto replied. "Most of the time." Artemis frowned slightly. "I don't like that it's a calculation." Leto smiled faintly. "Neither do most people. They just do it anyway." They climbed higher, the town spreading below them in white and gold. Artemis paused near a low wall, twisting the bottle cap open and taking a long drink. The water was lukewarm, grounding. She wasn't fixed. She wasn't even confident. But she had adjusted—not in theory, not in hindsight, but in motion. The realization didn't bring relief. It brought something closer to determination. Understanding wasn't about replacing one rule with another. It was about finding the line—and being willing to redraw it every time it shifted. That sounded exhausting. Artemis straightened, rolling her shoulders once. She kept walking anyway. They left Greece at dawn. The sky was pale, washed thin of color, the kind of morning that felt unfinished. Artemis stood on the deck as the ferry eased away from the harbor, the town retreating into white shapes and memory. She felt lighter than she had days ago—and heavier for knowing why. This wasn't relief. It was accumulation. Every moment of restraint, every correction mid-thought, every misstep carried forward with her. She hadn't shed anything in Greece. She'd learned how much she was willing to carry without dropping herself entirely. A man leaned against the opposite rail, headphones on, eyes closed, face turned toward the breeze. Artemis noticed him—and then noticed how she noticed him. Not as a potential problem. Not as something to brace against. Just as a presence. The realization came quietly, without triumph. She didn't hold onto it. She let it pass like everything else. Italy had taught her to pause. Greece had taught her to endure the pause without becoming smaller. As the ferry cut through open water, Artemis felt the effort settle into a steadier rhythm. Still tiring. Still deliberate. But no longer sharp at the edges. The ache behind her eyes dulled into something manageable. She hadn't found peace. She had found practice. Leto joined her at the rail, hands folded loosely, gaze fixed on the horizon. They didn't speak. They didn't need to. The silence between them was not empty—it was full of unfinished work and shared understanding. "Next," Artemis said eventually, not a question. "Yes," Leto replied. Italy had been trial. Greece had been resistance. What came next would ask something different of her. Artemis felt it in the set of her spine, in the quiet readiness beneath the fatigue. She was not bracing anymore. She was preparing. As the sun climbed higher and the water brightened, Artemis turned her face into the wind. She wasn't looking for certainty. She wasn't seeking forgiveness—from herself or anyone else. She was learning how to move forward without the old tools. And though the effort still weighed on her, she no longer wondered whether it was worth making. She stepped back from the rail as the ferry surged onward, carrying her away from home—not in escape, but in continuation. The work wasn't finished. But it had begun to move with her, instead of against her. America announced itself loudly. The airport was all glass and steel and motion, a constant churn of people moving with purpose or pretending to. Artemis felt the shift immediately—the pace faster, the distances wider, the assumptions different. No shared history pressed in here. No memory clung to her skin. That should have made it easier. It didn't. She walked beside Leto through the terminal, senses alert in a way that felt reflexive rather than chosen. Here, the crowd didn't look at her the same way. Curiosity skimmed instead of lingering. Boundaries were implied rather than negotiated. She didn't know which version of herself this place expected. Outside, the air was dry and bright, the sun harsher than Greece's warmth. Cars streamed past in steady lines, engines humming like insects. Artemis paused for a half-second too long at the curb, recalibrating. A man behind her cleared his throat—not impatient, just present. She felt the reaction start, caught it early, let it soften. Stepped forward. They found a small place to stay not far from the river—clean, anonymous, forgettable in the best way. Artemis set her bag down and stood still for a moment, listening to unfamiliar sounds through the open window: traffic, voices drifting upward, music leaking from a passing car. This place did not know her. The thought should have comforted her. Instead, it unsettled her. Later, they walked along the river path, the water wide and steady, reflecting the sky in broken fragments. People passed in pairs and groups, runners threading between them, cyclists ringing bells in warning. Artemis tracked the movement without sinking into it, effort humming quietly beneath the surface. A man jogged past too close, shoulder nearly brushing hers. She adjusted her step without snapping, without vanishing. The correction was clean. That surprised her. She slowed, letting Leto move a step ahead. "This feels… different," Artemis said. "It is," Leto replied. "Different rules. Different expectations." "I don't know what I'm supposed to be here." Leto didn't answer immediately. "Then don't decide yet." Artemis frowned. "That's never been an option." "It is now." They stopped near the rail, watching the river slide past with unhurried confidence. Artemis rested her hands against the cool metal, grounding herself. She felt the effort still there—present, persistent—but it wasn't fighting her anymore. She wasn't defaulting. She wasn't overcorrecting either. She was… holding. As dusk settled, the lights along the river flickered on, one by one. Artemis watched the reflections ripple, break, reform. Understanding didn't arrive in a neat shape. It didn't resolve anything. But for the first time, standing in a place that owed her nothing and expected even less, Artemis felt the possibility of movement without armor. She stayed at the rail a while longer, not waiting for certainty—just letting the moment exist. Tomorrow would ask more of her. For now, this was enough. The diner smelled like grease and coffee and something sweet trying too hard to be comforting. Artemis slid into the booth opposite Leto, vinyl sticking faintly to her skin. The place was half-full—late afternoon, between rushes. People talked in low clusters, forks scraping plates, a bell chiming softly every time the kitchen door swung open. This was supposed to be easy. Anonymous places usually were. A server approached—male, mid-thirties, tired eyes, practiced smile. He placed menus down with quick efficiency. "I'll be right back with water." Artemis nodded, already bracing for the interaction she told herself she didn't need to brace for. When he returned, he leaned in slightly to set the glasses down, one hand steadying the table as it wobbled. His arm crossed her field of view for half a second longer than necessary. Her chest tightened. Too close. The judgment formed fast, slick and familiar. Her mind filled in intention where there was only proximity. She felt the old reaction press forward, ready to shape her voice, her posture. She stopped it. Not smoothly—hesitantly. The pause stretched just long enough to be noticed. "Everything okay?" the server asked, brow creasing. "Yes," Artemis said, a beat too late. "Sorry." The word came out wrong. Not defensive—uncertain. He straightened immediately, hands lifting in an unconscious gesture of retreat. "Didn't mean to crowd you," he said. "Old tables." He moved away quickly, expression neutral but guarded now. Artemis sat frozen. That hadn't been a success. She'd caught herself—but not in time to prevent damage. The misread had already colored the moment, already shifted the dynamic. She felt it settle like grit between her teeth. "I assumed," Artemis said quietly, more to herself than to Leto. "Yes," Leto replied. "I didn't snap." "No." "But I still—" Artemis trailed off, frustrated. She pressed her fingers together under the table, grounding herself in the small pressure. "I still treated him like a problem." Leto didn't contradict her. The server returned later to take their order, polite but distant. Efficient. Careful. Artemis felt the cost then—not sharp, not dramatic, just present. A small fracture where there hadn't needed to be one. She ordered simply and kept her tone neutral, correcting nothing, explaining nothing. It wasn't her job to fix every misstep. She knew that. But knowing didn't make the weight disappear. When the food arrived, the plate was right this time. Artemis ate slowly, appetite dulled by the lingering awareness of her mistake. She hadn't defaulted into aggression. She'd defaulted into assumption. As they paid and stood to leave, the server nodded at them, professional and contained. Artemis returned the nod, feeling the unfinished nature of the exchange press against her ribs. Outside, the air felt cooler, wider. "That one cost you," Leto said gently. Artemis nodded. "Not much." "No," Leto agreed. "But enough to feel." Artemis looked back once at the diner window, then turned away. Understanding, she realized, wasn't about getting it right. It was about noticing why she got it wrong—and carrying that forward without armor or apology. They walked afterward without deciding where to go, letting the streets choose for them. The city thinned as they moved away from the river—brick buildings giving way to quieter blocks, trees breaking the lines of concrete. Artemis felt the diner incident follow her like a low-grade ache. Not shame. Not anger. Something closer to irritation with herself for having misjudged—and then for not knowing how to correct without making it worse. The work didn't end when the moment did. That was the part she resented most. At a small bookstore tucked between a laundromat and a florist, Leto slowed and went inside. Artemis hesitated, then followed, the bell over the door chiming softly. The place smelled of paper and dust and lemon cleaner. Narrow aisles forced proximity. Artemis noted it and adjusted—not by stiffening, but by choosing where to stand. She kept her space without claiming more than she needed. A man browsing nearby reached for a book on the same shelf she was scanning. Their hands brushed. This time, the reaction came—and passed. She felt it rise, recognized it instantly, and let it dissolve without ceremony. No pause. No explanation. No tightening in her voice. "Sorry," he said automatically. "It's fine," Artemis replied, and kept reading. That was it. No echo. No cost she could feel afterward. The ease of it surprised her more than the success itself. She closed the book she'd been pretending to examine and stepped aside, making room without retreating. The man took the book, nodded once, and moved on. Artemis stood there, oddly steady. This hadn't been a breakthrough. No insight had clicked into place. She hadn't suddenly understood anything new about men or the world. She had simply adjusted—mid-motion, without drama. When Leto found her a few minutes later, Artemis didn't look up right away. "That one didn't hurt," she said. Leto's mouth curved faintly. "Because you didn't make it into a test." Artemis considered that. "I didn't try to be right. Or careful." "You were present," Leto said. "That's usually enough." They left the bookstore together, the bell chiming again as the door closed behind them. Outside, the afternoon light had softened, shadows stretching long and forgiving across the pavement. Artemis felt tired—but not scraped raw the way she had earlier. The effort was still there, but it moved with her now, not against her stride. She hadn't gained approval. No one had noticed what she'd done differently. And for the first time, that didn't feel like a loss. It felt… correct. She walked on, carrying the adjustment forward—not as a rule, not as a promise, but as something she could do again if she needed to. That was enough. The afternoon thinned into evening without announcement. They found themselves back near the river as the sky began to dull, light flattening into softer tones. Artemis leaned on the rail, elbows resting against cool metal, watching the water carry reflections that never stayed whole for long. This was usually where she grew restless. Too still. Too much time to think. A man stood a short distance away, sketchbook balanced against his knee, pencil moving in small, decisive strokes. He wasn't watching her. He wasn't watching anyone. His attention stayed on the page, expression set in quiet focus. Artemis noticed—and didn't interpret. She watched the way his hand moved, the economy of it, the certainty. It reminded her of archery practice long ago, when the body learned what to do before the mind interfered. The comparison came uninvited, then faded. He shifted slightly, glanced up, and caught her looking. The moment hung. She didn't flinch. Didn't harden. Didn't soften either. He nodded once, polite and neutral, then returned to his sketch. Artemis felt something ease in her chest—not relief, not approval. Just the absence of strain. The interaction ended exactly where it should have. After a few minutes, he packed up his things and walked past her, close enough to register but not close enough to intrude. As he passed, he spoke without slowing. "Good light tonight," he said, more observation than invitation. "Yes," Artemis replied. That was all. He didn't turn back. Didn't linger. The exchange dissolved into the evening as cleanly as it had formed. Artemis stayed at the rail, aware of how unremarkable the moment had been—and how much that mattered. She hadn't measured herself. Hadn't checked her reactions after the fact. The effort had been present, but light, like a muscle finally learning its range. Leto joined her, gaze following the path the man had taken before returning to the water. "That was different," she said. Artemis nodded. "I didn't think about it." "Exactly." The city lights flickered on, one by one, reflections stretching across the river's surface. Artemis watched them without urgency. Understanding didn't arrive as a revelation. It came as competence—quiet, repeatable, unspectacular. She wasn't fixed. But she was functioning. And for the first time, the idea didn't irritate her. She pushed away from the rail and straightened, the movement unguarded. Tomorrow would bring new moments, new chances to misread or misstep. She didn't dread them. She simply acknowledged they would come—and that she could meet them without armor, without erasure, without making every interaction a trial. That felt like progress she could live with. The evening air cooled just enough to make walking comfortable. They found a small food truck tucked into a corner lot, strings of lights buzzing faintly overhead. People stood in loose clusters, waiting, talking in half-finished sentences. Artemis took her place in line beside Leto, hands relaxed at her sides. A man ahead of them turned slightly, checking the menu posted above the truck. "You been here before?" he asked, tone easy, not angled. The question landed without friction. Artemis felt the reflex stir—then settle. No tightening. No spike. Just awareness. "No," she answered. "But it smells good." He smiled, quick and unassuming. "It usually is. Takes a while, though." "That's fine," she said. And meant it. The line crept forward. The man commented once more about the wait, about how the city never seemed to agree on how hungry it was at night. Artemis responded when it felt natural, let silence exist when it didn't. She didn't track his hands. Didn't weigh his proximity. She simply stood there, present. Nothing pressed. Nothing demanded. When they reached the counter, he ordered and stepped aside, giving her space without being asked. She ordered next, voice steady, unguarded. As they waited, he nodded toward the river lights in the distance. "Looks different at night," he said. "Yes," Artemis replied. "Calmer." He considered that, then shrugged. "Depends on where you're standing." She almost smiled. The food came. He thanked the server, wished them both a good night, and walked off without expectation. The exchange ended cleanly—no residue, no cost. Artemis realized she wasn't replaying it. They sat at a nearby table, eating in companionable quiet. The food was good—simple, satisfying. Artemis noticed the taste instead of the room. "That one didn't take anything out of you," Leto observed. Artemis paused, checking in with herself. The fatigue was still there, low and constant, but it hadn't spiked. She wasn't hollowed out by effort. "No," she said slowly. "It didn't." "Why?" Artemis thought about it. "I didn't try to manage it. Or myself." Leto nodded, satisfied. Artemis finished her meal, wiped her hands, and leaned back in the chair. The night moved around them—voices, laughter, footsteps—without demanding her attention. This wasn't understanding as theory. It wasn't a lesson she could recite. It was something quieter: the knowledge that she could have a normal conversation, let it exist, and walk away without feeling diminished or armored. She stood when they were done, posture easy. For once, she didn't feel like she'd won or lost anything. She'd just… been there. And that felt like something she could build on. The river path curved ahead, widening where the bank dipped low and the water slowed. Night settled in layers—cooler air, softer edges, sound carrying farther than it should. Artemis walked a few steps ahead of Leto, not out of impatience, just momentum. She was tired. Not scraped, not raw—used. The good kind of tired, earned by choosing again and again. A group of people gathered near the railing farther on, laughter lifting and falling. Artemis adjusted her path to pass wide, unthinking. The adjustment came easily now, like a foot finding ground it had already learned. Then she saw him. He stood apart from the group, half-turned toward the water, attention fixed on something downstream. There was nothing remarkable about him at first glance—dark hair, unassuming clothes, posture relaxed without being careless. What caught her wasn't his shape. It was the stillness. Not absence. Presence without noise. Artemis slowed without realizing she had. Her breath caught—not in alarm, not in anticipation. Just… recognition of a moment that refused to be background. He shifted, glanced over his shoulder, and met her eyes. The world narrowed—not sharpened. Softened. She felt the reflex rise and found nothing to anchor to. No threat assessment. No need to brace or retreat. The effort she'd been carrying didn't spike; it went quiet, as if waiting. They held the look for a heartbeat too long. Then he smiled—not wide, not practiced. A small, uncertain curve, like someone acknowledging a coincidence they didn't fully understand. Artemis returned it before thinking. The realization landed after: she hadn't checked herself. Hadn't moderated or measured. The reaction had simply happened—and hadn't cost her anything. He stepped closer to the rail, giving her space without ceremony. "It's calmer over there," he said, nodding downstream. Not an invitation. Just information. "Yes," she replied. The word felt sufficient. They stood like that for a moment—parallel, not intersecting—watching the water carry light and shadow together. She noticed the way his attention stayed outward, not on her. Not probing. Not seeking. It felt… safe, in a way she hadn't defined before. Leto paused a few steps back, watching without interrupting. After a beat, he nodded once, polite and oddly formal, and turned back toward the group. The moment ended as cleanly as it had begun. Artemis exhaled, slow and steady. She didn't chase it. Didn't analyze it. She let it remain what it was: brief, unclaimed, unresolved. As she walked on, the feeling followed—not hunger, not fear. Something quieter and heavier. A moment that stayed without asking permission. Understanding, she realized, wasn't always effort. Sometimes it arrived as silence that didn't need defending. And she carried that with her into the night, unsure why it mattered—only certain that it did. The moment did not follow her the way she expected. Artemis waited for it to bloom into something—curiosity sharpening into pursuit, memory looping back with insistence, the old patterns demanding meaning be assigned. None of that happened. Instead, the feeling settled. It stayed where it had landed, like a stone dropped into water after the ripples had already passed. She walked with Leto through the quieter streets, the city thinning into pockets of light and shadow. Her body felt normal again—no heightened awareness, no dull ache of restraint. Just motion. Just breath. That unsettled her more than the encounter itself. She kept checking for the effort she had grown used to carrying, half-expecting it to surge back in response to the memory. It didn't. The silence around the moment remained intact, undisturbed by doubt or analysis. "I didn't do anything," Artemis said eventually. Leto glanced at her. "You did plenty." "I didn't stop myself. I didn't correct anything. I didn't—" She searched for the word. "Work." "No," Leto agreed. "You didn't." Artemis frowned, not in irritation, but in confusion. "That's not how it's been." "No," Leto said again. "But it doesn't mean it was wrong." They reached their lodging and climbed the stairs in quiet steps. Artemis paused at the window afterward, looking out over the street below. A couple passed, arguing softly. Somewhere farther away, someone laughed. Life continued with careless confidence. She touched the glass lightly, grounding herself. The encounter replayed once—not as a loop, not as an intrusion. Just a single, complete memory. His stillness. The absence of pressure. The way her body hadn't prepared for anything at all. It hadn't asked her to choose. That was new. She waited for the discomfort to arrive—for the sense of vulnerability, for the warning bells that usually followed unguarded moments. None came. What remained wasn't peace, exactly. It was space. Artemis sat on the edge of the bed, elbows on her knees, and stared at the floor. Understanding wasn't a declaration. It wasn't a rule she could enforce. It was the recognition that not every interaction required vigilance—and that noticing this didn't mean abandoning herself. She didn't know what the moment meant. She didn't need to. When she lay down later, sleep came easier than it had in days. The city hummed beyond the walls, distant and unconcerned. The stone remained where it had landed. And for the first time, Artemis didn't feel the need to pick it up. Morning arrived without urgency. Light filtered through the curtains in thin bands, warming the floor before it reached the bed. Artemis woke slowly, not startled, not already braced. The city outside was beginning its daily rearrangement—cars starting, voices rising, doors opening and closing. The moment from the night before was there when she surfaced. It didn't rush her. She noticed that first. She lay still for a while, cataloguing the absence of familiar reactions. No spike of anticipation. No compulsion to revisit the place by the river. No pull to search the crowd for a shape she barely remembered. That, somehow, felt intentional—even though she hadn't decided it. When she joined Leto for breakfast downstairs, the space was busy but unremarkable. Artemis moved through it with the quiet competence she'd been practicing, ordering coffee, taking a seat by the window. She felt steady. Awake. "You're not restless," Leto observed. Artemis considered the statement. "I thought I would be." "And?" "I'm not." The truth surprised her again. She'd spent centuries learning how to track, how to follow threads until they revealed themselves. The urge to pursue—to know—was ingrained. This time, she let the thread lie. They went out afterward, walking the same streets as the day before. Artemis found herself glancing toward the river once, then deliberately looking away—not as denial, but as choice. The moment didn't vanish when she did. It stayed intact, unthreatened by distance. A man passed close behind her on the sidewalk. She adjusted her step without thinking, body flowing around the movement. No tension followed. No need to check. That mattered more than she expected. "I could go back," Artemis said suddenly. Not as a suggestion. Just a statement. "Yes," Leto replied. "But I don't need to." Leto smiled—not approving, not relieved. Simply present. "That's the difference." They crossed an intersection and paused at the corner, waiting for the signal to change. Artemis watched the crowd surge and settle, people moving with their own internal logic. She didn't feel separate from them. She didn't feel absorbed either. She felt placed. The light changed. They crossed. Artemis didn't look back. Understanding, she realized, wasn't found in pursuit. It was in the ability to let something meaningful exist without demanding it turn into more. That didn't make the moment smaller. It made her larger. And as the city carried on around her, Artemis walked forward—unhurried, unburdened—choosing not to chase, and finding that nothing was lost in the choosing. The afternoon tested her in quieter ways. They stopped at a small community center near the river—open doors, a bulletin board cluttered with flyers, the low hum of voices drifting from inside. Leto wanted to ask about a local exhibit; Artemis followed, expecting nothing more than a few minutes of polite waiting. Inside, the air was cooler. A woman at the desk looked up, smiled, and gestured for them to come closer. Artemis stepped forward without thinking—and then halted, realizing she'd moved too fast, too decisively, crowding the space. She corrected, taking a half-step back. The woman didn't seem to notice the adjustment. She explained the exhibit dates, her voice practiced, friendly. Artemis listened, nodding at the right moments, keeping her posture open without leaning in or pulling away. It took effort again. Not the sharp, draining kind—but a steady application, like holding a line against current. When the explanation ended, Artemis asked a question. Not a safe one. Not a neutral one. One that showed interest. The woman brightened, launching into details, gesturing toward a hallway where framed photographs lined the walls. Artemis followed, then slowed, letting the woman lead instead. She felt the calculation hum and resisted the urge to simplify it by retreating. Halfway down the hall, a man stepped out of a side room carrying a stack of folders. He startled when he saw them, nearly dropping everything. Artemis reacted—too quickly. She reached out to steady the folders, hand brushing his wrist. The contact was brief, functional—but she felt the flicker of awareness spark after the fact. The man recovered, smiling in thanks. "Appreciate it," he said. Artemis nodded. "Of course." The woman glanced between them, then continued talking, unfazed. The moment passed without consequence. Still, Artemis felt the aftershock—not fear, not irritation. Attention. She replayed it once, assessing. She hadn't defaulted into defense. She hadn't erased herself. She'd acted—and then stopped. That was new too. Outside again, the light had shifted, clouds moving in thin layers overhead. Artemis breathed in, slow and deliberate, letting the effort settle instead of resisting it. "That took work," she said. "Yes," Leto agreed. "But it didn't undo anything." "No." Artemis walked on, muscles loosening incrementally. Understanding wasn't something she reached and kept. It was something she reapplied, again and again, adjusting to context, to fatigue, to the shape of the moment. She didn't resent that anymore. Effort didn't mean failure. It meant engagement. And as the river reappeared ahead, Artemis felt grounded—not because the work was finished, but because she knew how to continue it without losing herself along the way. The last evening came quietly. No marker announced it, no sense of completion or ceremony. The city simply dimmed into itself as the sun slid low, light flattening against buildings and glass until everything looked briefly unreal. Artemis stood at the river one final time. She didn't search the crowd. That choice felt settled now—not effortful, not performative. The place where she'd stood before looked no different than any other stretch of railing. People leaned, talked, passed through. The water kept moving, indifferent and patient. She rested her forearms against the rail and let herself be there. The work of the past days surfaced—not as memories, but as weight distribution. How she stood. How she breathed. Where her attention settled and where it didn't. Nothing was sharp anymore. Nothing needed correction in advance. A man passed behind her, close enough to register. She shifted a fraction to make space, fluid and unremarkable. The movement didn't linger in her mind after. That was the clearest sign of change. Leto joined her, shoulder brushing hers lightly. Artemis didn't tense. She leaned into the contact without thinking, grounding herself in the familiarity of it. "I don't feel finished," Artemis said. Leto smiled faintly. "Good." Artemis exhaled, a quiet huff of agreement. "I used to think understanding meant resolution." "And now?" "Now it feels like… maintenance." She considered the word. "Choice. Over and over." "Yes." They stood together, watching the river carry light and shadow toward places Artemis would never see. She thought of Italy—the pause. Greece—the resistance. America—the adjustment, the mistake, the ease she hadn't expected. And the moment that had stayed without asking to be more. She didn't romanticize it. Didn't elevate it into meaning it couldn't hold. It remained what it was: a point of reference, not a destination. That felt right. As they turned away from the river, Artemis glanced once more at the water—not in longing, not in reluctance. Just acknowledgment. She would return to Greece changed—but not transformed. She would still misstep. Still tire. Still default sometimes when she was careless or exhausted. But now she knew the difference between resistance and choice. She knew she could engage without losing herself. And as she walked back through the city, posture easy, stride unguarded, Artemis carried that knowledge with her—not as a promise, not as a victory. Just as something earned. Something human.
