It didn't challenge her, didn't glare, didn't dare her to react. It simply existed—hot, loud, sprawling—and she stepped into it with the faint, unsettling realization that nothing in her pushed back. She adjusted the strap of her bag and moved with the flow of people exiting the terminal. Men passed close enough to brush shoulders. Voices overlapped. Someone laughed too loudly near the curb. Her body waited for the familiar tightening—for the coil of instinct that used to arrive before thought—but it never did. The absence was… quiet. Not relief. Just space. Leto followed half a step behind, the way she always did when Artemis was not meant to lead. Not command. Not correct. This visit had boundaries, and Artemis was keenly aware of them. She was banned. Officially. No authority over the Hunters. No orders. No restructuring. She was here for a check—health, morale, stability—and then she was supposed to leave. She repeated that to herself as they crossed into the parking structure. "I'm not here to run anything," Artemis said, tone even. "Just to see them." Leto's mouth curved, barely. "You say that as if saying it enough times will make it boring." "It should be boring," Artemis replied. "Routine is good." "And yet," Leto said lightly, "you chose Texas." Artemis didn't answer right away. Heat shimmered across the concrete. Cars idled. A man argued into his phone about directions. Ordinary things. She found that she didn't resent them. "The Hunters are here," she said finally. "And if I can't protect them the way I used to, I can at least make sure they're standing." Leto hummed in acknowledgment, neither approving nor discouraging. That was new, too. Once, Leto would have corrected her phrasing. Once, Artemis would have bristled at the implication. They reached the car. Artemis slid into the passenger seat, checked her phone, and saw the message waiting—coordinates, a short status update, sent by one of the younger Hunters. Efficient. Precise. Tech-savvy. The kind Artemis would have dismissed years ago as unfocused. She stared at the screen a second longer than necessary. "Still there?" Leto asked, starting the engine. "Yes," Artemis said, locking the phone. "They're expecting a check-in. Nothing more." "And that's all you intend to give them?" Artemis looked out at the city as they pulled onto the road. The skyline rose in uneven lines, glass and steel catching the sun. She felt tall in her body again—not imposing, not diminished. Just present. "That's all I'm allowed," she said. Leto's smile flickered, unreadable. "For now." Artemis didn't respond. The car merged into traffic, Texas unfolding ahead of them—wide, indifferent, and unaware that she was passing through under rules she had never needed before. Texas unfolded in layers the longer they drove—wide roads feeding into narrower ones, glass giving way to brick, heat softening the edges of everything. Artemis watched it pass without cataloguing angles or exits. The habit surfaced once, then slipped away when she didn't reach for it. They stopped at a café near a strip of offices and repair shops, the kind of place that smelled like burnt coffee and sugar and stayed open because people needed it to be. Artemis ordered without thinking. The barista smiled, handed over the cup, moved on. No ceremony. No scrutiny. The exchange ended where it began. She stood near the window, letting the cup warm her palms. Outside, a group of men argued cheerfully over a parking spot. One gestured too broadly, laughed at himself, conceded. It was all terribly… normal. "You're staring," Leto said, dry as dust. "I'm observing," Artemis replied. "Same thing," Leto said. "Different excuses." Artemis took a sip. The coffee was bad. She didn't mind. "I'm recalibrating." "That sounds suspiciously like thinking." Artemis glanced at her. "I do think." "Not like this," Leto said. "This is the kind where you don't look ready to throw something." "That's progress," Artemis said, and surprised herself by meaning it. They left the café and walked a block, the sun pressing down with a lazy insistence. A man brushed past Artemis and muttered an apology without stopping. She nodded without irritation. The moment ended. Her body didn't store it. That, too, was new. Leto slowed, matching her pace. "You know," she said lightly, "once upon a time, you would've taken that as a personal failing of the universe." "Once upon a time, the universe encouraged it," Artemis said. "And now?" Artemis considered. "Now it's… inefficient." Leto's eyebrow lifted. "That's the word you're going with?" "It's accurate." They reached the car again. Artemis checked her phone. Another message from the same Hunter—short update, nothing urgent. All accounted for. Waiting. Waiting. The word sat strangely in her chest. Not as pressure. As expectation. "I'm not staying," Artemis said, more firmly than necessary. "I'm not stepping in." "Mm," Leto said. "You've said that." "I mean it." "I know," Leto replied. "That's why I'm curious." Artemis frowned. "About what?" "How long you'll be satisfied just watching." The question landed without sting. Artemis leaned back against the car, eyes lifting to the sky—too big, too blue, unconcerned with her rules. "Long enough," she said. "To make sure they're alive. Stable. That's it." Leto smiled, small and knowing. "For someone with no authority, you sound very invested." Artemis pushed off the car and opened the door. "Investment isn't command." Leto followed her in. "Keep telling yourself that." As the engine turned over and they pulled back into traffic, Artemis felt it—a faint, unnameable awareness, like a line tightening somewhere just out of sight. Not danger. Not alarm. Attention. She didn't turn her head. She didn't need to. Artemis was looking at her phone when it happened. Not walking blindly—just distracted enough to be human about it. The message from the younger Hunter was brief, efficient, and irritatingly vague. She slowed her steps near the curb, thumb hovering as she reread it, attention split between the screen and the crosswalk ahead. Someone stepped into her path at the exact wrong moment. They collided shoulder to shoulder—not hard enough to hurt, just enough to jolt. Artemis shifted instinctively, weight already correcting, irritation rising out of reflex more than intent. "Sorry," the man said at the same time she did. They both stopped. That, too, was irritating. Artemis looked up, already prepared to dismiss the interaction and move on. He had done the same—half-turned away, phone still in his hand, apology clearly habitual rather than apologetic. Then their eyes met. Nothing dramatic happened. No spark. No lightning. No sudden realization. Just a pause that lasted a fraction longer than it needed to. He frowned slightly, as if reassessing something he hadn't meant to notice. Artemis mirrored the expression without meaning to, irritation cooling into something more neutral. Curious, maybe. Annoyed at herself for being curious. "Didn't see you," he said, tone easy, almost amused. Not defensive. Not flirtatious. Just… normal. "Likewise," Artemis replied. That should have been the end of it. It wasn't. He shifted his grip on the phone, glancing past her toward the crosswalk and then back, like he was deciding whether to continue or comment on the shared inconvenience. "Guess that's what I get for multitasking." "Or standing in the middle of a sidewalk," Artemis said, dry without being sharp. His mouth twitched. "Fair." The light changed. People began to move around them, flowing past as if they were obstacles no one wanted to acknowledge. Neither of them stepped away immediately. Leto watched from a few steps back, saying nothing. "I'll, uh—" he gestured vaguely toward the street, then stopped himself. "You're not wrong, by the way." Artemis raised an eyebrow. "About?" "The sidewalk thing." He shrugged. "Occupational hazard." "Of what?" she asked before deciding whether she cared. He considered the question, then smirked. "Bad timing." That earned him a look. Not hostile. Appraising. "Right," Artemis said. "Well. Good luck with that." "Yeah," he said, stepping aside to let her pass. "You too." She walked on without looking back, phone slipping back into her pocket. The interaction faded as quickly as it had formed—unremarkable on the surface, forgettable by design. And yet, three steps later, Artemis realized she'd slowed. Not because she wanted to turn around. But because, inexplicably, she was still thinking about the way he'd smiled like the moment hadn't mattered—while clearly registering that it had. She straightened and kept walking. It meant nothing. Which was precisely why it bothered her that it didn't feel like nothing at all. They should have separated. That was the natural conclusion to the moment—two people colliding, apologizing, moving on. Artemis had already taken a step to do exactly that when he spoke again, not stopping her, not insisting, just… continuing. "You know," he said, glancing at the crosswalk as people streamed past them, "if this were a movie, this is where dramatic music would kick in." Artemis stopped despite herself. She turned back slowly. "What kind of movie?" He considered it, head tilting slightly. "Depends. Bollywood, probably. Lots of eye contact. Someone's mother would show up in the background for no reason." That earned him a look—sharp, incredulous, but not unamused. "You bumped into me on a sidewalk." "I did," he agreed easily. "Which is exactly how those start." "That's not reassuring." "Didn't mean it to be." The corner of his mouth lifted, faint and unguarded, like he wasn't trying to impress her so much as entertain himself. Artemis noted that before she could stop herself, then immediately resented the fact that she'd noted it at all. "You narrate accidents often?" she asked. "Only the interesting ones." "And this qualifies?" He shrugged. "You stopped walking." She opened her mouth to deny it, then closed it again. He wasn't wrong. That irritated her more than the joke. "Careful," she said. "You're assigning meaning where there isn't any." "Am I?" He glanced around at the city—people moving, cars idling, life resuming around them like they were temporarily irrelevant. "Feels like there's at least some meaning. Otherwise this would already be over." Artemis crossed her arms. "Or you just don't know when to disengage." "That too," he said cheerfully. "I've been told." Leto, standing a few paces back, watched the exchange with quiet interest and said nothing. The light changed again. A fresh wave of pedestrians pressed through, nudging the moment forward. Artemis felt the subtle pressure of choice—stay, go, justify neither. "You're blocking traffic," she said, because it was true and because it gave her something solid to hold onto. He stepped aside immediately, holding up his hands in surrender. "Fair point. I'll relocate my cinematic ambitions." She moved past him, then hesitated just long enough to add, "For what it's worth, your movie would be terrible." He laughed—not loud, not performative. "Most of them are." She walked away after that, no dramatic exit, no backward glance. The city swallowed her up within seconds, the moment dissolving into heat and noise and motion. Behind her, he stood where she'd left him for a heartbeat longer than necessary, watching nothing in particular with a faint, thoughtful expression. Leto fell into step beside Artemis a few yards later. "You didn't scowl," she observed mildly. Artemis kept her eyes forward. "I didn't have reason to." "Mm," Leto said. "Interesting." Artemis didn't respond. She told herself the encounter was forgettable, which was why it bothered her—just slightly—that it didn't feel forgotten at all. They walked for a block before Artemis realized she was still replaying the conversation. Not the words themselves—those were unremarkable enough—but the ease of it. The way it hadn't demanded anything. The way it hadn't tried to become more than it was. That, she decided, was the problem. Ease had a way of slipping past defenses because it didn't announce itself as a threat. She checked her phone again, grounding herself in the coordinates and the short status update waiting there. All accounted for. Waiting. Practical. Necessary. Real. She folded the device away and lengthened her stride, as if momentum could reset whatever had briefly gone off-course. Leto matched her pace without comment. After a few seconds, Artemis said, "That was nothing." "Mmm," Leto replied. Artemis shot her a look. "You're agreeing too easily." "I'm acknowledging," Leto said. "There's a difference." "There isn't," Artemis said. "There is," Leto said calmly. "You just don't like it." Artemis turned her attention back to the street. Cars rolled past. A delivery truck double-parked with its hazards on. Someone argued with a parking meter like it had personally offended them. The city kept going, indifferent to moments that didn't know they were moments. "He talks too much," Artemis said, because it felt safer than saying anything else. "He talked enough," Leto corrected. "You stayed." "That doesn't mean—" "It means exactly what it means," Leto said, not unkindly. "Nothing more. Nothing less." Artemis frowned. "You're being deliberately vague." "I'm letting you decide how much space it gets," Leto replied. They reached the car again. Artemis unlocked it and slid into the seat, exhaling as the door shut. The interior felt smaller than it had earlier, as if the city had followed her inside. She stared through the windshield for a second longer than necessary. "I'm here for the Hunters," she said, firmly this time. "Not for… distractions." "Of course," Leto said, starting the engine. "And you're very good at staying on task." The words weren't teasing. They were factual. That somehow made them worse. As they pulled back into traffic, Artemis felt the earlier ease recede, replaced by the familiar steadiness of purpose. This was better. This was where she belonged—decisions with edges, responsibilities with weight. Conversations that didn't matter had a way of complicating things. And yet— A faint, unwelcome thought surfaced: It didn't feel like a complication. She pushed it aside before it could settle. The car turned onto a quieter road, buildings thinning as they moved away from the city center. Artemis straightened, attention narrowing, the soft bubble of almost-comfort collapsing into something sharper. Whatever had just happened—whatever almost happened—was irrelevant now. She had somewhere to be. And whatever waited for her there would not be gentle. They drove in silence for a few minutes, the kind that wasn't uncomfortable so much as unfinished. Artemis watched the road narrow, storefronts giving way to longer stretches of asphalt and low buildings. The city thinned. The air felt heavier, less distracted. She welcomed it. Leto didn't interrupt right away. She never did when Artemis was still arranging things internally—stacking impressions, filing away what didn't belong. When she finally spoke, her tone was mild, almost conversational. "You know," she said, eyes on the road, "you didn't correct him." Artemis didn't look over. "About what?" "Anything," Leto replied. "The movie. The timing. Himself." "That wasn't my responsibility." "No," Leto agreed. "It wasn't." The pause stretched just long enough to invite a response. Artemis gave none. Leto continued, carefully casual. "Once upon a time, you would've ended that exchange in three sentences." Artemis folded her arms. "Once upon a time, I was in charge." "And now?" "And now I'm here to check on the Hunters," Artemis said, crisp. "Which is what I intend to do." Leto's lips curved faintly. "You're very good at intentions." Artemis turned to her then, expression cool. "Say what you want to say." Leto met her gaze briefly, then returned her attention to the road. "I already did." Artemis scoffed. "You implied something." "I observed something," Leto corrected. "There's a difference." "There really isn't." "There is," Leto said. "You just don't like when I point it out." The car slowed as they approached a turn. Artemis watched the signal blink, irritation surfacing—not at Leto, not at the conversation, but at the lingering echo of something she'd meant to discard. "He didn't matter," Artemis said. "I didn't say he did." "You're thinking it." Leto considered that. "I'm thinking you didn't dismiss him." "That's not the same thing." "No," Leto said. "It isn't." The light changed. Leto turned onto the side road leading toward the coordinates Artemis had memorized. The surroundings grew quieter, more subdued. This was better. This was manageable. Leto spoke once more, softly. "You don't have to explain it to me." "I'm not explaining anything." "I know," Leto said. "I'm just noting that you didn't harden." Artemis stared ahead, jaw tightening. "I didn't soften either." Leto smiled, small and unreadable. "That's why it stood out." The road stretched on. Artemis felt the moment finally loosen its grip, slipping into the background where it belonged. She let it go without ceremony. "Drop it," she said. Leto inclined her head. "Already did." They drove the rest of the way without another word, the earlier lightness dissolving into something quieter and more focused. Whatever that encounter had been—whatever it had almost become—was now firmly behind her. Or so Artemis told herself as the coordinates drew closer, and the weight of responsibility settled back into place. The location wasn't wrong. That was the first thing Artemis registered as they pulled in. The coordinates matched. The buildings were the right kind of nondescript—rented, temporary, easy to abandon if needed. Vehicles were parked where they should be. On the surface, it looked like a functioning operation. On the surface. She stepped out of the car and felt it immediately—not danger, not threat, but strain. The air carried it, tight and contained, like a room where everyone had agreed not to speak about the same thing. A couple of Hunters noticed her arrival and straightened out of habit, then stopped themselves. Artemis clocked the correction without comment. She didn't blame them. They'd been trained to respond to her presence for centuries. Undoing that kind of reflex took time. "I'm not here to run anything," she said before anyone could say it for her. Her voice carried just enough to reach the small cluster forming nearby. "I'm here to check on you. That's all." A beat passed. Then Zoe stepped forward. She looked the same—composed, sharp-eyed, controlled—but the tension around her mouth hadn't been there the last time Artemis had seen her. That mattered. "You didn't need to come," Zoe said, not unkindly. "I know," Artemis replied. "That's why I'm not staying." Zoe studied her for a second longer, then nodded once. "All right." No challenge. No resentment. Just acceptance layered over something heavier. Artemis didn't like that weight, and she liked even less that she hadn't caused it—but she felt responsible for it anyway. They moved inside. The space that should have been loud with movement and quiet chatter was subdued instead. Hunters sat too still. Conversations cut off when Artemis passed, not out of fear, but habit—and something else underneath it. Worry, tightly leashed. A younger Hunter approached, tablet tucked under her arm. The same one who'd been sending the updates. She stopped short, unsure whether to salute, nod, or simply speak. "Health check," Artemis said gently, saving her the choice. "You can start with the basics." The Hunter nodded, relief flickering across her face, and launched into logistics—rotations, supplies, minor injuries. Everything she said was efficient. Everything she avoided was not. Artemis listened without interrupting, eyes moving, cataloguing details she wasn't supposed to act on anymore. Bruises that hadn't been there before. Equipment left where it shouldn't have been. The absence of two familiar presences she had already noticed and not yet named. Zoe fell into step beside her as they walked the perimeter. "They're holding together," she said quietly. "That's not the same as being fine," Artemis replied. Zoe didn't argue. They stopped near the back of the building, away from the others. Artemis turned to face her fully. "I'm not here to issue corrections," she said. "And I won't interfere unless there's an immediate risk." Zoe met her gaze, something unreadable passing behind her eyes. "Understood." The word landed heavier than it should have. Artemis looked past her, taking in the space again—the too-quiet rooms, the waiting that clung to everything. This wasn't routine. This wasn't stability. Whatever had happened here, it had already begun to hollow the place out. And she was only just arriving to see it. It took Artemis less than five minutes to confirm what she already knew. Nothing was actively wrong—and that was the problem. The Hunters moved when spoken to. They answered questions. They followed routines that had clearly been maintained out of discipline rather than confidence. The place functioned, but without rhythm. Like a body still going through the motions after the shock had already passed. Artemis stood near the center of the room and let her gaze settle, not on individuals but on the space between them. Empty chairs. Gear racks that weren't full. The subtle way conversations didn't resume once they stopped. This wasn't grief. Not yet. This was waiting. Zoe noticed her attention linger and exhaled slowly. "They haven't been sleeping much," she said, quietly enough that only Artemis could hear. "No nightmares. Just… restlessness." "That's worse," Artemis replied. Zoe's jaw tightened. "I know." A Hunter laughed somewhere down the hall—too loud, too sudden. The sound cut off almost immediately, as if whoever had made it realized it didn't belong. Artemis didn't turn toward it. She didn't need to. Forced normalcy was always louder than the real thing. "How long?" Artemis asked. Zoe hesitated. "That's not an answer," Artemis said gently. "Three days," Zoe replied. "Since the ambush." There it was. The word didn't ripple through the room because everyone already carried it. Artemis felt it settle into place with unpleasant clarity. Three days was long enough for hope to fray, short enough for denial to still exist. The worst stretch of time there was. "Were you moving locations?" Artemis asked. "Yes," Zoe said. "Gas stations. Short stops. They were with the group until—" She stopped, recalibrating. "Until they weren't." Artemis closed her eyes briefly, then opened them again. Control first. Reaction later. "They were taken," she said, not as a question. Zoe nodded once. No one else spoke. They didn't need to. The silence thickened, no longer just strained but weighted, pressing down on everyone in the room. Artemis felt eyes on her—not expectation of command, but something more fragile. Permission. She straightened slowly, making sure her posture didn't signal authority. That would have been easy. Habitual. Wrong. "Has anyone been hurt since?" she asked. "No," Zoe said. "Not physically." Artemis absorbed that. Lily and Rose were gone. The rest were intact—for now. That meant whoever had taken them had been precise. Selective. This hadn't been chaos. It had been intention. That was worse. Artemis glanced around the room again, committing faces to memory. She met a few gazes and held them, not as a leader issuing reassurance, but as someone who refused to look away. "I'm here to check on you," she said, voice steady. "So this is me checking." The words landed quietly, but they anchored the space in a way nothing else had yet. Zoe watched her closely, something unreadable in her expression. The waiting didn't end. But now, it had a name. Zoe didn't start with names. She started with sequence—because that was how she coped, and Artemis recognized it immediately. Events laid out cleanly, one after another, as if clarity could blunt the damage. "They were running support," Zoe said, voice controlled, eyes fixed on a spot just past Artemis's shoulder. "Nothing frontline. Just logistics. Fuel, supplies. Short stops only." Artemis nodded once. She didn't interrupt. Interruptions made people defensive, and Zoe didn't need that right now. "We rotated locations every few hours," Zoe continued. "No patterns. No repeated stops. Lily was tracking routes. Rose was… complaining about the coffee." The faintest ghost of a smile tried to surface and failed. Zoe inhaled, steadying herself. "It was a gas station off the interstate," she said. "Middle of nowhere. Broad daylight. They were gone less than six minutes." Artemis felt something tighten—not in her chest, but lower, steadier. Anger didn't flare. Panic didn't follow. This was information, and she treated it like information. "Taken inside?" Artemis asked. "No," Zoe said. "Outside. Parking lot. Two vehicles. Clean. Quiet. No weapons drawn that anyone saw." That ruled out chaos. Ruled out impulse. "They didn't fight," Zoe added quickly, the words coming faster now. "They didn't have time." Artemis met her gaze. "That's not a failure." Zoe's jaw clenched. "I know. But—" "But you'll still carry it," Artemis finished. Zoe exhaled, the breath shaking despite her control. "They didn't leave anything behind," Zoe said. "No blood. No gear. Phones were gone. Everything suggests they were the objective." Artemis looked past her, mind assembling the shape of it. Selective extraction. No collateral. No message. Someone had known who they were, where they'd be, and when to move. That wasn't random violence. "That's why we didn't scatter," Zoe continued. "We locked down. Waited. We didn't want to make it worse." "That was the right call," Artemis said immediately. Zoe blinked. "You're sure?" "Yes." The certainty in Artemis's voice surprised even her. She let it stand. Silence settled again, heavier now that the truth had been spoken. Around them, the Hunters pretended not to listen, which meant they were listening to every word. Artemis shifted her weight slightly, conscious of every signal she was sending. She was not here to command. She was not here to absolve. But she could do this much. "Lily and Rose are capable," she said. "And they were taken alive." Zoe's eyes flickered. "You don't know that." "No," Artemis agreed. "But everything you've described points that way." That mattered. Zoe looked away, jaw working. "We don't know where they are." Artemis nodded slowly. "Not yet." The word carried more weight than reassurance. It wasn't a promise. It was a statement of intent. For the first time since Artemis had arrived, the waiting in the room shifted—not easing, not lifting, but changing shape. And that was when Zoe's gaze sharpened, something cautious and questioning entering her expression. Because she understood exactly what that word implied. And she wasn't sure Artemis was allowed to mean it. Zoe spoke before Artemis could. "No." The word wasn't loud. It wasn't sharp. It was firm in the way that came from long habit and longer responsibility. The kind that didn't need volume to hold its ground. Artemis turned to her slowly. "You're not in command," Zoe continued, voice steady, eyes clear. "You know that. And even if you were—this isn't something you shoulder alone." "I know what I'm allowed to do," Artemis said. "That's not what I'm talking about." Zoe gestured toward the room—not dramatically, just enough to encompass the Hunters pretending very hard not to listen. "They're already on edge. If you step in now, if you start moving pieces, they'll read it as panic. Or worse—permission to break formation." Artemis absorbed that without flinching. Zoe was right. Which was exactly why she'd spoken when she did. "I'm not stepping in," Artemis said evenly. "And I'm not issuing orders." Zoe frowned. "Then what are you—" "I'm taking responsibility," Artemis said, cutting in—not sharply, but decisively. "Not as your commander. As myself." The distinction landed like a line drawn on the floor. Zoe stared at her. "That's not how this works." "It is now," Artemis replied. "Because I'm not acting as Artemis of the Hunt. I'm acting as a mortal with a vested interest in two missing people." Zoe's eyes narrowed. "You're still banned." "Yes," Artemis said. "From running you. From commanding you. Not from caring." "That's a dangerous loophole." Artemis inclined her head slightly. "I'm aware." Zoe took a breath, visibly weighing her response. "If you do this," she said carefully, "you do it without us. No resources. No backup. No expectation of intervention." "I wouldn't ask for any of that," Artemis replied. "And if it goes wrong?" "Then it goes wrong for me alone." The room felt very quiet. Zoe studied Artemis's face, searching for something—hesitation, defiance, pride. She found none of it. What she saw instead unsettled her more. Resolve, stripped of authority. "You're serious," Zoe said. "Yes." Zoe exhaled slowly, hand coming up to rub at her temple. "You always pick the worst timing." Artemis's expression softened just a fraction. "You taught me to." That earned her a short, humorless huff. Zoe dropped her hand. "I don't like this." "You don't have to." "I don't trust that you won't get hurt." Artemis met her gaze fully. "That's the risk of being human." The words hung between them, heavier than Artemis had expected. Zoe looked away first. "Fine," Zoe said at last. "You do whatever you're going to do. But you don't pull us into it." "I won't," Artemis promised. Zoe nodded once, sharp and final. "Then I'll keep them together. Calm. Alive." Artemis held her gaze for a second longer, then inclined her head in acknowledgment. Not command. Respect. As she turned away, she felt it settle—not anger, not urgency, but something colder and clearer. She was not in charge. Which meant there was nothing stopping her from acting alone. And for the first time since Lily and Rose's names had been spoken aloud, Artemis knew exactly what she was going to do next. Time Skip: West Bengal India [Just after events of chapter 14: Revalations] The house was too quiet. Not empty—never empty—but subdued in a way Naruto hadn't noticed before. Or maybe he had noticed it and simply hadn't known what to call it yet. He sat at the dining table with a cup of tea he'd already forgotten to drink, watching the steam curl and vanish like it was trying to tell him something he didn't feel ready to hear. Srishti was in the other room, humming softly to herself as she reorganized a shelf that didn't need reorganizing. She did that when she was nervous. Or bored. Or pretending not to think too hard about things that hovered just out of reach. Naruto leaned back in his chair and stared at the ceiling. Post-Revelations had done that to him—left things hanging without giving him the courtesy of answers. Power, identity, responsibility. All of it had been dragged into the light and then abandoned there, unresolved. He knew what he could do. He knew what he had done. What he didn't know was how much of his life was still his own. He rubbed a hand over his face, exhaling slowly. "Stop brooding," Srishti called from the other room without looking up. "You're doing the thing." Naruto smirked faintly. "I don't brood." "You brood," she replied immediately. "You just call it thinking so it feels productive." He laughed under his breath. She wasn't wrong. "I was just wondering," he said, standing and joining her by the shelf, "when exactly I became the guy who drinks tea and stares into space." "You always did that," she said. "You just used to do it after punching something." "Growth," Naruto said solemnly. Srishti rolled her eyes, but the smile she gave him didn't quite reach them. She nudged a book into place, then another, movements precise. "You're restless," she said. "Maybe." "Something's coming," she added, quieter. Naruto didn't answer immediately. He didn't need to. That feeling had been sitting in his chest all morning, low and insistent. Not dread. Not excitement. Just the certainty that whatever calm existed right now was borrowed time. He looked at her—really looked this time. She'd grown used to his silences, learned how to live around them without demanding explanations. That fact sat heavier on him than any revelation ever had. "Hey," he said suddenly. "If I told you we might need to take a short trip soon—no details yet—would you trust me?" Srishti froze, hand still on the shelf. She turned slowly. "That depends," she said. "Is this one of your normal trips or one of your 'don't ask questions' trips?" Naruto huffed a breath. "I was hoping for the first one." She studied his face for a long moment, then sighed. "You're bad at lying when you're tired." "Good thing I'm not lying." "Yet," she said. The words settled between them, heavy but familiar. Before Naruto could respond, the doorbell rang. Once. Twice. Three times—precise, measured. Naruto's posture shifted immediately. Whatever he'd been about to say vanished, replaced by something colder and sharper. He glanced at the door, then back at Srishti. "Stay here," he said. She nodded without argument. As Naruto moved toward the door, the quiet in the house changed—not broken, just redirected. He knew that sound. The sound of personal time ending. And when he opened the door to find the faces waiting for him, he understood exactly why the morning had felt like borrowed time. 13 Event 13 — The State Is Bleeding (~450 words) They didn't waste time with pleasantries. Naruto barely had the door shut before the weight of the room shifted, his home turning into something else entirely. The IB chief stood closest, posture stiff, expression carved into neutrality. The RAW chief lingered half a step behind, eyes sharp, already measuring. The Eastern Command General filled the remaining space without trying to—presence doing the work for him. Srishti hovered at the edge of the hallway, silent and alert. "We'll be direct," the IB chief said. "We don't have the luxury not to be." Naruto nodded once and gestured toward the table. "Then sit." They did. Files came out. A tablet lit up. Images scrolled past—maps, names, redacted blocks stacked on top of one another like scars. Naruto didn't comment. He watched. Listened. "The IC-81B attacks didn't just cost lives," the RAW chief said. "They burned networks. Assets. Trust." "Most of our external field agents are compromised," the IB chief continued. "Names. Locations. Families. Some of them didn't even know they were exposed until it was too late." Naruto's jaw tightened, but he said nothing. "We traced the leak to a sleeper," the RAW chief said. "Embedded. Deep. Acting under our flag." A pause. Then, quieter: "ISI." Srishti's fingers curled slightly at her side. Naruto felt it, but didn't look back. Naruto leaned forward, forearms resting on the table. "And you can't retrieve it." "We can," the Eastern Command General said at last, voice steady. "But not cleanly. And not with anyone whose identity is already compromised." The word settled heavily between them. "All standard field operatives are off the table," the RAW chief said. "Every single one. The moment they move, they light up." Naruto exhaled slowly. "So you're out of options." "We're out of safe options," the IB chief corrected.
